HEADLINE: Champagne Makers Promise Plenty to Cry Into on Dec. 31

BYLINE: By CRAIG R. WHITNEY 

DATELINE: AY, France

 BODY:
Churchill, who knew a thing or two about fine living, used to say of his favorite Champagne, "In victory I deserve it, and in defeat, I need it." This New Year's Eve, partygoers apparently have decided that when it comes to French Champagne they, too, have to have it.
Sales of French Champagne have been setting records for months. The fact that the American economy is booming, along with a taste for luxury goods, is one reason. So is the notion of ringing in the millennium with a flute full of the real stuff. 
 Wine merchants in the United States say French Champagne is being rationed to them month by month. Advertisements from Champagne makers are warning people to prepare for their parties by laying in a supply early. Food and wine writers have been debating the possibility of a shortage for more than a year.
But here in the heart of the Champagne-making region of France, talk of a shortage is laughed off as nonsense. Why would any smart winemaker mismanage the millennium?
"There are a few makers who don't have any Champagne left," said Rene Goutorbe, who took a moment off from directing tractors to his wine presses as the 1999 harvest arrives here. "But they're the same ones who didn't have any left last year at this time, and they won't have any next year, either."
"They probably have the same problem with money," Mr. Goutorbe added, laughing.
Several years ago, the Champagne houses and the growers of the three grapes essential for the genuine bubbly -- pinot noir, pinot chardonnay and pinot meunier -- agreed to produce enough grapes to make an average of 270 million bottles of Champagne a year for the rest of the 1990's, about 50 million bottles more than they had been selling in the recession years early in the decade.
The producers also agreed to draw down the equivalent of 132 million bottles of reserve wine, normally saved for years with a poor harvest, and use it to put more Champagne on the market in 1999 than ever before.
The result is that there will be plenty of nonvintage wine to go around. Champagne sales this year will most likely far surpass 300 million bottles and set a record, up to 15 percent more than last year's figures, according to executives at Champagne Laurent-Perrier.
"All our expectations have been surpassed," said Christian Pol-Roger, whose Champagne Pol Roger company in Epernay is one of the few French houses in private hands. "There'll be enough for everybody. All that saying, 'Buy now or there won't be anymore,' does is stimulate even more sales."
Champagne, like so much in France, is a subtle and delicate business, with as many layers of complexity as the sparkling wines that take their name from the eastern French region. "What we sell is tears of happiness," Mr. Pol-Roger said down in his cool brick-walled caves, some lined with bottles of his Sir Winston Churchill vintage wine that will stay here for years. "They have to go to university, study at Oxford and get a good education before they go out into the world," he joked.
But he and other Champagne makers do not like to talk about the number of bottles they produce, any more than people of refined manners like to talk about how much money they make. Any suggestion of a glut on the market might cheapen the luxury image, and besides, makers and growers are eager to keep prices stable. They suffered a sharp decline in sales when high prices in the early 1990's collided with an economic recession in Europe and the United States after the Persian Gulf war.
As 2000 approaches, Champagne companies seem to be trying to encourage people to celebrate with serenity -- preferably with Champagne, which is made only here, not the sparkling wines made in Italy, Spain, Germany and the United States, where real Champagne still accounts for only 10 percent of the sparkling wine market.
Early this month, Veuve Clicquot invited more than 600 customers and celebrities, including descendants of Louis XIV, to his Chateau de Versailles, rented by the company for the evening. Ladies in evening gowns and gentlemen in tuxedos sipped Veuve Clicquot La Grande Dame 1990 in the garden before going into the Orangerie to eat their way through lobster ravioli, veal and roast figs, to Champagne accompaniment highlighted by a 1979 La Grande Dame.
The great lady's 19th century namesake, the widow Clicquot, invented the "riddling tables" that are still used to tilt Champagne bottles upside down as they approach the end of the aging process, so that the yeast sediment can collect around the cork to be popped out before the bottles are recorked and sold.
Mireille Guiliano, president of the American subsidiary Clicquot, Inc., said the company would not have thought of putting on such a show if there was any prospect of running out of product to sell before New Year's Eve.
But Veuve Clicquot, like other makers, rationed some big wholesale distributors this year, doling out consignments month by month a little at a time rather than all at once in January and February.
For American retail liquor store owners like Michael A. Correra in Brooklyn, this meant that his distributor, Paramount, told him in early September that it could not supply him with any more Veuve Clicquot yellow-label nonvintage Champagne until October, Mr. Correra said.
He still had plenty on the shelves, at $31.99 a bottle, early this month, but he said: "With Champagne, you can buy it one week, and then the distributor is out another week. I don't know if people are squirreling it away or what, but there's 60 percent more demand this year and only 20 percent more supply."
Mrs. Guiliano said: "You can't just turn the faucet on, of course. There are customers who would like to have three times as much as they will get, but I'm persuaded that if you go to the stores on Dec. 30 you'll still find a wide choice available."
In advertising campaigns earlier this year, Moet & Chandon, the biggest Champagne seller in America and, like Veuve Clicquot and many other houses, owned by the LVMH Moet Hennessy Louis Vuitton conglomerate, was urging customers to "prepare." But, as Philippe Pascal, president of Champagne Veuve Clicquot Ponsardin in Reims, said, "We foresaw the increased demand, and we said let's get ready, because we wanted to avoid too much tension with prices."
French Government regulations and longstanding cooperative arrangements in the Champagne trade provide ways of controlling both quantity and quality that came into play to insure adequate supplies for the millennium.
Nonvintage Champagne is produced by blending wines made from pinot noir, pinot chardonnay and pinot meunier grapes that come from many different vineyards. In good years, around 20 percent of this still wine is held in reserve, "blocked" in bottles or vats to be mixed with wine from poor harvest years, to even out the hills and valleys of the production cycle.
But in the years leading up to 1996, according to Jacques Peters, Veuve Clicquot's cellar master, growers agreed with the Champagne houses to produce enough grapes to increase production to an average of 270 million bottles each year until 2000. At the same time, they agreed to draw down the equivalent of up to 132 million bottles of wine built up in reserve stores. This would add to the mix in the "assemblage," as the process is called in French, that would make the nonvintage Champagne that went on sale this year.
Next year, after all the millennium parties, they expect demand to fall again and plan to build up their reserve supplies over a few years.
"This year's 1999 vintage Champagne should be a great one, unless we get hail or something before the harvest is over," Mr. Peters said, "but it won't be ready to drink until 2006 or 2007." Vintage Champagnes are made only from grapes from the harvest of the vintage year, which is on the label, and usually also have a special name, like La Grande Dame or Sir Winston Churchill.
Distributors in the United States still have special bottles of the acclaimed 1988 and 1990 vintages of many houses, and most makers keep a few hundred or even a thousand bottles of other great years like 1978 and 1983 around for special occasions. These wines go for premium prices; a magnum of 1988 Dom Perignon goes for about $250 this year in New York City, for example.
As for such wines, Frederic Panaiotis, another vintner with Veuve Clicquot Ponsardin, advised: "If you must have a specific vintage from a particular year, don't wait until November to buy it. I personally wouldn't wait until Dec. 24, either."

HEADLINE: Lost Chances to Avert a Disaster;
Competing Interests Stalled New Jersey Flood Project

BYLINE: By ANDREW C. REVKIN 

DATELINE: BOUND BROOK, N.J., Sept. 22

 BODY:
Ever since six men died in a flash flood in 1973, the Army Corps of Engineers has been planning a vast construction project to tame Green Brook and other flood-prone streams around this borough and a dozen nearby communities.
But work has been delayed over and over -- by Federal budget cuts, by drastic changes demanded by Congress, by the corps's cumbersome bureaucracy, by environmental questions, and by political tussles over how to apportion the costs and benefits of the $330 million flood-control plan. 
 And now, as thousands of residents pick through the ruins of precisely the kind of flooding the corps set out to prevent a quarter-century ago, officials are left to contemplate both a meteorological nightmare and a bureaucratic one.
"If we had better foresight, that project could've been done now and Bound Brook wouldn't have been flooded," said Clark Gilman, 60, the chief of flood-plain management for the New Jersey Department of Environmental Protection, who has been working on the plan for 25 of the 30 years he has held the position. "It's real sad. Nero fiddled and now Rome's burned."
Corps officials who assessed the damage this week found that flooding from Tropical Storm Floyd was two feet higher than even the theorized catastrophe that they had used as a worst-case scenario. What had been a computer-generated line on their maps was now drawn on the town's streets and buildings in rainbow stains of oil and brown smudges of sewage and mud.
And as Bound Brook, Green Brook Township, Middlesex and other communities in the low plains of northern New Jersey continued to tally losses from the floods, debate intensified over the long-planned, but never-executed Green Brook Flood Control Project.
It was only out of respect for the two people who died when the Raritan River and Green Brook combined to immerse this place that some officials refrained from saying "I told you so." But several came close.
William F. Slezak, the civil works chief in the corps's regional headquarters in Manhattan, said that despite the epic dimensions of this deluge, it would have been largely held back by the planned network of levees, channels and flood walls. The corps, saddled with a reputation for excessive building and wasteful spending, this time appeared to have the right idea. The plan called for levees with an extra three feet of height, just in case.
"It just tears your heart out, the fact that we just weren't able to provide the protection in time," Mr. Slezak said.
The long, strange history of the Green Brook Flood Control Project is a study in the tortuous course that must be run by complicated engineering projects, particularly when they span 3 counties and 13 municipalities, as this one does.
Over two decades, competing political interests at the downstream and upstream ends of Green Brook pressed different representatives in Congress either to drop aspects of the plan or to change its focus, each time causing delays. Communities with fewer flooding problems questioned whether they should have to share costs with those, like Bound Brook, that faced the biggest risks.
The debate played out year after year in The Courier News of Bridgewater and other local newspapers. Even though the Federal Government would pay 75 percent of the costs and the state 19 percent, elected officials focused on the impact on municipal and county budgets.
Another impediment was simply the peculiar kind of amnesia that settles in after disasters, said Eric Evenson, the New Jersey district chief for the United States Geological Survey.
"It's my personal observation that the public at large, between droughts and floods, tends to forget," Mr. Evenson said. "The level of urgency for dealing with rare events just isn't there."
No one has ever doubted the potential for devastation when the lacework of rivers and brooks that feed into the Raritan River swells during extreme storms.
The plain below the 500-foot ridges known as the Watchung Mountains has flooded at least since Colonial times, with Green Brook developing a particularly nasty reputation as an amplifying funnel for water cascading from the hills.
Through the late 1880's and into this century, floods along Green Brook occurred regularly. Finally, in August 1932, New Jersey officials took the first stab at finding ways to hold back the waters, publishing a report titled "Control of Floods on Green Brook in the Counties of Middlesex, Somerset and Union."
Nothing came of it.
It took back-to-back severe floods in 1971 and then 1973, and the loss of six lives in the 1973 flash flood, which occurred on a tributary of Green Brook, to prompt new action. Altogether, floods along the Green Brook watershed from 1900 to 1996 caused $2.4 billion in damage, the Army Corps of Engineers has calculated.
In 1973, with strong local support, the corps began assessing whether levees, concrete channels and other means could minimize damage.
At the same time, New Jersey empowered communities that shared flooding problems to form flood control commissions, and the Green Brook Flood Control Commission was formed. It remains the only such commission in the state, Mr. Gilman said.
The group, comprising representatives from each county and town, met 10 times a year, year after year, working as a prod to the Army Corps and as a lobby in Washington to try to push Congress to keep the money moving.
It took the corps seven years to devise a preliminary plan. In 1980, the experts concluded that a project tightly focused on preventing flooding in the hardest-hit areas, like Bound Brook, could save lives and money. That conclusion met the agency's rules that economic benefits outweigh costs.
But prosperous suburbs farther up the Green Brook watershed, like Berkeley Heights and Scotch Plains, were upset that they were left out. They, after all, had suffered some of the worst effects of past floods, recalled Vernon A. Noble, 80, a former Mayor of Green Brook who has been chairman of the flood commission since its inception.
The commission pressed New Jersey's Congressional delegation to force the Army Corps to redo its plan.
But it had to wait six years to get its wish.
Pressure for the plan ebbed as memories of the floods faded. At the same time, as part of a war on pork-barrel spending, President Ronald Reagan cut nearly all money for water projects around the country.
Mr. Noble and other commission members -- who are unpaid -- made annual pilgrimages to Washington to testify about the need for protection. They got their five minutes, sandwiched between delegations from other states.
In 1986, Congress authorized money for the Green Brook project, but only if the Army Corps expanded it to include protection for communities all along the stream, not just at the soggiest end.
The engineers had concluded that this was not economically justified, but Congress essentially demanded that they find a way to justify it, said Mr. Gilman, the New Jersey flood official.
"That sent us back to square one," said Mr. Slezak of the Army Corps regional office. By the late 1980's, the plan had to change fundamentally anyway, he said, because so much time had passed that the entire area had changed. There were corporate parks, parking lots and shopping centers where there had been vacant land -- some in spots where levees were supposed to go. More years slid by.
Private environmental groups also began to fight more vigorously, opposing two big flood-control reservoirs that were planned for wooded, wild areas at the base of the Watchung range. Such basins hold back flood waters temporarily and release them slowly.
The groups also contended that the plan might encourage more development close to rivers, said Sunil Somalwar, a physics professor at Rutgers University who is a vice chairman of the New Jersey chapter of the Sierra Club.
"For a lot of people, there's this promise that once the Army Corps steps in, floods will disappear, so let's go build close to the river," he said.
The project also lost steam within the corps as managers came and went, lured to higher-paying jobs in industry, Mr. Slezak said. Other water experts in the project point to the frequent rotation of officers through the top corps post in the region, which caused a loss of momentum.
Budgets also tightened again as the Clinton Administration cut Federal spending on flood projects within individual states, New Jersey and corps officials said.
A bad flood in October 1996 reinvigorated the flood plan.
But the following summer, it was changed once again. Senator Robert G. Torricelli, after strong lobbying by residents of Berkeley Heights, pushed through a law prohibiting the corps from building the basins at the upstream end. Over the years, the upstream communities had become more focused on environmental issues and less on the flood threat.
That part of the project was put on hold so that construction around Bound Brook could begin. Many officials involved in the plan say they doubt the upper part of the Green Brook project will ever be built.
In essence, a flood-control plan that began in 1980 as a focused effort in the lower portion of Green Brook, then was expanded at the behest of Congress, now reverted to its original dimensions. Along the way, a decade was lost.
In August, Gov. Christine Todd Whitman and Federal officials gathered in a Bound Brook park to announce that ground would finally be broken in the spring of 2000 on the first stage, the replacement of a 68-year-old bridge.
Now that park is a swamp, and the curbs of adjacent streets are banked high with discarded furniture.
Until late this summer, some residents of Bound Brook were still trying to block parts of the plan. And even now, others along the watershed, including some officials from neighboring Middlesex County, still question the need for the levees and pumping stations. Local environmental groups, and the state's Sierra Club chapter, still oppose the plan, saying the government would do better if it bought homes and businesses in harm's way.
In case the project drags on, corps officials this week said they were planning to update the old brochure promoting the flood plan -- adding photographs of the latest disaster.
As the remains of Floyd blew in on Sept. 16, J. B. Wiley Jr., an engineering consultant for the local flood control commission, was in his house in Bedminster Township, 20 miles upstream from Bound Brook, watching the upper Raritan River rise.
"When the water came across the field and began lapping against the road, I knew it was going to be bad down there," he said. He jumped in his car and drove toward the borough. "I was thinking about all those nice people who'd said this would never happen, therefore we didn't need the project," he said.
He arrived at Bound Brook as the evacuations began. "Watching all those poor people," he said, "really fired up my determination to see this thing through."

Chart/Map: "Fixing a Flood Zone"
Bound Brook lies at the southern end of a 12 mile corridor in northern New Jersey that has been slated for a flood-control system for more than two decades. Although too late for the deluge that overwhelmed Bound Brook this month, construction of the system, detailed here, is scheduled to begin next spring.
 
1. DRY DETENTION BASINS
Upstream embankments designed to temporarily pool water during heavy storms, protecting communities downstream. Construction of these is on hold.
 
2. CHANNEL MODIFICATION
Widening and deepening of the brook to improve flow.
 
3. LEVEES AND FLOOD WALLS
Mounds of earth (levees) or concrete barriers (flood walls) designed to hold back high water.
 
(Sources: U.S. Army Corps of Engineers; Federal Emergency Management Agency)(Illustrations by Tom Zeller/The New York Times)(pg. 43)

HEADLINE: In Praise of Country Churches

BYLINE: By RICHARD RUDA; RICHARD RUDA, who lives in Maryland, is a lawyer with an interest in architecture.

 BODY:
EVERY fan of Sir John Betjeman, the late poet laureate of Britain, is aware that parish church-crawling is a national pastime in England. "I know no greater pleasure than church-crawling," Sir John wrote. "It leads you to the remotest and quietest country. It introduces you to the history of England in stone and wood and glass which is always truer than what you read in books. You meet all sorts of people on your travels."
What Sir John's fans may not know is that his observations apply equally to the Colonial churches of rural eastern Virginia. Nor is this surprising. When they were built in the 17th and 18th centuries, Virginia was an English colony, and the Church of England was the established church in Virginia. The majority of churches built in Virginia before the Revolutionary War served parishes of this established church. After the war and disestablishment, these churches became Episcopal, and most remain so today. But Virginia's Colonial parish churches still have this in common with their Anglican counterparts in England: they are laden with historical associations and are architecturally delightful. 
 There are more than 50 pre-Revolutionary churches in eastern Virginia -- virtually all made of brick, thanks to an abundance of clay in the region. Most of the churches are still in use, and all are between a 15-minute to three-hour drive of Washington. I recently revisited 10 rural masterpieces -- Virginia's urban churches are frequently overwhelmed by their late 20th-century surroundings -- and returned more convinced than ever that Colonial church-crawling in Virginia is a must for the historically and architecturally minded traveler.
What should you look for in a Colonial church in Virginia? Here are a few suggestions, together with my favorite examples: churchyard and setting (St. Peter's), porches, towers and other exterior features (Yeocomico), brickwork of walls (Abingdon; Vawter's), decorative work around doors and windows (Aquia; Christ Church, Lancaster County), Colonial furnishings (Aquia; Christ Church), Colonial gravestones (Abingdon), the indefinable quality of instantly evoking the Colonial past (Merchant's Hope), and sheer architectural excellence (Christ Church, in a class by itself in America).
Aquia Church, 38 miles south of Washington and three miles north of Stafford, just off the traveler's inferno that is I-95, is a perfect place to begin. It excels in virtually every way that a Colonial church can excel. The sophistication of its craftsmanship and design is due to its relatively late date of construction, 1751-57, and the affluence of its congregation of tobacco planters. Several of Aquia's fine exterior features are immediately striking -- the elaborate stone decoration of its doorways and quoins (carved stones at corners where walls meet) and its 36 large windows, rectangular on the first story and arched on the second.
But Aquia's true glory is its interior. Try to visit on a sunny day, when the effect of light pouring in the 36 windows, together with the spaciousness and brilliance of its two-story interior in pure white, is literally breathtaking. Aquia's triple-decker pulpit with an intricate sounding board, its architectural altarpiece and its elaborate gallery are all original and superb in quality and state of preservation.
From Aquia I drove farther south on I-95 until I escaped onto U.S. 17 south, one of eastern Virginia's many scenic rural highways. At Loretto I stopped to admire Vawter's Church (1731). It is a charming building surrounded by cedar and walnut trees. Vawter's has some of Virginia's finest Colonial brickwork -- its walls laid in Flemish bond and its two doorways made of decorative rubbed brick.
Continuing south on U.S. 17, I crossed the Rappahannock River at Tappahannock and ventured on to the intensely historic peninsula known to Virginians as the Northern Neck. It is home to two of the most interesting Colonial buildings in the United States: Yeocomico Church near Hague, and Christ Church, Lancaster County, near Irvington.
Just finding Yeocomico on a tiny road two miles east of Hague is something of an accomplishment. Built in 1706 and named after a nearby river, it is one of the most wonderful -- and overlooked -- of America's Colonial buildings.
The most striking feature of diminutive Yeocomico Church is its decorated porch. It encloses an extraordinary late medieval artifact, what James Scott Rawlings describes as "an enormous Tudor battened door that also includes the only wicket door known in Colonial America." The wicket is a smaller door within a door for use in bad weather. The great door of Yeocomico weighs 1,000 pounds and is said to be a vestige of the earlier wooden church of 1655.
Other exterior features not to be missed are the incised initials and decorations on the south and east walls discreetly placed there, after the custom, by Yeocomico's Colonial vestrymen and the craftsmen who built the church. And be sure to have a look at the 1717 sundial in the churchyard, and the graves of Daniel McCarty, and his wife, Elizabeth Pope, adjacent to the east wall. McCarty was speaker of the Virginia House of Burgesses from 1715 to 1718. Pope, who had four children from her first marriage and eight with McCarty, died in 1716 at age 39.
During my midweek visit I was lucky enough to run into the then-rector of Yeocomico, the Rev. Philip Wainwright, who showed me the interior of the church. Although extensively altered in post-Colonial times, the interior is just as delightful as the exterior and contains a noteworthy Colonial communion table. As we left the church, the rector ruefully remarked, "I can't understand why we don't get busloads of visitors here."
Christ Church, Lancaster County, built in the early 1730's, is just outside Irvington at the southern end of the Northern Neck. It is one of America's finest Colonial buildings -- elegant, majestic, superbly proportioned, a building of timeless beauty. As no less an authority than G. E. Kidder Smith puts it in his Source Book of American Architecture, Christ Church exhibits "a perfection that makes it a strong contender for the most handsome church exterior in the United States." Its designer is unknown.
Christ Church is operated by a foundation and is open every day from April through Thanksgiving. Visitors should start in the excellent small museum next door. There is a 12-minute videotape and exhibits on the history of the church, the design and use of Colonial churches, and the Virginia planter who had the church built, Robert (King) Carter, who was very rich and highly cultured. He and his two wives are buried in elaborate raised tombs next to the church.
Friendly and knowledgeable guides are available to escort visitors through the church interior. The Colonial furnishings of the 1730's are of singular interest and importance, and include a combination of triple-decker pulpit and high box pews that is rare today even in England. (Your guide may encourage you to try out the Carters' immense pew.) The stone floor is original; the stone comes from the Isle of Purbeck in Dorset. It is the same stone used since medieval times to decorate English churches and cathedrals.
From Christ Church I drove south 27 miles to Ware Church, built in the early 18th century, just east of Gloucester, and Abingdon Church (1755) seven miles south of Ware. Both have superb brickwork and exceptionally interesting churchyards. Abingdon, in particular, is built of brick that is ethereal in color, salmon pink and pastel gray, and has a wonderful array of Colonial gravestones.
My next stop was St. Luke's Church, near Smithfield. I always approach St. Luke's, a red brick church set back from Route 10, with trepidation. On one hand it is a genuine late (very late) Gothic parish church that must be seen -- its Gothic tower, buttresses and windows are fascinating and unique in this country. On the other hand, the date of 1632 claimed for the church is almost certainly 50 years too early, and the late 19th-century stained glass that fills the windows is very distracting. (Fortunately, the electronic carillon in the tower was broken during my visit.)
After leaving the church, I headed west on Route 10, another scenic and historic Virginia byway. Fifteen miles from St. Luke's is Bacon's Castle, a mid-17th-century brick manor house with extraordinary Jacobean features, which is open to visitors from March to November. A half mile farther are the eerie remains of Lower Southwark Church (1754), an imposing rectangular structure that burned in 1868 and was never rebuilt, together with its ancient churchyard. Visit at dusk, as I did, but not on Halloween.
Continuing west on Route 10 for another 30 miles (and then half a mile south on State Road 641) is one of my favorite Virginia buildings, Merchant's Hope Church. Its likely date of construction is 1657, which makes it the oldest church in the state still serving a congregation. A small rectangular structure, it has a charming "kick" in the eaves, decorative brickwork in the gables, and Colonial doors. Because of its simplicity, authenticity and isolated location on a back road miles from the nearest town, Merchant's Hope is uncanny in the way it evokes the Colonial past.
I ended my church crawl at St. Peter's, New Kent County, 20 miles north of Merchant's Hope via Route 106. This delightful building should be enjoyed, not analyzed. The approach to the church and the churchyard are the most magnificent in Virginia. The brick church building dates from 1703, with an imposing tower added 40 years later. The Flemish strapwork above the gables may be fanciful, but the walls and tower are original and the ensemble is extremely picturesque.
In the 1960's the interior of St. Peter's was stripped down to the bare brick walls and dirt floor and meticulously restored to a late 17th-century appearance. Its restored interior furnishings and windows include a triple-decker pulpit, a beautiful east window and the seal of Queen Anne (monarch in 1703). But the outstanding features of the interior are two 18th-century wall monuments. One, carved in Bristol in 1737, is fully equal in quality to similar monuments in English churches.
I had phoned ahead in order to see the interior on a weekday. The Rev. Brian McGurk met me at the church and shared some of its ample history. St. Peter's has close associations with Martha and George Washington, Robert E. Lee of the Confederacy and Gen. George McClellan of the Union. Some people think that the Washingtons were married at St. Peter's and call it "the first church of the first First Lady." Historians are unsure whether the wedding took place there or at the nearby Custis Plantation. I like to think that George and Martha had the good sense to get married at one of Virginia's most charming Colonial churches.
 
Gravestones, box pews and triple-decker pulpits
Although Newport News, Hampton and Norfolk were declared part of a major disaster area after Hurricane Floyd hit Sept. 16, the storm turned northeast at this point and did not enter Chesapeake Bay, sparing the churches described here except for minor damage to trees and grounds.
 
Touring the Churches
Christ Church, Lancaster County, (804) 438-6855, and St. Luke's Church, (757) 357-3367, are operated by foundations and regularly open to visitors. Christ Church and its museum are open every day from April through November; St. Luke's, every day except Monday from February to December. Admission is free, although donations are accepted.
All the other churches, except Lower Southwark, serve active parishes. They are open for services on Sundays, and while visitors can't explore the inside of a church during a service, they can usually duck in before or after. You can try to call ahead to arrange to see a church interior on a weekday, but keep in mind that most interiors have been greatly altered over the centuries and that the principal Colonial features of many churches are on the exterior and in the churchyard.
 
Getting There
Aquia Church is on a hill on the northeast corner of the intersection of State Road 610 and U.S. 1, just east of Exit 143 off I-95.
Vawter's Church is on the east side of U.S. 17 in Loretto, 25 miles south of Exit 126 off I-95.
Yeocomico Church is near Hague in Westmoreland County, on the Potomac side of the Northern Neck. It is reached via State Highway 202, which connects Templeman and Cal-
lao. Take 202 to its intersection with State Road 604 (Sandy Point Road), two and a half miles south of Hague. Go east on 604 for one and a half miles to State Road 606 (Old Yeocomico Road). Turn left onto 606 and Yeocomico Church will be on your left in less than a mile.
Christ Church, Lancaster County, just outside Irvington on Route 646, can be reached via State Highway 200 from Kilmarnock (from the north) or Irvington (from the south).
Ware Church and Abingdon Church are both near Gloucester, in Gloucester County. Ware is a mile east of Gloucester, on the south side of State Highway 3/14. Abingdon is six miles south of Gloucester, on the east side of U.S. 17.
St. Luke's is at the intersection of State Highways 10 and 32, five miles southeast of Smithfield.
The ruins of Lower Southwark Church are on the south side of Highway 10, 11 miles west of Smithfield.
To get to Merchant's Hope, six miles east of Hopewell, continue west on 10 to its intersection with State Road 641 (Merchant's Hope Road), 30 miles west of Lower Southwark. Turn left on 641 and the church will be on your right in half a mile.
St. Peter's in New Kent County can be reached via State Highway 106 from the Merchant's Hope vicinity or I-64 east from Richmond (use Exit 211). Take 106 north to its intersection with State Highway 249 (one and a half miles north of I-64), where 106 becomes State Road 609 (Old Church Road). Continue north on 609 for less than a mile to the intersection with State Road 642 (St. Peter's Lane). Turn right on 642, which takes you to the gates of the churchyard.
 
Where to Stay
Southeastern Virginia has innumerable chain motels; on my most recent trip I stayed 500 yards from St. Luke's at the Econo Lodge, 20080 Brewers Neck Boulevard, in Benn's Church; (800) 553-2666. My spotless room, with microwave and refrigerator, cost $36. On other trips I have been very happy at Marriott's Towne Place Suites, 200 A/B Cybernetics Way, Newport News; (800) 228-9290. All rooms have kitchens; a two-bedroom suite is $119.
Church-crawling in rural Virginia need not be an exercise in abnegation, however. In Irvington, close by Christ Church, are the Tides Inn, 480 King Carter Drive, (800) 843-3746), and the less formal Tides Lodge, 1 St. Andrews Lane, (800) 248-4337. Both have extensive golfing facilities and marinas. Doubles about $170 at both hotels; package plans available. In downtown Richmond, an easy drive from St. Peter's and Merchant's Hope, is the magnificent 1895 Jefferson Hotel, at the corner of Franklin and Adams Streets, (800) 424-8014, fax (804) 225-0334, a registered historic landmark. Rooms from $195, double occupancy; suites from $295.
The Linden House Bed and Breakfast Plantation, 11770 Tidewater Trail, (800) 622-1202, also on the National Register of Historic Places, is a restored planter's home built in 1750. It is on a quiet stretch of U.S. 17 in Champlain, five miles south of Vawter's Church, less than an hour's drive from Yeocomico Church. Rooms: $85 to $135, with breakfast.
 
Books and Maps
The state road map published by the Virginia Department of Transportation is excellent for planning and also indicates scenic byways. It can be obtained from state tourism offices along Interstates and from the Virginia Department of Transportation, Administrative Services Division, 1401 East Broad Street, Richmond, Va. 23219; (804) 786-2838. The Virginia Atlas and Gazetteer (DeLorme, $16.95) is indispensable for finding your way to (and from) churches like Yeocomico, Christ Church and Merchant's Hope.
Two invaluable books, both unfortunately out of print, are George Carrington Mason's "Colonial Churches of Tidewater Virginia" (Whittet & Shepperson, 1945) and James Scott Rawlings's "Virginia's Colonial Churches: An Architectural Guide" (Garrett & Massie, 1963). Church crawlers may also find inspiration in Sir John Betjeman's "In Praise of Churches" (John Murray, 1996), an anthology of Sir John's inimitable prose and verse about English parish churches. RICHARD RUDA

HEADLINE: A Refuge for Urbanites in Back Country Virginia

BYLINE: By JAMES R. HARDCASTLE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Va.

 BODY:
BECAUSE it is in the rugged Blue Ridge Mountains and is not served by railroads or major highways, Rappahannock County's residential and commercial growth has lagged far behind the counties that abut it on three sides, which are considered part of the Baltimore-Washington metropolitan area.
This suits most people here just fine because the county's natural beauty and relaxed pace of life are what makes it such a good place to live, says John McCarthy, the County Administrator. Between 1930 and 1970, the county's population declined from 7,717 to 5,199 as the move from farms to the cities continued. 
 However, slow but steady construction of new homes since -- currently at a rate of about 50 a year, with some costing more than $1 million -- has brought the population of the 267-square-mile county to about 7,200 as it becomes an important getaway for affluent city dwellers and a year-round home to urban refugees.
Dr. Werner Krebser, the chairman of the County Planning Commission, said there had been general agreement between old-time landowners and newcomers on a slow-growth policy. Since 1966, the county zoning ordinance has required a minimum residential lot size of 25 acres on most of the land outside its four hamlets and the Town of Washington, the county seat.
There are no supermarkets, convenience stores, fast food restaurants or stoplights in the county. Day-to-day needs are met by traditional general stores, which also serve as community gathering places. (The county does, however, have a widely known and luxurious restaurant and hotel, the Inn at Little Washington.)
The absence of these amenities is one of the main reasons why James Abdo, a Washington, D.C., real estate developer, said he bought a 60-acre farm south of Sperryville from David Brinkley, the retired news commentator. "I hope I don't live to see them there," he added.
Mr. Abdo, who is 39 and single, says he drives the 80 miles from the nation's capital to the 60-acre farm he bought seven and a half years ago for $335,000 nearly every weekend. He said he wanted to expand the main house, but that he been holding off until he meets "the woman of my dreams."
"This is a very special place to me," he said. "It's part of the fabric of my life. I want it to be a special place for that person, too."
Mr. Brinkley, who owned the farm for more than 30 years, wasn't the only notable to find the county a convenient place for a hideaway or a year-round home.
The conductor Lorin Maazel has built a house, guest cottages and a recording studio on a 193-acre farm here. He is also a key supporter of the Hearthstone School, which was built on his property and which his young children attend. Former United States Senator Eugene McCarthy, the political cartoonist Jeff MacNelly and the political commentators Rowland Evans and James Kilpatrick also maintain homes here.
David Adoff, a real estate agent, and his wife, Hildy Teegen, an associate professor at George Washington University in the nation's capitol, decided to settle here after driving through the county on their way to the Shenandoah National Park four years ago.
WHAT clinched their decision to buy a home here was a chance stop at the Mountainside Market, a 4,000-square-foot general store/deli/laundromat built along the road to the national park that serves as the community hangout for the artists, musicians and alternative culture element in Rappahannock County.
After the couple looked at the store's eclectic deli menu and inspected its diverse stock -- which includes art objects and shelves of vegetarian foods, herbal and homeopathic medicines and 150 types of beer -- "we decided that if the community can support this kind of food and drink, this is a place we want to live," he said.
Mr. Adoff, a former home builder who publishes an online guide to the county and works part-time for a real estate firm, and his wife have since purchased three lots along a hollow leading up to the Skyline Drive. They have also built a 2,000-square-foot, cedar-sided home with views of the surrounding mountains on one five-acre lot and a 1,000-square-foot guest house, which they rent out to tourists, on a four-acre lot. All three of the lots were laid out prior to the passage of the 25-acre zoning ordinance.
Recently, the couple put the house up for sale for $279,000 and the cabin for $160,000 so they can build a new house elsewhere in the county.
Mr. Adoff said that because of the 25-acre zoning, Rappahannock County homes are expensive. Residential lots will sell for $3,000 to $5,000 per acre, depending upon the view. To this must be added the cost of digging a well, installing a septic field and building a driveway.
Because of the expense of housing, the typical buyer is a professional in his or her 50's or 60's who wants to spend weekends in the county until moving there permanently at retirement, Mr. Adoff said.
A powerful visitor's draw to the county is the Inn at Little Washington, an 80-seat restaurant and a 14-room inn that is consistently ranked as one of the finest country inns in the nation. The inn draws its name from a local name for the Town of Washington, population 190. It is named for George Washington, who as a young surveyor laid out its five-block by two-block street grid in 1749.
The inn's co-owner and chef, Patrick O'Connell, a native of Washington, D.C., said he had fallen in love with the county's countryside as a child in family drives to Luray, Va. In 1967, the self-taught chef purchased a three-acre property near here while he was still in his early 20's.
Five years later, he purchased a 100-acre farm at the foot of nearby Old Rag Mountain, where he raised vegetables for sale to health-food shops in the District of Columbia. His vegetable business soon evolved into a catering enterprise that took him into the homes of many well-to-do families in the Virginia hunt country which includes the eastern part of Rappahannock County.
Learning of his clients' need for a place where they could entertain, he and his partner, Reinhardt Lynch, leased half of an old garage in the Town of Washington for $200 month and converted it into the restaurant. The inn opened in 1978 and over the years, the lush rooms, with rates of $340 to $640 a night, not including meals, and a $5 million kitchen wing have been added.
All along the way, the Inn has eagerly sought out specialty products, such as smoked trout, fallow deer, exotic vegetables, mushrooms and rabbits from local farmers. In recent months, David Cole, the former president of America Online's Internet services unit, announced plans to convert two historic buildings across the street from the inn into a center that would retail premium organic food items such as the Kobe beef and premium berries he is raising at his nearby Sunnyside Farm.
One of the buildings would also become headquarters for walnutacres.com, an online organic-foods store that would sell products from Sunnyside and Walnut Acres Organic Farms, a leading provider of organic foods based Penns Creek, Pa., that Mr. Cole acquired a controlling interest in this summer.

HEADLINE: Philanthropy The Smart Way

BYLINE: By Ron Chernow; Ron Chernow is the author of "Titan: The Life of John D. Rockefeller Sr." He is currently working on a biography of Alexander Hamilton.

 BODY:
In his celebrated 1889 essay "Wealth," Andrew Carnegie admonished fellow grandees of the Gilded Age that "the man who dies thus rich dies disgraced." Carnegie devoutly believed that businessmen should repay their debt to society, applying their skills to philanthropic enterprise. He wanted active involvement, not just a fistful of checks.
If necessary, Carnegie favored stiff inheritance taxes that would force the rich to disgorge their money to society rather than leave it to pampered heirs. True to his beliefs, Carnegie, by his death in 1919, had divested more than 95 percent of his fortune. 
 Bill Gates has now transferred $17 billion to the Bill and Melinda Gates Foundation, including a gift of $1 billion for college scholarships to minority students, suggesting that our software king may have heeded Carnegie's message. Skeptics may discern an attempt by Mr. Gates to burnish his image or deflect attention from the Microsoft antitrust trial. But whatever his motives, he has begun a metamorphosis from businessman to philanthropist, something far too few of his business peers have made a serious effort to do. He ought to be be widely emulated, lest history castigate ours as an age of oversized egos, self-indulgence and hubris about our technological supremacy.
We are quick to revile the so-called robber barons of the Gilded Age, perhaps to assert our own moral superiority. We denounce their rapacious monopolies and brutal labor practices, their flagrant contempt for public opinion. And our image of their social life is a gaudy cartoon of private railroad cars, sybaritic balls in Newport cottages and luxurious steam yachts.
While the stereotype contains much truth, it overlooks a redeeming aspect of that opulent time: our harshest industrial overlords proved our most enlightened philanthropists. The lives of John D. Rockefeller and Andrew Carnegie, tough men from hardscrabble backgrounds who lacked college education, furnish rich lessons for would-be benefactors. They transcended the sentiment and haphazard methods of Victorian charity, substituting the rigor of modern philanthropy. Instead of sponsoring another hospital or museum wing, Carnegie and Rockefeller promoted ideas.
Rockefeller, for example, bankrolled the campus for Spelman College in Atlanta, dedicated to educating black women, and the University of Chicago. He provided backing for many medical triumphs: a treatment for meningitis, a campaign that cured 500,000 cases of hookworm in the South and a vaccine against yellow fever.
Gilded Age moguls operated in a poisonous atmosphere of suspicion since their fortunes and industrial empires seemed menacing to a public reared in a more innocent, pastoral America. By forcing them to exercise extreme care in their charitable priorities, the truculent mood of the day may actually have had a salutary effect on Rockefeller and Carnegie. Both saw philanthrophy as something embedded deep in belief systems that viewed money-making, in isolation, as harmful to society.
As conservative businessmen, Rockefeller and Carnegie frowned on projects that might breed passivity. Carnegie's promise to build a splendid library building for any town came with a catch: municipal authorities had to provide a central site, stock the building with books and guarantee maintenance money. In exchange for coveted Carnegie pensions for professors, universities needed to satisfy strict standards of educational excellence. Both Rockefeller and Carnegie enshrined a momentous principle: the philanthropist should serve as catalyst, not owner, of his creations.
Bolstered by vast endowments and broad charters, the Carnegie Corporation (1911) and the Rockefeller Foundation (1913) gradually acquired a large measure of autonomy from their founders. While huge, multi-purpose foundations are today rife with problems, ranging from bureaucratic rigidity to lack of focus to political faddishness, they remain part of an implicit social contract that stipulates that wealth, beyond a certain point, should revert to society.
It is strange, then, that the American public has been so tolerant of the huge agglomerations of money in the 1980's and 90's. We have a generation of young businessmen accorded the adulation due to folk heroes because they have created "shareholder value" -- a euphemism for higher stock prices. Nearly 20 years of high employment, low inflation, steady growth and a booming stock market (the great American antidote to class conflict) have dulled demands for inventive, large-scale philanthropy.
Surely one can applaud the business accomplishments of our titans in high technology, biotechnology and finance without imagining that they have thereby exhausted their obligations to society. But the political pressure that prompted Carnegie and Rockefeller to creative breakthroughs has been conspicuously missing.
On paper, anyway, it would appear that Americans in recent years haven't stinted on charity -- last year they boosted their annual giving to a record $175 billion. Still, cynics cannot be blamed for wondering about the motives behind it all, as the ambitious jockey for seats on prestigious nonprofit boards and tables at charity balls. Our era has seen plenty of old-fashioned gifts to cultural, medical and educational institutions, with the donors' names prominently gracing doorways. Yes, we should doubtless be grateful that such gifts keep alive many an artistic group or drug abuse program that might otherwise wither. The real problem is the absence of adventurous initiatives that seek social change in a manner beyond the capacity of government.
For that reason, one fervently hopes that the Gateses' largesse -- along with commitments made in recent years by George Soros, Ted Turner and other super-rich business figures -- will stimulate many laggards to follow suit. When a Warren Buffett, the nation's second richest man, elects to defer major donations until after his death, he deprives us not just of his money but of his acumen.
An age of business innovation should be counterbalanced by analogous ingenuity in good works. One is heartened by the small-scale "venture philanthropy" undertaken by some Silicon Valley denizens who have shown business savvy, a hands-on style and a quest for measurable results in their giving. And several businessmen -- including Theodore J. Forstmann, the Wall Street financier, and Eli Broad, the chairman of Sun America -- have showered huge sums on education in the form of scholarships, vouchers, awards and training grants, to provoke overdue reform.
The brains and abundant resources now exist for the greatest flowering of philanthropy in American history. Let us hope that the acquisitive streak in our culture will be matched by a new altruism. Otherwise, history may stigmatize us as a second Gilded Age, but one devoid of the audacious giving that proved the saving grace of the first.

HEADLINE: TECHNOLOGY;
A Founder of a Year 2000 Service Quits to Try a Different Approach

BYLINE: By BARNABY J. FEDER 

 BODY:
Saying that people are losing interest in efforts to organize neighborhoods and communities for potential Year 2000 computer threats, the co-founder of an Internet-based clearinghouse for such grass-roots activities has resigned as the service's research director.
"We seem to have hit a wall," said Paloma O'Riley, who co-founded the clearinghouse, the Cassandra Project, in June of 1997. Her activism brought invitations to testify before Congress and made her a leader in the pursuit of grass-roots responses to potential Year 2000 problems. 
 "People are developing a false sense of security," she said last week.
Far from abandoning Year 2000 work though, Ms. O'Riley said she was leaving Cassandra to press public officials to prepare more aggressively for possible disasters. Her new focus on political advocacy might have jeopardized Cassandra's tax-exempt status had she stayed, she said.
Cassandra (www.cassandraproject.org) will continue under Catherine Moyer, a Denver-based data-base designer who co-founded the group and had already assumed much of the public appearance responsibilities from Ms. O'Riley in recent months.
The clearinghouse is being redesigned to focus more on specific areas of concern, including preparations at mental health centers and environmental threats from Year 2000 problems, Ms. Moyer said. The site lists several hundred Year 2000 community groups in the United States and overseas, but no one is sure how many are active.
Ms. O'Riley said advocates of individual and community preparedness had been undermined by the steadily increasing drumbeat of optimistic news about the nation's readiness for 2000.
The final blow, she said, was the recent assertion by the Federal Reserve chairman, Alan Greenspan, that there was a "negligible" chance of widespread disruption of banks and other key institutions in the United States. Mr. Greenspan warned that fear of Year 2000 disruptions was more likely to hurt the economy than was the computer problem itself, especially if people emptied grocery store shelves and bank accounts in anticipation of widespread disasters when computers failed to recognize "00" as 2000.
In the current atmosphere, Ms. O'Riley said, even those officials who believe that it makes sense to prepare for disruptions like loss of power, phones or water are afraid they will be stymied and exposed to ridicule if they act on those beliefs. To counter the inertia, Ms. O'Riley is hoping to come up with ways to make local officials believe that they could face legal or financial liabilities if their failure to act results in breakdowns in local services or public safety.
But Steven Davis, a former computer manager for Montgomery County, Md., who frequently works with community-based Year 2000 programs, said: "Threatening to hold government officials accountable is a tough way to get them to act. I wish her luck."
Ms. O'Riley, a 44-year-old New York native, became a believer in the value of self-sufficiency while living in rural Alaska with her husband and two sons in the 1980's. She first encountered Year 2000 computer issues as a computer security officer at the Oakland Naval Hospital in 1990 during the war in the Persian Gulf. A stint as the Year 2000 project manager for the Rover Group Ltd. in England several years later convinced her that disruptions could be global and widespread.
Ms. O'Riley decided to leave Rover and concentrate on raising public awareness of the potential Year 2000 threat. She and her husband, Kevin, chose Colorado as their base of operations. Mr. O'Riley is a mechanical engineer at the Maxtor Corporation, a maker of computer hard drives in Longmont, Colo., and is active in that company's Year 2000 program.

HEADLINE: Media Talk;
New Magazine to Challenge Los Angeles

BYLINE: By CHRISTIAN BERTHELSEN 

 BODY:
Ask magazine insiders why Los Angeles cannot support more than one city glossy, and they will offer a handful of theories: the city is too disparate demographically; the upstarts are of poor quality; Angelenos, they contend, don't read.
In 37 years, one city magazine , Los Angeles, has survived. Next month, another challenger, Channel Los Angeles, will be spun off from Channel Miami, owned by a former stockbroker, Richard Bronson. It will be mailed to high-income households and given away at trendy boutiques, with the hope of eventually drawing paying subscribers. 
To distinguish itself, Channel is taking an approach that makes the soft-focus Los Angeles seem hard-boiled. The first issue, scheduled for Oct. 13, is heavy on celebrity sightings, with profiles of the actress Angela Bassett and Randy Gerber, a nightclub proprietor, and an article on the fashion accessories of celebrities' dogs.
"What Channel magazine won't be doing is nasty expose stories," said Sue Cameron, the magazine's executive editor. What there will be, she said, is a plastic surgery column written by the former Manhattan madam Sidney Biddle Barrows.
The economics of the industry require a delicate balance. The key is reaching the most affluent readers in the city to draw advertisers, though that market ends up being minuscule -- many place it around 200,000 readers. Los Angeles already has 183,000 of those readers, said its publisher, Liz Miller.
Some in the magazine business give the newcomer little chance. "I don't think the world needs another celebrity-oriented magazine," said Allan Mayer, former editor of Buzz.
But Ms. Cameron said, "I think Los Angeles is a city that can support more than one magazine."
 CHRISTIAN BERTHELSEN

HEADLINE: TECHNOLOGY: E-Commerce Report;
Many Internet companies have focused on attracting customers. The bigger challenge is fulfilling orders.

BYLINE: By Bob Tedeschi 

 BODY:
COMPANIES involved in E-commerce have achieved most-envied status in the late 1990's business world with their riches from initial public offerings, gaudy marketing budgets and news clippings that laud them as pioneers of an industrial transformation.
Given the glamour, then, it is not a little ironic that the most important issue facing the industry is the retail function with the least board room cachet: order fulfillment. 
 Picking, packing and shipping products was difficult enough in recent years for Internet retailers who had no experience in such matters. But with consumers' expectations rising and E-commerce companies preparing to ship a wider array of merchandise to customers this holiday season, distribution issues loom even larger than before.
"This is emerging as a huge issue," said John Hagel, who leads the E-commerce practice for McKinsey & Company, the consulting firm. "And it's a real bottleneck to growth. Too many companies started with the assumption that the challenge was getting people to buy things, when, in fact, the real challenge is getting it to them."
According to Forrester Research, an Internet consulting firm, Web retailers will ship roughly 230 million packages this year -- mostly during the holiday season. Large companies like PlanetRx and Etoys ship more than 1,000 packages daily, while the industry behemoth, Amazon.com, ships more than 10,000 a day. And last week, the research firm of Jupiter Communications predicted that the busiest sites, including Amazon, CDNow and Barnesandnoble.com, may each reach 58,000 transactions a day in November and December.
That process is rife with potential pitfalls and inefficiencies. In their infancy, most merchants simply took customers' orders, had them handled by a distributor and hoped that the items were in stock and shipped on time to the right people. In those days -- which, in some cases, extend to today -- customers could all but count on E-mail telling them their ordered items were out of stock or would be shipped late.
Competition is starting to halt such practices. To gain customer allegiance, Internet merchants are seeking more control over the shipping process. In many instances, most notably in the case of Amazon, they are building huge warehouses of their own near major shipping hubs. But because a high-technology distribution center can take two to three years to complete, Internet merchants are scrambling to find third parties to handle distribution for them. Two leaders in this category are Federated Department Store's Fingerhut Business Services Inc. and Hanover Direct's Keystone Fulfillment Inc.
Keystone handles fulfillment for Mercata, KBkids and Dress Barn, while Fingerhut does the same for Wal-Mart Stores, Levi Strauss and Etoys, among others. Fingerhut's 1.2-million-square-foot distribution center in Provo, Utah, will employ as many as 1,200 workers during the holiday peak, processing orders, shipping items and handling returns.
To the dismay of many E-commerce executives, such arrangements entail far more than simply writing a check and getting back to the business of planning the company's initial public offering. Because consumers are increasingly demanding so-called real-time inventory -- which includes access to information about product availability and shipping status -- distribution warehouses must be closely wired to the merchant's Web operations. Third-party distributors, however, typically have their own electronic tracking and ordering systems, which require painstaking efforts to link with the retailer.
"For every order, we have to pass information to their system," said Louis Zambello, senior vice president of Etoys, which recently began shipping products from Fingerhut's Utah warehouse.
"The warehouse then has to pass it back to us for confirmation," Mr. Zambello said. "When the item ships, that information goes to us for confirmation. When they get inventory in stock, that also has to go to our system, and if you do anything special for the customer, it has to flow from you to the outsourcer, back to you for confirmation."
Integrating the retailer's computer system with the distributor's, he said, "takes dozens of people on both sides working long hours."
Mr. Zambello added, "The misconception is that outsourcing is easier, but it's just different."
Though Etoys and Fingerhut would not disclose the costs associated with building warehouses or maintaining outsourcing relationships, Rakesh Kaul, president and chief executive of Hanover Direct, said the costs for both options could be steep.
Companies can expect to pay between $60 million and $80 million to build a one-million-square-foot warehouse, Mr. Kaul said, while they would pay a third-party distributor roughly 10 percent of each sale to fulfill their orders. In building distribution centers of their own, E-commerce companies "have to bear in mind fixed costs," he said.
"It's like owning a 747," Mr. Kaul said. "You have to be big, or fortunate enough to fill it, because if you don't fill it and operate all the time, you're guaranteed to lose money."
Mr. Hagel of McKinsey agreed. "Unless you do high volume, it doesn't make sense to build this capacity in the long run. There are significant economies of skill and scale in these businesses," he said, referring to third-party distributors.
Gaining assistance from fulfillment experts will be even more critical, Mr. Hagel said, as E-commerce companies evolve. Not only will Web retailers increasingly seek to sell products abroad -- an exercise that presents logistical nightmares -- but they will also be pushed by consumers to even greater levels of service, including customized products.
More immediately, though, Mr. Hagel said, Internet retailers are complicating matters by broadening their product selection in an effort to increase the amount spent by each customer. That means potentially shipping multiple items from multiple points, using different shippers.
"On one hand, I understand the importance of that as a way to derive more revenue from customers," Mr. Hagel said. "But on the other hand, if these companies think they've been challenged by fulfillment issues to date, it's nothing compared to what's coming."
If nothing else, the sudden ascendancy of fulfillment is vindicating a segment of the business world that has long suffered from a lack of respect.
"We used to be those guys in the dirty suits out there," said John Buck, president of Fingerhut Business Services. "Would you believe that fulfillment is the darling of the day? It's actually stunning to me."

HEADLINE: World Bank Chief to Be Renominated

BYLINE: By The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 26

 BODY:
President Clinton has decided to renominate James D. Wolfensohn as president of the World Bank, Administration officials said today -- a political victory for the former investment banker who openly split with the Administration over its early handling of the Asian financial crisis.
Treasury Secretary Lawrence H. Summers informed finance ministers of the other major industrial nations of the decision on Saturday, telling them that "Jim Wolfensohn has done an extraoridinary job of bringing energy and passion to the bank's crucial development mission." Mr. Wolfensohn is in the midst of restructuring the bank, whose mission is to combat poverty around the world. 
 In late 1997 and early 1998, Mr. Wolfensohn openly accused both the Treasury and the World Bank's sister institution, the International Monetary Fund, of devising national bailouts in Asia that paid too little attention to hardships that would result for the poorest residents of Indonesia, Thailand, South Korea and other nations.
The rescue packages, he contended, were too skewed toward aiding banks and international lenders. But in recent months he has smoothed over relations with the Administration, and officials say there was no real debate about his reappointment, which is likely to be announced formally on Tuesday.
Today, speaking at the annual meetings of the World Bank and the I.M.F., Mr. Wolfenson said that while Asia is recovering, "the economic situation is especially difficult in Latin America," and that "we will still be going backward rather than forward in the fight to reduce global poverty."

HEADLINE: General Motors and Union Negotiators Said to Be Close to 4-Year Agreement

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER 

DATELINE: DETROIT, Sept. 26

 BODY:
General Motors and the United Automobile Workers hope to reach a new, four-year labor contract within the next several days as the relationship between the world's largest company and its main union appears to have improved considerably since last year's 54-day strike in Flint, Mich., auto industry and union officials said today.
A G.M. official close to the talks said, "They're making progress; they met until very late last night." 
 Negotiators worked through the weekend to mold an agreement similar to the one reached by the union with DaimlerChrysler A.G. on Sept. 16. DaimlerChrysler workers voted on that agreement on Saturday. While the results are not scheduled for release until Monday, local union officials predicted that the contract would be easily ratified.
The union's previous, three-year agreements with G.M., DaimlerChrysler and the Ford Motor Company expired on Sept. 14, but the union had agreed to keep workers on the job while it negotiated with each company.
The U.A.W. agreement with DaimlerChrysler and a pact by the Canadian Auto Workers union with Ford, which was reached last Tuesday, both called for wage increases that exceeded the inflation rate by nearly 3 percent. The contract also limited how quickly DaimlerChrysler could shrink its work force through attrition, a provision that might cause some problems for G.M.
If G.M. accepted an identical provision, it could continue downsizing but with some limits on how quickly it did so. Another issue involved the extent to which workers at G.M.'s former auto parts division, which was spun off in May, would be able to return to jobs at G.M.
Labor relations between G.M. and the U.A.W. have improved since last year's strike for several reasons. G.M. appears to have suffered a small but permanent loss of market share because of the strike, making neither the company nor the union eager to repeat the experience.
The American auto market is booming, leaving every auto maker straining to keep up with production and auto workers reluctant to risk their profit-sharing checks on another walkout. And G.M. has revamped its approach to labor negotiations.
G.M. strongly criticized union leaders during last year's strike in an unsuccessful effort to convince employees that the work stoppage was a mistake. After the strike, G.M. brought Gary L. Cowger, a quiet-spoken American executive who had been running Opel, a German subsidiary, back to the United States and gave him unusually broad authority as group vice president for labor relations. His predecessor, Gerald A. Knechtel, had been only a vice president and had less authority.
Mr. Cowger, who likes to talk about his grandchildren and who spent the first 13 years of his career working his way up through supervisory positions at a G.M. assembly plant in Kansas City, Kan., has gotten along well with U.A.W. leaders, said one union official.
But a deal with the U.A.W. would not guarantee a strike-free autumn for G.M., which must still reach an agreement with the Canadian auto workers.
The Canadian union said it wanted G.M. and DaimlerChrysler to sign contracts similar to Ford's, which has already been ratified.
The union said it would next seek a labor contract with DaimlerChrysler, setting a strike deadline of Oct. 5.
Basil Hargrove, president of the Canadian union, said he was leaving G.M. for last partly because those negotiations posed the toughest issues, most involving job security.
"We have an incredible amount of problems at G.M.," Mr. Hargrove said.

HEADLINE: Russia Hotly Protests Audits Demanded by I.M.F. for Loans

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 26

 BODY:
Only hours after hearing Treasury Secretary Lawrence H. Summers describe the new conditions Russia must meet to receive continued international aid, the head of the country's central bank said his country was "being treated like whipping boys" by Washington and suggested that he would resist intensive audits of Russia's books.
The comments by Viktor Gerashchenko, a wily veteran of Russia's shifting political scene who also ran the Soviet Union's central bank, marked the first time that Russia has openly and harshly criticized the extensive new auditing demanded by Washington before lending new money to Moscow through the International Monetary Fund. 
 But American officials said Mr. Gerashchenko's outburst -- in which he suggested that the new restrictions on lending to Russia were chiefly a product of Washington politics -- was in sharp contrast to his behavior during the meeting with Mr. Summers. Several suggested that he may have been playing to a domestic audience in Russia, where the accusations of money laundering and the demands for new audits have smacked of more American interference in the running of Russia's chaotic economy.
His criticism of the United States also runs counter to the message that Russia's Foreign Minister, Igor S. Ivanov, has been delivering here. Mr. Ivanov was asked by Russia's President, Boris N. Yeltsin, to reassure American officials, news media and other groups that Moscow is strongly interested in cracking down on corruption and welcomes international scrutiny.
"It's fair to say," a senior Administration official said, "that they don't have their talking points in full agreement."
In contrast to Mr. Ivanov's promises of cooperation, Mr. Gerashchenko, speaking to reporters after meeting with Mr. Summers, said he was already providing the fund with as much information as it needs.
"We can improve the technology once," he said, "but why should we have to account for everything every day? Do we have to publish our accounts in the gutter press or at official councils?"
Among the American demands, which were incorporated in a financial communique issued by the finance ministers of the Group of 7 industrialized nations on Saturday night, Russia must agree to quarterly audits of its central bank reserves, the public money that is a key measure of the Government's fiscal health and a major factor in the value of the ruble.
The fund was surprised by the discovery last year that the central bank was linked to an offshore investment company in the Channel Island of Jersey and that the bank had misreported its asset levels to the fund. The offshore company, Fimaco, was acquired by a subsidiary of the central bank during Mr. Gerashchenko's previous tenure as central bank chief, although the Fimaco operations that drew most attention to date fell during the 1994-98 period when he did not head the central bank.
Mr. Summers continued his public warnings to Russia today, repeating the line he took at the opening of a Congressional inquiry last week into corruption, money laundering and the still-unsubstantiated charges that I.M.F. funds had been illegally diverted.
"We are concerned about Russia," Mr. Summers said. "The capacity of the I.M.F. and the World Bank to help bring about effective policies depends on the will and capacity of Russian authorities and the Russian people to carry forward reforms in their national interests." He specifically said it was "critical" that Russia develop "adequate safeguards to ensure that funds provided by" the bank and the fund "are used for their intended purpose."
As the two men sparred from a distance, Russian officials on the sidelines of the annual meetings here of the World Bank and the I.M.F. said they did not know when they would receive an additional dose of aid that had been scheduled for release this month. It is essentially money that will be used to pay back the fund for interest due on past loans -- in this case, about $370 million -- and as an additional safeguard the money will never actually enter Russia. Instead, the I.M.F. will transfer it from one fund to another; it is akin to getting a loan from a bank to pay one's mortgage from that bank for the month.
