HEADLINE: Retailers Scramble To Find Holiday Help;
Surging Economy Contributes To Shortage of Seasonal Workers

BYLINE: By LESLIE EATON 

 BODY:
In the elegant Trustees Room on the sixth floor of the Museum of Modern Art, 10 blond wood tables and 20 chrome-and-leather armchairs were carefully arranged to allow for intimate conversation. The rich smell of freshly brewed coffee wafted in from the antechamber, where bottles of Italian mineral water were lined up next to wineglasses and a golden heap of cookies.
This setting, so suitable for wooing art world bigwigs and financial backers, was created on Tuesday evening to entice a group that is almost as important to the museum's finances and a great deal harder to attract these days: holiday store clerks. 
The museum was having a job fair, trying to find a dozen or so temporary salespeople for its bookstore and for its design store across 53d Street, which is to reopen Oct. 4 after renovations. "It's more of a challenge to find these people," said James D. Gundell, president of the museum's retail operation. "They have a lot of opportunities."
Seasonal salespeople, the backbone of New York City's Christmas shop-a-thon, have been in increasingly short supply in recent years. And retailers fear that 1999 will be the toughest recruiting season ever.
"The paucity of qualified workers is greater now than it has been in a long time," said Kurt Barnard, publisher of Barnard's Retail Trend Report. "People are looking for better jobs, permanent jobs." And they are finding them.
The city's booming economy, its declining unemployment rate, and the surge in store openings in Manhattan have combined to create high anxiety among retailers of all sorts, from high-priced boutiques to drugstore chains to cultural institutions. Even the Salvation Army is worrying about recruiting enough bell ringers for its Christmas kettles.
While the labor shortage causes headaches for retailers -- and may induce irritation in shoppers -- it seems to guarantee happy holidays for students, part-time workers and other New Yorkers who, for one reason or another, are looking for temporary work.
"I'm more optimistic than I've ever been before," said Seth Chodosh, 45, who just moved back to Brooklyn from San Francisco. Mr. Chodosh, who has worked in publishing and retailing, said he was looking for a holiday job while settling in, and would later seek a permanent job.
Store managers are stepping up their hiring efforts, starting ever earlier in the year. (Tiffany began running holiday help-wanted ads in August.) They are guaranteeing every applicant an interview, holding out the hope of permanent employment after Christmas, and hiring people with no retailing experience.
Employee discounts are becoming increasingly generous; 30 percent is now common. Museum stores promise free passes. And Century 21, the discount department store near the World Trade Center, for the first time is offering a $300 gift certificate for new employees who stay through December.
What does not seem to be rising much is pay. Job seekers report that $8 an hour is the going rate, though some workers without experience make the minimum wage, $5.15.
Some stores prefer to stress how much salespeople can make on commission: Bergdorf Goodman, the elegant emporium where a handbag can cost as much as a compact car, estimates that its seasonal employees can make as much as $25 an hour if they are successful sellers.
"It's getting tougher to find people, the economy being so good," said Bill Brobston, general manager and senior vice president of Bergdorf Goodman, which is looking for roughly 100 seasonal employees. "Spend the Holiday Season in Style," suggest the store's help-wanted ads, which promise that the store offers "a more reasonable schedule than our competitors."
Smaller retailers, too, have trouble finding enough help.
Erwin Pearl, the jewelry company with a boutique on Fifth Avenue, is looking for three or four people to start work next month and stay through January. Angela Hinton, the assistant vice president of human resources who has been hiring seasonal help for seven years, said that she gets only a dozen or so resumes now, down from as many as 30, and that the applicants tend to be less educated than before.
"There's a lot of competition now, in terms of salary, benefits and little perks," she said.
Even at its easiest, seasonal hiring is a big job. Since the recession ended in 1992, retailers in New York City have tended to add about 30,000 people to their payrolls between August and December, according to data compiled by the New York State Department of Labor.
That is because many stores make more than 20 percent of their sales in the holiday period, according to the National Retail Federation. For jewelry stores, the percentage is much higher, but even drugstores' sales pick up late in the year.
And 1999 promises to be a good year. Consumers have been spending money hand over fist, confounding economists and raising concerns at the Federal Reserve, which is on the lookout for signs that the economy is overheating.
The Fed is also keeping a close eye on labor shortages around the country; its economic survey released on Wednesday noted ominously that "retailers in the Boston, Kansas City and New York districts reported that they cannot find enough staff to fill vacancies."
That is certainly true at Duane Reade, the ubiquitous drugstore chain, which has about 100 empty job slots right now. Available people with "retail experience are nonexistent, so we're adapting and going outside of the industry," said Anthony J. Cuti, the company's chairman.
Part of Duane Reade's problem is that it has been expanding rapidly, as have many other retailers. The Gap is opening so many Gap, Banana Republic and Old Navy stores that its appetite for store workers appears to be infinite.
The American Museum of Natural History is looking for store cashiers -- along with security guards, custodians, telephone operators and other workers -- for its new planetarium, Rose Center for Earth and Space, which is to open in February. With 250 slots to fill by December, in time for training, the museum has been holding its first job fairs ever, said Barbara Dwyer Gunn, senior vice president for operations and government relations.
Indeed, employers in general plan to hire people in the fourth quarter of the year, according to a survey by Manpower, a staffing company. It found that 34 percent of businesses in the New York City region -- and 50 percent in Manhattan -- planned to add staff members in the next three months, both of which are big increases from last year.
All of this offers a lot of opportunity to people who might otherwise work seasonal jobs but want permanent ones.
Lynette Richardson, 40, of Brooklyn, for example, works as a cashier at Giants Stadium during the football season but said she was looking for "something on a consistent basis." She has been filling out a lot of job applications lately, she said, adding that this seems to be a good time to be looking for a year-round job.
New York is unusual in that so many of its retailing jobs are full time; in many parts of the country, store work is almost always part time. But by New York standards, the pay is low. The average retail salesman or saleswoman in the city makes less than $21,000 a year, according to Federal statistics, less than half the citywide average of roughly $47,000 for all occupations.
It might seem odd, with all the money flowing into stores, that retailers do not offer higher pay to attract workers, but industry experts say the fattening of paychecks is rare. That is because retailing companies are under pressure from their shareholders to keep costs down and profits up.
New Yorkers who are looking for holiday employment say they are well aware that the pay is low and the work is hard. But they have their reasons, as several people at the Museum of Modern Art job fair explained.
Some, like Veronica Ledford, 23, are just looking for a little extra cash for Christmas gifts. "I want to guarantee that when I go home, I'm not empty-handed," said Ms. Ledford, a freelance graphic designer from Lynchburg, Va., who keeps body and soul together by working at a bank.
Others, like Cheryl Traylor, 42, are returning to the job market after an absence. Ms. Traylor, who lives in the Bronx, used to work at J. C. Penney before the company left the city; she kept her hand in by working as a freelance artist while raising two children, now 8 and 15.
Members of the museum staff interviewed 51 people, and the references of 29 are being checked, said Joe Meny, general manager of the retail operation. He is pushing his staff to move quickly to check references and make offers; last year, after its first job fair, the museum offered jobs to 20 people, but only 6 or 7 took the offers.
Mr. Meny said he would like to hire at least that many this year, "but I'd be thrilled to get more than that."

HEADLINE: DROP IN RECRUITS PUSHES PENTAGON TO NEW STRATEGY

BYLINE: By STEVEN LEE MYERS 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 26

 BODY:
With more and more young people choosing their challenges in college and their rewards in civilian jobs, a serious shortage of military recruits is forcing the Pentagon to revamp its strategy for filling its ranks.
And some of the Pentagon's own consultants say the armed forces have not yet faced up to the severity of the recruiting gap and need to do still more. 
 When the fiscal year ends on Thursday, the Army will fall short of its recruiting goal for the second year in a row, signing up nearly 7,000 fewer enlistees than the 74,500 it needs to maintain its force at current levels. It is the worst shortfall since 1979, when the Army was recruiting twice as many recruits as it does now.
The Air Force, too, is expected to miss its target of 33,800 by 1,500 to 1,800 people, despite having paid for commercial television advertising this past year for the first time.
The Navy, which fell 12,000 recruits short last year, may just squeak by, but only after lowering its goal from last year and accepting thousands more recruits who never made it through high school, but earned only general equivalency diplomas.
Only the Marine Corps, the smallest force and the one with arguably the strongest traditional appeal to prospective recruits, has kept pace.
The recruiting shortfall has prompted growing concern at the Pentagon, where officials are scrambling to reverse the trend. They stop short, though, of calling it a crisis.
The Army, like all the services, has increased its advertising and raised its signing bonuses: recruits can get $6,000 just for signing up before Thursday, on top of other bonuses. It has also increased the money it offers recruits for college tuition, now as much as $50,000, up from $40,000.
And in a striking break with tradition, the Army has sent hundreds of fresh young soldiers on recruiting assignments back to their hometowns in the hope they can persuade others like themselves to join.
Pvt. Dennis L. Neal, who recently undertook such a mission in a tough neighborhood in the nation's capital, where he used to live, has barely been in the Army long enough to know if he likes it. His entire military experience so far has been a couple of months of basic training and truck-driving school. Now the Army has turned to him to do what veteran sergeants are hard pressed to accomplish these days: find new recruits.
"What I tell them," Private Neal, who is 21, said the other day, as he stood with colleagues from one of Washington's two recruiting stations at a street music festival only a few blocks from where he grew up, "is that it ain't so bad."
Raising an all-volunteer military has never been easy, but the difficulties with recruiting have been mounting in recent years as the economy courses along and college becomes an attainable goal for more young people than ever. Even as Congress and the Pentagon pour millions of dollars more into advertising and marketing, many officials fear that the problem may only get worse.
In a report last week, a Federal advisory commission studying national security in the 21st century included recruiting shortfalls as one of the threats the American military is expected to face. The commission, headed by former Senators Gary Hart and Warren B. Rudman, cited surveys showing a sharp drop in young men, especially young black men, who even consider joining.
"It is not clear how the military establishment, then, will sustain the volunteer force over the next generation and, particularly, how it will manage to recruit and retain enough highly skilled personnel to meet the increasing technical needs of an advanced military," the commission said.
Next month the Army plans to inaugurate a pilot program to pay 6,000 high school dropouts to study for G.E.D.'s so they can qualify to join the active force or the reserves. Another program will let prospective recruits sign up, but put off basic training for up to two years to finish college, to tap the growing number of young men and women who go straight to college after high school.
"There are so many unexploited opportunities that we haven't taken advantage of to improve recruiting," the Secretary of the Army, Louis Caldera, said in an interview.
And yet even Mr. Caldera warned that the Army needed to reverse the waning interest in military service among the first generation of young people whose fathers came of age after the draft ended in 1973.
"If unabated," he said, "it might not make a difference what kinds of resources you're plowing into recruiting."
Officials at the Pentagon attribute poor recruiting to a variety of forces that have collided at once.
The pool of young men and women between the ages of 18 and 22 -- the optimum age for recruits -- has shrunk to roughly 21 million, 5 million fewer than in 1980. Making matters more difficult for the military, the nation's economic boom has created the lowest unemployment rate in years, creating abundant job opportunities for those who might otherwise be interested in enlisting
"We are in a very tough environment right now," Gen. Henry H. Shelton, the Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff, told the Senate's Armed Services Committee this month.
But there is evidence that the problems may lie with the Pentagon as much as with economic and demographic trends.
Over the summer, Secretary of Defense William S. Cohen hired two prominent Washington political consultants -- Carter D. Eskew, a Democrat, and Michael R. Murphy, a Republican -- to review the Pentagon's recruiting strategy and the $300 million its spends on advertisements. Their preliminary findings, presented to Mr. Cohen and other senior officials last month, were sharply critical of the services' recruiting effort.
The Army, Navy and Air Force have failed to articulate a clear image and purpose to prospective recruits, they told the officials. Only the Marine Corps has a distinct image to sell, they said.
"The other services need to find themselves, post cold war," Rudy de Leon, the Under Secretary of Defense for Personnel and Readiness, said.
The consultants also recommended that the services conduct more thorough research into the attitudes and habits of the young, and rely less on traditional approaches to television advertising like buying time during basketball or football games that reach only a certain segment of young people.
They also said the Pentagon increasingly needed to focus its pitch on "influencers" -- parents, teachers and coaches, many of whom appear to be discouraging young people from considering the military because either they never served or they have lingering hostility from the Vietnam era.
The Army has already taken the consultants' critiques to heart. One of the first casualties may be the jingle that the Army has used for the better part of two decades -- "Be All That You Can Be" -- even though Advertising Age magazine recently ranked it as one of the most memorable of the century.
"There's a lot of value in that, because it's very recognizable," Mr. Caldera said. "But the question we have to ask is, Is it the right message for the young people you are trying to attract?"
Others have said more drastic measures are needed.
Charles C. Moskos, a sociologist at Northwestern University who served on the national security advisory panel, has been calling on the services to offer shorter enlistments, of 15 or 18 months, something the Pentagon says wastes money spent on training. Mr. Moskos, though, argues that shorter stints could be tailored to college students interested in serving on missions like those in Bosnia or Kosovo that are straining the Army's resources.
"Call it junior year abroad," he said.
But Representative Steve Buyer, the Indiana Republican who heads the House's subcommittee on military personnel, said the problem was the missions themselves, a complaint echoed by the leading Republican Presidential candidate, Gov. George W. Bush of Texas, in a speech last week.
"We're fooling ourselves if we believe we can solve the problem with more G.E.D. programs or more money for ads," Mr. Buyer said. "What we need is a change in foreign policy."
In the meantime, the burden has fallen on the Army's recruiters, who put in long days trolling for recruits in an ever-dwindling pool of prospects. Each month the recruiting station in Washington on Florida Avenue is supposed to enlist 12 or 13 recruits. On average, the station's 10 recruiters have been getting 6 to 8.
First Sgt. Beth Ann Lockett, a 12-year veteran, said the hardest part was finding people who even qualify, let alone show interest. She has to turn away single mothers, people who score too low on the military's aptitude test and those who have used drugs recently -- not uncommon traits among young people, especially in poorer neighborhoods. Those convicted of serious crimes are also disqualified, though she hastened to add, "We can work with legal problems."
Most of the recruiters' time is spent on the phone chasing down leads, or setting up shop in places where young people gather, as Sergeant Lockett and her crew of soldiers, including Private Neal, did the other evening at a radio station's street festival in Potomac Gardens, a gritty housing project in Southeast Washington.
As hip-hop music blared, though, the recruiters had little to do. Almost no one over the age of 10 paid much attention to the brochures neatly stacked on two rickety folding tables. Finally, after more than an hour, the first prospect appeared: Damall Kellem, a 17-year-old senior in the Class of 2000.
Mr. Kellem took a brochure but said that most young people did not give military service much consideration.
"It's not even in their vocabularies," he said with a shrug and walked off.

HEADLINE: A Sparkling New Penn Station Gets a Push With $160 Million in U.S. Loans

BYLINE: By THOMAS J. LUECK 

 BODY:
Providing a large piece of financial support for a new Pennsylvania Station, Federal officials are to announce today that the project has been selected to receive $160 million in loans through a new Congressional program for transportation improvements.
The loans, to be announced at the World Trade Center by senior officials of the Federal Department of Transportation, could provide a critical step forward for the project, which would convert the James A. Farley Building, the Beaux-Arts main post office on Eighth Avenue, into the new station. The project has been widely endorsed by preservationists and city officials as a means of reclaiming some of the grandeur of the old Pennsylvania Station, which was demolished 36 years ago. Advocates say it would also give the city a more advanced terminal for Amtrak train service. 
 While the financial assistance through the Congressional program, authorized under the Transportation Infrastructure Finance and Innovation Act of 1997, would provide only part of the estimated $750 million cost of the project, state and Federal officials said the loans would provide a needed financial and political lift for the plan.
President Clinton, in a statement prepared for today's presentation ceremony, described the Congressional act that provided for the loans as "a catalyst for creating jobs and continuing our nation's economic growth." The President visited the Farley Building in May to announce his support for the rail terminal project.
Besides senior representatives of the Transportation Department, officials expected to attend today's announcement include Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan, who has been the project's most outspoken advocate for years, and Senator Bob Graham of Florida, who was a co-sponsor of the transportation finance act.
Charles A. Gargano, the senior economic adviser to Gov. George E. Pataki and the chairman of Pennsylvania Station Redevelopment Corporation, said in an interview that the Federal loans would "fill a huge gap" in paying for the project.
The Federal financing, to be extended at interest rates below those normally paid to banks or holders of municipal bonds, includes a $140 million loan and a $20 million line of credit. Mr. Gargano, whose redevelopment corporation was formed in 1996 to oversee the project with commitments of $75 million from the state and $25 million from the city, said the Federal loan package would make it easier to raise more money through the sale of municipal bonds.
Even though all the necessary financing is not in place, Mr. Gargano said the renovation of the Farley Building would begin next year and be completed in 2003.
Under the plan, the United States Postal Service has agreed to vacate about one-third of its space in the 1.4 million-square-foot building, which was designed as a sister to Pennsylvania Station and opened in 1913. The original Pennsylvania Station, directly across Eighth Avenue on the site of what is now Madison Square Garden, was completed in 1911 and demolished in 1963, provoking such impassioned protest that it remains a rallying point for preservationists.
President Clinton's visit to the Farley Building corresponded with the release of architectural drawings by the firm Skidmore, Owings and Merrill, of a plan to renovate the interior of the building as a railroad terminal concourse enclosed in steel and glass, with access to Amtrak platforms along tracks that already run beneath the building.
The final price for the project remains to be determined. Mr. Gargano and other officials acknowledged in recent months that the budget was being inflated by a construction boom in New York City, which has pushed up the cost of labor and materials. With many of the bills for architectural designs and other preliminary work already paid, Mr. Gargano said in May that the remaining budget was $484 million, but he revised that figure on Friday to $550 million.
Proponents of the project are seeking other contributions, including a $60 million Federal grant that President Clinton has said he would ask Congress to authorize before the end of the year.
In announcing the project's financing under the 1997 act, Federal officials said they would also use today's ceremony in Manhattan to designate four other transportation projects across the nation that are to receive loans.
Those projects are a nine-mile toll road to be built between San Diego and the Mexican border, which is to receive $127 million in loans; road improvements and a "people mover" system at Miami International Airport, which is to receive $436 million in loans; a rapid transit system under construction in San Juan, P.R., which is to receive $300 million in loans, and the Metrorail system in Washington, which is to receive a $600 million loan guarantee for improvements.

HEADLINE: Ecuador to Default on Debt Backed by U.S.

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: QUITO, Ecuador, Sept. 26

 BODY:
Ecuador will default on payment of millions of dollars of debt backed by the United States, President Jamil Mahuad announced tonight.
Last month, Ecuador chose to delay a $98 million interest payment on its Brady bond debt for 30 days. It had until Tuesday to make the payment or become the first country ever to default on a Brady bond debt. 
 Brady bonds, named after former United States Treasury Secretary Nicholas Brady, were created to resolve the Latin American debt crisis of the 1980's. They are partly backed by Treasury securities.
In a televised address, Mr. Mahuad said his country would only pay interest on the bonds that are not guaranteed by the United States Treasury. He said Ecuador would pay "a little less than half" of the $98 million interest payment.
International lenders and creditors have been fixated on Ecuador's economy. Although the country does not rank among the region's largest economies, some fear a default on the once-sacrosanct Brady bonds could spark a tumble in emerging bond markets.
Ecuador had been working on proposals to restructure its entire Brady bond debt of $6 billion, and was preparing to present strategies for foreign debt restructuring this week during the annual International Monetary Fund meeting in Washington.
In another economic development, Colombia announced that its peso would be allowed to float freely against the dollar, Reuters reported.
The move, another blow to an economy already reeling, came at the end of a week in which the bank sold more than $400 million in precious foreign reserves in a vain bid to defend the embattled peso from speculative attack.

HEADLINE: JAPAN GIVES HINT THAT IT WILL ACT TO HALT YEN'S RISE

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 26

 BODY:
The head of the Bank of Japan strongly hinted today that he was prepared to reverse course and act to halt a three-month-long rise in the value of the Japanese yen before it chokes off Japan's fragile economic recovery and provokes further clashes with the United States.
In early trading in Australia and Asia, the vaguely worded announcement from Masaru Hayami, governor of the Bank of Japan, and a communique issued on Saturday night by the finance ministers of the Group of 7 largest industrialized nations proved enough to strengthen the dollar slightly and weaken the yen. At midmorning on Monday in Tokyo, the dollar was trading at 104.98-105.00 yen, up from 104.09 at the Friday close in New York. 
 Although he denied he was changing course, Mr. Hayami's statement appeared to mark a full retreat from the position the bank took less than a week ago, when it defied Japanese politicians and Washington by refusing to stop the rise in the yen's value.
The behind-the-scenes struggle over Japan's monetary policy dominated this weekend's opening of the annual meetings of the World Bank and the International Monetary Fund, which are also focusing on corruption in Russia. Articles, page A8. A new arrangement was also agreed to that would revalue 14 million ounces of the I.M.F.'s gold reserves to pay off some of the debts of the world's poorest nations.
Mr. Hayami's comments today were important in part because Wall Street reacted badly to moves last week by the Bank of Japan. Investors feared that more expensive Japanese goods could help set off inflation in the United States, as consumers pay more for Toyotas and Sony electronics, among other goods. That, in turn, could convince the Federal Reserve to raise interest rates, slowing the economy.
As a result, the future of the American stock markets seemed increasingly dependent on the decisions of Japan's central bank, an institution that operates by its own rules and market rationale.
Today, as Mr. Hayami seemed by turns to want to explain the bank's new thinking and to insist nothing was changing, he issued a statement saying that "the bank is prepared to use the flexibility it has in the context of the present monetary policy stance to respond appropriately and timely to developments in the economy as well as financial markets, including the foreign exchange market."
The Group of 7 ministers, in a statement last night, put the onus on Japan to solve the problems stemming from the surprising strength of the yen. Only if Japan acts to loosen further what the World Bank already considers a dangerously loose monetary policy -- interest rates are at zero in Japan -- would the other countries consider stepping in and intervening in the currency markets to weaken the yen, the statement suggested. And today, as traders went to work in Australia and Tokyo, they were watching every delphic utterance of Mr. Hayami.
Less closely followed was a decision announced late today in which the fund said that 14 million ounces from its gold reserves would be used to relieve debt, but not -- as originally planned -- by selling them on the open market. That suggestion had drawn strong objections from lawmakers in gold-producing parts of the United States and from countries like South Africa.
Instead, the World War II-era gold is now being revalued at current prices, and then, through bookkeeping sleight-of-hand, used by the poor countries to repay their loans.
A week ago Mr. Hayami said he would not adjust the country's monetary policy simply to control foreign exchange rates. That, he said, is how Japan got into trouble in the early 1990's.
Ever since those statements, the yen had continued to strengthen, making Japanese exports like cars and electronics more expensive, and thus less competitive, on world markets. At a news conference here this afternoon, Mr. Hayami, speaking in Japanese, said that he had been "misunderstood" by the markets and by major governments around the world.
"Our policy of appropriate and timely response to developments in the economy and financial markets was not understood by the Group of 7," he said. "Now they understand."
Other officials at the meetings say that it is Mr. Hayami who now understands that the yen's rise could choke off the country's recovery and thus hurt both Tokyo and Washington.
The United States has been the world's only real engine of economic growth in recent years, paying for this with a huge trade deficit as it sucks in the rest of what the world manufactures. Unless Japan recovers, the thinking goes, that deficit will not be reduced by much.
A weaker dollar and a stronger yen would ordinarily be good news for American exporters. But in this case, there are doubts that Japanese consumers will stop saving and start buying again unless they fear the onset of modest inflation -- exactly what pumping more money into the economy could create.
So the finance ministers meeting here on Saturday hammered out language to try to sow doubt in the minds of currency traders over whether the yen would be allowed to appreciate further. "We share Japan's concern about the potential impact of the yen's appreciation for the Japanese economy and the world economy," the statement said.
Mr. Hayami, who said last week that controlling foreign exchange rates was "not an aim of Bank of Japan monetary policy," began to suggest last night that he was revising his view.

HEADLINE: With a Step Back, a Telescope Propels Japan Ahead

BYLINE: By HOWARD W. FRENCH 

DATELINE: MAUNA KEA, Hawaii

 BODY:
On the final ascent up this volcanic mountain, the gleaming white housing of Japan's new national telescope, which looks vaguely futuristic with its cylindrical shape, immediately sets it apart from the more traditional domes that sprout nearby in the cold, thin air.
But it is only upon closer inspection of the device, named the Subaru, that one begins to see that the design of the telescope responsible for Japan's great leap forward in deep-space observation commences with what would seem to be a decided step backward: the use of a single huge piece of glass as its primary mirror. 
 Since the completion of the revolutionary 200-inch telescope on Palomar Mountain in 1948, telescope designers have been confronted with a seemingly intractable problem preventing the design of ever larger light-gathering mirrors: the gradual distorting effects of gravity on massive pieces of suspended glass.
Telescope designers in recent years have resorted to a variety of tricks to overcome this problem. For example, the primary mirrors in Subaru's closest neighbors on the mountain, the twin 400-inch Keck telescopes, the two largest in the world, are each made up of many joined segments with a common focus. Several other new telescopes have featured very thin "floppy" mirrors, combined with computer-controlled supports that can constantly adjust the shape of the mirror to compensate for gravity's sag and even atmospheric blur.
Another strategy, being used by the European Southern Observatory for its array of four 320-inch telescopes at La Serena in Chile, is to combine the light from separate telescopes to obtain the resolving power of a vastly larger lens. The Kecks are designed to be so linked eventually.
Japan has long suffered the image of being a nation of scientific followers. In astronomy, for example, the country had long been limited to the use of an 80-inch device built decades ago. But when it came to designing a new telescope, the country's small astronomy establishment shed the follower image and wagered boldly that the magic of the single-mirror approach -- something of a classical ideal among telescope builders -- was far from played out. The result was the Subaru at 8.3 meters wide, about 323 inches. (The name does not indicate a connection to the car company; instead, it is the Japanese name for the constellation Pleiades.)
In pulling off its creation, Japan has applied the nation's exquisite skills in engineering, design, construction and miniaturization in ways that experts say will strongly influence the future course of building very large telescopes.
Among the features that will be looked at most carefully are its flexible, relatively thin, seven-inch-thick mirror and an enclosure that is of a piece with the telescope itself and rotates to change views of the sky. Scientists say that the enclosure design, once criticized for its cost, has already proven its superiority in the critical areas of temperature and wind-flow control.
Finally, there is the system of servo-controlled active optics, which uses 264 motors to adjust the mirror, to correct for minute distortions.
All of this cost Japan $400 million at current exchange rates, or several times the price of the first Keck telescope. But astronomers say that Japanese telescope builders, unlike those in the United States and Europe, had to build virtually everything from scratch, something that must be taken into consideration in evaluating the price.
"Within a couple of years, a conventional wisdom will form as to which approach to big telescope construction is most advantageous and most economical," said Dr. Robert McLaren, interim director of the University of Hawaii's Institute for Astronomy. "Right now, most people expect that the single-lens approach will prove superior."
Even though it is not yet equipped with the devices that astronomers will need to take full advantage of its powers, the Subaru telescope has already exceeded many of its designers' expectations. It has, for example, already broken the resolution record for ground-based telescopes, with the ability to see angular details as small as 0.196 arc seconds, about five times better than conventional telescopes.
When a new adaptive optics system is installed, allowing for correction of atmospheric distortions, the resolution is expected to increase to 0.06 arc seconds. Such sharpness, scientists say, is the equivalent of being able to see a baseball clearly from 180 miles away.
The next generation of space-based telescopes are expected to take decisive advantage of the dark vacuum of space to offer resolution superior to any Earth-bound telescope. For now, though, Subaru's astronomers readily offer side-by-side comparisons of their device's images with those taken by the Hubble Space Telescope, and believe many of their images are clearly better.
The astronomers who will operate Subaru say the telescope's ultra-high resolution, its superb light-gathering capacity and its so-called prime-focus view, which permits the capturing of images of wider sections of the sky than most other telescopes, will make the device a leader in astronomical research. Two particular areas of expected strength are deep-field research and the quest to find other solar systems.
Deep-field research involves collecting information on the darkest, most remote regions of space in an effort to understand the evolution of the universe and predict its future. The search for new planets outside of our solar system, meanwhile, requires the spotting of extremely faint objects that on the scale of the universe are not only distant, but like our own solar system's planets, are relatively tiny.
In an interview during testing of the telescope last March, Subaru's director, Dr. Norio Kaifu, who previously directed research at Japan's large radio telescope at Nobeyama, fairly brimmed with confidence about these capabilities.
"There have been many reports of the discovery of planets outside of our solar system, but no one has yet produced clear images," said Dr. Kaifu. "Clearly this represents demanding work, but we want to show these planets."
Asked what Japanese name might be given to the first planet discovered by Subaru, Dr. Kaifu laughed. "We don't plan to discover only one," he said. "We will find many."
And in making the search, many are betting that the Subaru team will reinvigorate Japan's interest in astronomy, and indeed, its investment in science generally.
"'If you look at the history of Japan, you realize that we imported science from the Western world only about 100 years ago, so it is quite natural that we are behind in many ways," said Dr. Kaifu. "With Subaru we have reached a very high peak, but there is still not much of a base beneath our feet.
"Perhaps our biggest impact will be in encouraging the future scientists of Japan."

HEADLINE: BOOKS OF THE TIMES;
What Has Happened to Men? An Author Tries to Answer

BYLINE: By MICHIKO KAKUTANI 

 BODY:

STIFFED
The Betrayal of the American Man
By Susan Faludi
Illustrated. 662 pages. William Morrow & Company. $27.50.
In her 1991 best seller, "Backlash: The Undeclared War Against American Women," Susan Faludi argued that "an often ludicrous overreaction to women's modest progress has prevailed" among men. She suggested that many men defined their masculinity in terms of being a "good provider" for their families, and that "if establishing masculinity depends most of all on succeeding as the prime breadwinner, then it is hard to imagine a force more directly threatening to fragile American manhood than the feminist drive for economic equality." During the 1980's, she asserted, "free-floating anxiety over declining wages, insecure employment and overpriced housing" needed a focal point, and "women, especially feminist women, became the all-purpose scapegoats." 
 This didactic and highly simplistic analysis of what Ms. Faludi calls "the American masculinity crisis" has given way in "Stiffed: The Betrayal of the American Man" to a far more nuanced and sympathetic assessment of what has happened to men in the tumultuous decades since World War II. It is Ms. Faludi's contention, laid out in this thoughtful if bloated new book, that many American men feel they have "lost their compass in the world," that compared with their fathers' generation, they feel "less triumphal, less powerful, less confident of making a living."
 Although Ms. Faludi writes that she started this book wondering why men were "so disturbed by the prospect of women's independence," she began to see, as her reporting progressed, that the "gender battle was only a surface manifestation of other struggles," that the sources of men's current disillusionment were deeper and more obscure.
She says she put her "prefigured map" aside and gradually reached the conclusion that men's current predicament stems less from economic uncertainties or advancements made by women than from more fundamental changes in the American culture at large, in particular the shift from a traditional society that valued loyalty, team play and the mastery of a vocation to an "ornamental culture" driven by celebrity and image, "a society drained of context, saturated with a competitive individualism that has been robbed of craft or utility, and ruled by commercial values that revolve around who has the most, the best, the biggest, the fastest."
For Ms. Faludi, this media-driven, ornamental culture -- symbolized by Calvin Klein underwear ads and Details magazine -- subjected men to "the conformism, passivity and consumerist mirror-gazing traditionally held to be feminine," redefining masculinity as "something to drape over the body, not draw from inner resources" and manhood as something to be "displayed, not demonstrated."
Like "Backlash," this book is constructed around a series of interviews, but this time Ms. Faludi's profiles are not used to buttress a pre-established thesis but to explore her subject matter and provide the reader with some sharply observed case studies. Some of the men featured in this book are well known, including Sylvester Stallone and the playwright David Rabe; most of them are not. There are interviews with members of Promise Keepers and members of a support group called Alternatives to Violence. There are profiles of three brothers who grew up amid the gang violence of South Central Los Angeles; a Cleveland Browns fan known as Big Dawg, and a half-dozen porn stars.
Several of the chapters are constructed around pairs of contrasting groups of men. Laid-off workers from the Long Beach naval shipyard in California (which Ms. Faludi portrays as an old-fashioned community that encouraged mentorship and collegiality) and laid-off workers from the McDonnell Douglas plant in Long Beach (depicted as a modern corporate bureaucracy filled with middle managers). Teen-age members of the Spur Posse, the group that became infamous in 1993 for multiple alleged incidents of sexual assault, and male students at the Citadel, the military school forced to admit its first female student, Shannon Faulkner, in 1994. A minister who was disowned by his father for opposing the Vietnam War, and a Vietnam veteran who had long, agonizing arguments with his father over his determination to speak out about the My Lai massacre.
In fact, the estrangement between sons and fathers is a recurrent theme in "Stiffed." As Ms. Faludi sees it, the World War II generation bequeathed to its baby boomer sons its own definitions and requisites for manhood: the promise of a frontier to conquer, the promise of "a clear and evil enemy to be crushed," the promise "of an institution of brotherhood in which anonymous members could share a greater institutional" glory and the "promise of a family to provide for and protect."
Unfortunately for the sons, Ms. Faludi goes on, the rules of the game had changed by the time they came of age. Space, which was supposed to be the new frontier, proved "a place not much worth conquering." Vietnam provided no clear-cut mission, no moral payoff. And the institutions that were supposed to provide "masculine honor and pride" in exchange for loyalty all too often turned out to be corporations willing to sacrifice their employees to downsizing.
At the same time, the dynamics of the nuclear family had altered in response to the growing independence of women and economic pressures for two-income households. As a result, Ms. Faludi observes, "all the elements of the old formula for attaining manhood had vanished in short order. The boy who had been told he was going to be the master of the universe and all that was in it found himself master of nothing."
In laying out this narrative Ms. Faludi has a tendency to repeat herself -- to make the same point over and over again -- and she has a tendency, especially in the second half of the book, to allow her interview subjects to natter on and on and on. But while this volume would have benefited enormously from a strict editing, it nonetheless remains a fair-minded and energetically reported book. It is a book that eschews the reductive assumptions purveyed by many feminists to put the predicament of the American man (and by implication, the American woman) into a broader cultural context, while showing just how drastically this country has changed during the second half of the 20th century.

HEADLINE: Earthquakes: A Matter of Luck, Most of It Bad

BYLINE: By WILLIAM J. BROAD 

 BODY:
The doom mongers are wrong, scientists say. The killer earthquakes that have shaken the globe in the last month and taken thousands of lives in Turkey, Greece and now Taiwan are no sign that the end is nigh. By all estimates, the number of large jolts around the globe is below average this year, and it is simply bad luck that the recent upheavals have ripped through populated areas, leveling thousands of buildings and leaving tens of thousands of people dead, missing and homeless.
But more bad luck is coming, experts agree. Even though the rate of earthquakes over time seems to be more or less unchanging, the world's population explosion means that more people are moving into quake zones, which are often near coasts. The result, experts say, is the prospect of continuing trauma as urban areas erupt with what seems like nearly endless bursts of destruction. 
 A million people have died in earthquakes this century, experts say. The next century might see 10 times as many deaths, with a million lost in a single blow, they add, unless major steps are taken to fortify dozens of sprawling cities expected to teem with billions of added residents.
"It is inevitable," said Klaus H. Jacob, an earthquake expert at Lamont-Doherty, the earth sciences research center of Columbia University. "More and more people, and more and more buildings, are at stake. As the world gets more populous and richer, allowing a more built-up environment, higher buildings and all the infrastructure that supports our civilization, communications and the like, the risk goes up."
John Boatwright, a geophysicist at the United States Geological Survey in Menlo Park, Calif., agreed. "In many places in the world, we've been building up infrastructure like crazy," he said. "We have to regard ourselves as really lucky" and the rash of recent tragedies as warnings of trouble ahead.
Roger Bilham, an earthquake expert at the University of Colorado now at Oxford University on a Guggenheim fellowship to study the rising urban risk, said the next quarter century might see three "megacities" hit hard, with three million lives lost.
"That's quite possible," he said, adding that disasters of such biblical dimensions might finally prod the world to get serious about quake-resistant structures. "That will be the spur."
Frank Press, author of "Understanding Earth" (Freeman, 1998) and a past president of the National Academy of Sciences, said Tokyo was one of the biggest worries given its global financial importance and history of devastating quakes. One in 1923 took 143,000 lives.
He said a future blow to the Tokyo area, now home to more than 10 million people, might "damage the world economy," setting off a downward spiral in advanced and developing nations. Dr. Press added that the Bank of Tokyo has quietly studied the likely cost of such an upheaval to Japan and the world, and concluded that it could be dire.
The United Nations is accelerating studies of the urban earthquake hazard as part of its global effort to help stem the toll of natural disasters.
Paradoxically, given the growing danger to cities, experts say that the earth's dynamism, including its volcanoes and earthquakes, restless plates and splintering rocks, is essential to making the planet habitable. For life in general and humans in particular, they add, the overall benefits far outweigh the risks.
"A planet without plate tectonics is dead," said Dr. Press, who as President Jimmy Carter's science adviser helped propel the nation's program to explore other worlds.
What makes the earth alive geologically is the slow release from its interior of heat, which fires out molten rock as volcanoes and pushes a dozen or so huge crustal plates into one another, causing rocks to break apart in earthquakes. As in most rocky bodies of the solar system, the planet's heat arises mainly from the slamming together eons ago of its rocky components and the radioactive decay of such elements as uranium, potassium and thorium.
This heat forces the earth's plates to move like conveyor belts, recycling millions of tons of crust each day. New plate material is ejected volcanically at long mid-ocean ridges. From there it spreads out across the ocean floor and is eventually destroyed hundreds or thousands of miles away as the cooling slab collides with and then sinks beneath other plates, plunging headlong back into the hot earth.
Over time, scientists say, all this action has helped produce the oceans, the atmosphere, the continents and fertile dirt, making the planet remarkably lush.
Does the solar system harbor hints of similar dynamism? Are there extraterrestrial quakes?
The Apollo astronauts found the Moon to be geologically dead. Its small size, which makes for a large surface area relative to its mass, was judged as having let its interior heat radiate into space long ago.
But now scientists are looking closely at Mars and several of Jupiter's moons, which are squeezed and probably heated by enormous tidal forces from the giant planet. Such places are seen as likely candidates for quakes and possibly life thriving in warm, dark, alien ecosystems down below inhospitable surfaces.
Dr. Torrence V. Johnson of NASA's Jet Propulsion Laboratory in Pasadena, Calif., said planetary scientists were drawing up plans to land quake sensors on such remote bodies, adding that similar dreams had gone unfulfilled for decades and might be sidetracked again.
"It's embarrassing," he said. "We have a good qualitative view of what happened" in the early days of the solar system but few details about its current state of geological activity.
On the earth, the action can be disastrous when square miles of homes and buildings are erected near the borders of the ambulatory slabs of crust that make up the planet's surface and slowly grind past one another about as fast as fingernails grow. These seams are the world's main quake zones, producing the vast majority of jolts.
Over the ages, the collision and separation of plates has produced the continents and their coastlines. The invisible seams are often nearby and potentially deadly for cities. California's famed San Andreas fault, for instance, is part of the larger process whereby the Pacific plate inches past the North American plate, keeping the golden state jumping and Angelenos edgy. The whole Pacific is ringed by analogous seams.
Taiwan is near the border of the Philippine and Eurasian plates, insuring a long history of upsets. Last week's quake had more than 1,000 aftershocks and took at least 2,000 lives.
Thousands of earthquakes occur around the globe every day, though most are small and only a few strike cities.
Waverly J. Person, director of the National Earthquake Information Center, in Golden, Colo., an arm of the United States Geological Survey, said historical records showed that the shaking of the earth by big quakes was in a relative lull right now, despite the apparent rash of recent upsets.
"Many people say these quakes are a sign that the earth is ending," Dr. Person said. "Well, the earth has been coming to an end for a long time."
Despite the lull, experts say the dangers will rise as more people inhabit cities near coasts and quake belts. Today, a third of the earth's population lives within 60 miles of a coast, experts say, and that fraction is expected to grow, increasing the vulnerability of people to both earthquakes and hurricanes.
Dr. Bilham of the University of Colorado wrote a pioneering paper on the rising quake hazard that was published in the journal Nature in 1988 and has explored the field ever since. He said the world now has 27 megacities, which the United Nations defines as holding at least eight million people, and that more than a third of these dense urban regions are near quivering plate boundaries.
The major cities at risk, he said, in addition to Los Angeles, Tokyo and Taipei, the capital of Taiwan, include Athens; Istanbul, in Turkey; Teheran, the capital of Iran; Islamabad, the capital of Pakistan; Calcutta, India; Jakarta, Indonesia, and Manila.
"The probability of any one megacity being hit is quite low," Dr. Bilham noted. "But if you take a plate boundary that has 10 cities, then the probability increases and I would not be surprised if one of those went quite soon."
Kerry Seih, a seismic expert at the California Institute of Technology, said the overall risk was rising. "There are a lot of big cities around the world that are going to be eaten in the coming century," he said, "unless we pay better attention" to erecting stronger homes and buildings.
"These earthquakes in the last year of the millennium are going to be an interesting test," Dr. Seih added. "Do we go on with business as usual, and fatalistically accept that we're going to loose tens of thousands of people a year? Or do we say, 'No, we have the know-how to survive more completely.' "
He said he had just returned from Turkey, where he had examined a riot of devastated buildings. "A lot that collapsed were new," he said, noting that such failures killed widely.
Both Turkey and Taiwan have building codes similar to California's, which are meant to let people get out alive in a major earthquake even if a structure is seriously damaged. Protective steps include reinforcing concrete walls and pillars, digging deep foundations and adding special joints that ease internal stresses when buildings start to shake and sway.
Experts say the codes are better enforced in Taiwan than in Turkey. That discrepancy, they say, added to the size of the Middle East disaster, which killed more than 15,000 people, and perhaps as many as twice that number. The total is unclear because of the slow recovery from the ruin.
Dr. Bilham of the University of Colorado, who recently returned to his sabbatical at Oxford from inspection trips to Turkey and Greece, said he was optimistic for the new century because so many new buildings would be constructed that in theory could be much stronger.
"It's buildings that kill people," said Dr. Bilham, a geophysicist, "not earthquakes."
Retrofitting to modern earthquake standards is very expensive, he said, costing upwards of half a building's value. But in new construction, earthquake precautions can add just 10 to 20 percent to building costs.
"So the good news is that if we come to grips with the reality that earthquakes will keep hitting planet earth, and acknowledge a certain future of shaking in cities, then we'll build buildings that don't fall down," he said. "By acting now, we can save a lot of lives."
The problem, he added, is that most developing countries, which most need the safeguards, tend to be mired in economic conflicts worsened by exploding populations. For instance, in one part of India, he said, planners faced a choice of whether to erect 150 buildings that were earthquake resistant or 200 that were not.
"They built 200," Dr. Bilham noted. "That's the kind of decision that's going on around the world."
In time, he added, probably after a series of urban disasters, and surely by the year 3000, "I'd be very surprised if we don't build buildings that don't fall down."

HEADLINE: Asia Fires Its Rounds In the Flat-Screen War;
Get in Line for Those Notebook PC's

BYLINE: By CALVIN SIMS 

DATELINE: MOBARA, Japan

 BODY:
In the center of this verdant peninsula, just east of Tokyo, is one of the world's largest computer screen factories, where hundreds of humming robots and uniformed workers in clean rooms assemble 200,000 flat panels a month.
The factory operates around the clock. Workers have taken no vacations for more than a year. But they still cannot work fast enough to feed the enormous appetite for the flat-panel screens, used in computer notebooks, video games, communications terminals and now, increasingly, in desktop monitors. 
 "Right now, we are operating nonstop at full capacity, and we have more orders than we can possibly fill," said Zenzo Tajima, product manager for Hitachi Ltd.'s displays division, which expects its sales of the flat screens, known as liquid crystal displays, or L.C.D.'s, to more than double this fiscal year.
Business is booming for flat-screen manufacturers across Japan, South Korea and Taiwan. The main reason is a surge in demand from consumers and businesses around the world for personal computers and other electronic devices that feature the flat screens, which are increasingly appealing because they save space, look stylish and are technologically capable of providing brilliant color images and graphics.
Demand for the screens is so strong that Asian producers cannot fill 10 percent to 20 percent of orders, industry analysts and executives said.
 The earthquake that struck Taiwan last Tuesday could worsen the shortage if manufacturers based there cannot quickly restart production. Power failures could delay screen-making assembly lines on Taiwan for weeks.
"Screen makers will see a huge jump in revenues this year," said David Mentley, a vice president at Stanford Resources Inc., a market research firm in San Jose, Calif.
Analysts estimate that the sales of advanced flat screens used in laptops and desk monitors will reach $11 billion this year, up from $7 billion last year, and that sales could reach $15 billion in 2000 and $17 billion in 2001.
While the shortfall has been a boon to screen producers after years of oversupply that depressed prices and profits, it has created big headaches for computer makers like I.B.M., Dell Computer and Toshiba, which are scrambling to satisfy the appetite of customers for notebook computers and large desktop monitors.
The shortage has come at an awkward time. The back-to-school buying season has been under way for weeks, and the year-end holiday gift season is just a few months away. Many parents and students are shopping for personal computers. Moreover, companies are upgrading their computer equipment in advance of the new millennium. Many want to get rid of the clunky cathode ray tube monitors that dominate their desk space in favor of the flat screens.
But analysts say that neither large nor small customers are finding the discounts on notebooks and other computer equipment that they encountered at this time last year. That partly reflects the limited supplies of screens, which have helped push up the prices that screen manufacturers are charging computer makers by about 20 to 40 percent.
Consumers also find that they often cannot buy the machine they want without a long wait.
Adriana Maixner, a first-year M.B.A. student at Babson College outside Boston, said Dell Computer's representatives told her it would take at least three weeks to ship a popular $2,800 laptop model with a 14-inch screen. Dell, which helped pioneer direct sales of computers to consumers, typically can ship an order within five days.
"That's what they told me, that there's one supplier for the entire industry for this size screen," Ms. Maixner, 26, said. "And they said, 'You're welcome to call anyone else, but you're going to have the same problem anywhere.' "
Neisha Frank, a spokeswoman for Dell Computer, acknowledged that the shortage had forced the computer maker to extend its normal lead time for product delivery. "Some large corporate customers cannot get what they want right away," Ms. Frank said. "Yes, we could be selling more if it were not for the L.C.D. shortage, but exactly how much more, who can say?"
In some cases, corporate customers are waiting several months for delivery of popular models of computer notebooks and large desktop monitors with advanced flat screens.
"All personal computer makers are feeling the impact of this shortage, because they can't make or sell as much product as they would like," Masahiro Ono, a Tokyo-based analyst for Wahlberg Securities, said. "They also can't offer low discounts that consumers like because the companies are paying more for what used to be an insignificant part of the computer -- the screen."
I.B.M. said that the company was experiencing record demand for its Thinkpad notebooks and that despite the screen shortage, it was meeting the needs of most long-term customers. New customers, however, are facing delays, which the company declined to quantify.
Rick McGee, an I.B.M. vice president, said that because of the shortage I.B.M. was paying 30 percent to 40 percent more for flat screens but that the increase had not been passed on to consumers because the company had reduced the cost of other components in its computers.
Some American computer companies said the shortage had affected them only slightly. "We haven't had trouble getting a steady supply of L.C.D.'s from our supplier, but it has been hard to get them at a good price," Ray Aldrick, a spokesman for the Hewlett-Packard Company, said. "It used to be the processor was the most expensive part of a personal computer, but now it's the flat panel display."
Gateway Inc., which recently introduced a desktop flat-panel model, called Profile, said that it had encountered no difficulty meeting demand for laptops or desktops.
The shortage in flat-screen production is partly rooted in demand for larger screens in notebooks and desktop computers. At the same time, Asian manufacturers, hurt by the region's severe economic slump a few years ago, cannot increase production quickly because they failed to make necessary capital investments.
Last year, responding to consumer requests for larger screens, manufacturers shifted production of standard 12-inch displays to 13-, 14- and 15-inch screens. That reduced the number of screens they could produce from the same raw materials.
The shift in consumer tastes and the industry's seeming inability to foresee it has also contributed to the shortage.
"Customers want bigger, finer-looking screens with brighter colors and less battery power," said Aston Bridgman, a spokesman for the NEC Corporation, which makes flat-panel screens. "That's what's driving this surge in demand for L.C.D.'s."
Many panel makers and industry analysts expect the shortage to continue for another six months to a year, as worldwide demand for computers continues to grow. Analysts, however, expect the shortage to abate by the third quarter of 2000, when new factories begin production. Eventually, supply will once again outweigh demand and prices will drop, these analysts said.
The industry shortage underscores just how dependent the United States is on Asia for flat-panel screens. While American companies have the basic technology to make them, they have been reluctant to invest the hundreds of millions of dollars needed to build large factories.
Japan accounts for about 63 percent of global production of the flat-panel screens, while South Korea contributes 36 percent and Taiwan about 1 percent, according to an industry study by ING Barings Securities. The brokerage firm expects those percentages to shift to 52 percent for Japan, 30 percent for Korea and 18 percent for Taiwan next year.
"American computer companies had their chance to invest in L.C.D. manufacturing years ago, but they chose to let the Japanese and Koreans duke it out, spending tens of millions of dollars on new factories," Mr. Mentley of Stanford Research said. "I don't think that the L.C.D. guys have a lot of sympathy for these computer companies that have been squeezing them all these years."

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: BONDS;
Modest Gains in U.S. Share Prices Send Treasuries Lower

BYLINE: By Bloomberg News 

 BODY:
Treasury bond prices fell for the first time in four sessions yesterday as stocks recovered a little of last week's declines, dampening the demand for bonds.
"The rebound in equities is taking the wind out of the sails in Treasuries," said Bruce Alston, who invests $1.5 billion of debt at Value Line Asset Management. 
 The price of the benchmark 30-year Treasury bond fell 6/32, to 98 15/32. The bond's yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, rose to 6.01 percent from 5.96 percent on Friday.
Bonds briefly trimmed losses after Robert Parry, president of the Federal Reserve Bank of San Francisco, said the Fed should wait until inflation picks up before raising interest rates. The bounce did not last as some traders noted that the remarks did not rule out an increase at the central bank's Oct. 5 meeting and noted that Mr. Parry is not a voting member of the Fed's monetary policy committee this year.
Treasuries were also held back as Fannie Mae, the CIT Group, PSEG Energy Holdings and Amerada Hess readied corporate bond sales.
Fannie Mae, the nation's largest buyer of home loans, is preparing to issue $3 billion of 6.5 percent five-year global notes and $1 billion of 6.25 percent 30-year bonds. CIT, a leading commercial finance company, plans to sell $1.5 billion of debt.
Some investors could not shake concerns that the Fed might raise interest rates for a third time this year to curb economic expansion. It has already lifted its target for overnight bank lending, the Federal funds rate, in two quarter-point moves to 5.25 percent. Still, many analysts say the Fed will probably hold rates steady next week.
The value of the 30-year bond has fallen 8.5 percent so far in 1999, reflecting price declines and reinvested interest.

HEADLINE: World Briefing

BYLINE: Compiled By Terence Neilan

 BODY:

EUROPE
 
BRITAIN: OPTIMISM ON JOBS -- The powerful head of the British Treasury, Gordon Brown, roused the opening day of the Labor Party annual convention in Bournemouth by pledging an end to poverty and jobs for all. With unemployment in Britain well below the European average, he told cheering delegates, "Britain can deliver, in our generation, employment opportunity for all -- a goal that this party and this movement has long sought, a goal now within our reach for the 21st century -- full employment for our country." Warren Hoge (NYT) 

GERMANY: SOLACE FOR SCHRODER -- The Social Democrats voiced relief on Monday, the first anniversary of their return to power, after hanging on to city halls in working-class heartlands after a month of losses in local elections. In mayoral runoffs across North Rhine-Westphalia, the biggest of Germany's 16 states, Chancellor Gerhard Schroder's party won in Bonn and Dortmund but lost the state capital, Dusseldorf, as well as Cologne, Germany's fourth largest city. (Reuters)
 
GERMANY: NEO-NAZIS ATTACK G.I.'s -- Five United States Army musicians were attacked by three neo-Nazi youths shouting "Sieg Heil!" after a concert in the eastern town of Prenzlau, the German police said. The members of the First Infantry Division's touring big band were beaten with sticks as they returned from a restaurant to their hotel at 5 A.M. The youths were apparently rightist radicals "motivated by hate of foreigners," authorities said. Victor Homola (NYT)
 
ITALY: TURNCOAT DEBATE -- Political criticism of prosecutors' reliance on turncoat mobsters has heightened since the acquittal of former Prime Minister Giulio Andreotti in the killing of a journalist in 1979. Even before the verdict, Parliament was studying laws to restrict the use of such witnesses. "They must be used in the most balanced manner," Justice Minister Oliviero Diliberto said. "But without them we wouldn't know the first thing about the Mafia." Alessandra Stanley (NYT)
 
NETHERLANDS: ANNE FRANK HOME REOPENS -- Anne Frank House, where the Jewish teen-ager wrote her diary before being deported to a Nazi concentration camp, will be reopened today by Queen Beatrix after extensive restoration that began in 1995. The front has again become a warehouse and offices, looking much like the building Anne knew as she and her family hid in the secret annex in the back. The home is one of Amsterdam's most popular tourist sites. Marlise Simons (NYT)
 
MIDDLE EAST
 
EGYPT: MUBARAK RE-ELECTED -- As expected, President Hosni Mubarak won his fourth six-year term, the Interior Minister said, taking nearly 94 percent of the vote in a referendum on his presidency in which he was the only candidate. Mr. Mubarak is one of Washington's closest allies in the Middle East. Opposition groups boycotted the ballot, calling it a sham and demanding direct, multiparty elections for the presidency. (AP)
 
EGYPT: TORTURE COMPENSATION -- In an unprecedented ruling, a court ordered the Ministry of Interior to pay compensation to two accused Muslim Brotherhood members who were tortured by the police more than 40 years ago, police officials said. The court awarded $9,000 each to Mohammed Abdel Adhim and Hassan Mohammed in compensation for physical and mental torture they said they underwent during their 1954-56 imprisonment. Both men are in their late 70's. (AP)
 
IRAQ: NORTH AND SOUTH ATTACKED -- Western planes bombed a civilian target in northern Iraq, an Iraqi military spokesman said, and were fired on in the south. The spokesman, quoted by the official Iraqi News Agency, said American and British aircraft flew 22 sorties over the north. He did not mention any casualties. In the south, the spokesman said, Iraqi units fired missiles and antiaircraft guns and forced the planes to retreat to bases in Kuwait and Saudi Arabia. (Reuters)
 
IRAN, IRAQ: P.O.W. EXCHANGE -- Iran is to release an unspecified number of Iraqi prisoners of war on Wednesday to mark the centenary of the birth of Ayatollah Ruhollah Khomeini, the founder of its Islamic Government, officials here said. The handover will take place at a ceremony to be attended by Iranian political and military figures, they said. Baghdad says some 13,000 Iraqis remain imprisoned in Iran, while Teheran says 2,806 of its troops are detained in Iraq. (Agence France-Presse)
 
AFRICA
 
ALGERIA: MILITANTS SURRENDER -- Sixteen more Islamic militants have surrendered to Algerian authorities, taking advantage of a peace plan that would grant them amnesty, the daily Liberte in Paris reported. More than 98 percent of Algerian voters on Sept. 16 favored a peace referendum that called for Islamic militants to give up their arms and turn themselves in. (AP)
 
ANGOLA: BIG ARMY PUSH -- The Angolan Army waged a major offensive against a key stronghold of the rebel Unita movement, which said the town of Bailundo had been virtually razed and hundreds of civilians had been killed. Gen. Joao Baptista de Matos, the Angolan commander in chief, is leading the operation, an army spokesman said. (Agence France-Presse)
 
THE AMERICAS
 
MEXICO: VICTORY FOR THE PRI -- The governing party of President Ernesto Zedillo, known as the PRI, overwhelmed an opposition coalition in elections in Coahuila, which borders Texas. The PRI candidate for Governor, Enrique Martinez, won some 59 percent of votes, while the candidate representing four opposition parties, Juan Garcia Villa, received only about 33 percent, according to preliminary returns. PRI mayoral candidates were also leading in the state's three largest cities. Sam Dillon (NYT)
 
ECUADOR: ABDUCTED SPANIARD FREED -- A Spanish citizen who was one of a group of 12 foreigners kidnapped by armed men in a remote Amazon region of Ecuador has been found, Defense Minister Jose Gallardo said. The Spaniard, Ander Mimenza, left, appeared in the Lake Agrio region and contacted authorities. He was later flown to Quito at the request of the Spanish Embassy, Mr. Gallardo told local television. (Reuters)
 
ASIA
 
AFGHANISTAN: TALIBAN ACCUSES U.N. -- Three years after the militant Islamic Taliban movement captured Kabul, they are still unable to secure Afghanistan's United Nations General Assembly seat, and as a result they are threatening to reduce their already low level of cooperation with international organizations even further, a representative of the movement said in Pakistan. The Taliban, which has watched other governments change hands by force and get almost immediate recognition, overthrew an unruly coalition of former anti-Soviet mujahedeen once supported by the United States that is still fighting on the fringes of Afghanistan. Barbara Crossette (NYT)
 
AFGHANISTAN: TALIBAN'S NEW OFFENSIVE -- The Taliban movement reported taking several areas in the north where it has begun a second offensive within two months to dislodge opposition forces. The Taliban seized Dashti Archi in Kunduz Province and Hazratbagh in adjacent Takhar Province. (Reuters)
 
TAJIKISTAN: VOTERS BACK CHANGE -- Tajikistan has given strong backing in a referendum to constitutional changes that would extend the President's term to a single seven-year term, instead of two consecutive five-year terms as at present. It would also allow the opposition to set up Islamic political parties, an official said. Voters are hoping the changes will strengthen a shaky peace accord between the Moscow-backed secular Government and the Islamic United Tajik Opposition movement, which fought a civil war from 1992 to 1997. (Reuters)
 
SOUTH KOREA: DOGS GET A REPRIEVE -- The Government said it would not legalize the sale of dog meat, considered a delicacy by many Koreans, before the 2002 World Cup soccer finals get underway here. Several opposition lawmakers introduced a bill in the National Assembly last month to legalize the sale, but without Government support it is unlikely to pass. The Ministry of Agriculture and Forestry said authorizing sales of dog flesh would give South Korea a bad international image. (AP)

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
America Online Unit to Reduce Subscription Costs in Britain

BYLINE: By ANDREW ROSS SORKIN 

DATELINE: LONDON, Sept. 27

 BODY:
Hoping to stem its market slide in Britain, America Online said today that it would lower the monthly cost of subscribing to its service here.
AOL will charge users one British penny a minute, or 1.65 cents, in addition to the monthly subscription fee of 9.99 pounds , or $16.45, making it more competitive with Freeserve P.L.C., the British market leader, which requires only the cost of a local phone call to connect to the Internet. 
The move surprised some analysts because AOL U.K. had introduced Netscape Online, a subscription-free service similar to Freeserve, with considerable fanfare only a month ago. AOL U.K. is a unit of AOL Europe, which in turn is a joint venture between America Online and the German media conglomerate Bertelsmann.
In contrast to the United States, where Internet access over a telephone line is relatively inexpensive, access in Britain and most of Continental Europe can seem prohibitive. Users pay by the minute for local phone calls, in addition to the charge levied by the Internet access provider.
In Britain, the cost can be high as 6.6 cents a minute during peak hours, and range downward to 1.65 cents a minute at night or on weekends. As a result, Internet services like Freeserve and its imitators have emerged, luring customers by not charging a monthly subscription fee for Internet access. These subscription-free services make money by sharing a percentage of the revenue generated by the cost of the user's phone call with the telephone company.
AOL U.K. users spend an average of 17 minutes a day on line while users of America Online in the United States spend an hour a day on line. Now, AOL hopes to increase its share of business by keeping its metered charges on a par with those of its free-subscription competitors.
"If you make it cheap enough to use it all the time, people will use it all the time," said Robert W. Pittman, the president of America Online, in a telephone interview.
Some analysts said the new pricing system could cannibalize subscribers from America Online's new sister brand, Netscape Online. Bruce Kasrel of Forrester Research said, "It undermines Netscape Online, but they are willing to put Netscape Online on the chopping block if it means all of the subscription-free services are on the block with it."
AOL has traditionally relied on the monthly subscription model, which tends to provide significantly higher profit margins than the free-subscription model. As well as getting a part of local phone charges, the free services depend on revenue from advertising and electronic commerce.
Mr. Pittman maintained that America Online would not encroach on Netscape Online's customers because, he said, the two brands appealed to different segments of the market. Netscape Online, he said, is for the budget user, while AOL's proprietary content appeals to families.
Some analysts speculated that the move to what AOL U.K. labeled "off-peak all the time" would result in a surge of new subscribers that might overwhelm the company's network, much as happened in the United States in late 1996 and early 1997, when the parent America Online introduced flat-rate pricing. But Mr. Pittman said, "I hope we've learned from our mistakes."
Noah Yasskin, an analyst at Jupiter Communications here, predicted that as phone companies like British Telecommunications moved toward flat-rate pricing for local calls, the war between free-subscription Internet services and subscription-based services would blur.
Freeserve said today that it would offer 10 free hours of Internet access a month during off-peak hours if users placed a certain total of calls with its partner, Energis P.L.C.
Freeserve is expected to announce its first-quarter financial results on Tuesday, and analysts expect its growth to have slowed as a result of increased competition. http://www.nytimes.com
