HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Japanese Officials Insist Yen Policy Hasn't Changed

BYLINE: By STEPHANIE STROM 

DATELINE: TOKYO, Sept. 27

 BODY:
Japanese monetary officials have spent the last few days trying to dispel the impression that they changed monetary policy to weaken the yen as a result of foreign pressure at a weekend meeting of rich industrialized nations.
The efforts by the Bank of Japan and the Ministry of Finance came after the Group of Seven countries issued a surprisingly frank statement Saturday night that they considered the yen's recent strength undesirable. 
 The statement from the meeting, which was held in Washington, went further than financial markets had been expecting and the yen plunged in value against the dollar when markets reopened today on speculation that central banks of the G-7 nations might act together to weaken the Japanese currency.
Apparently worried that the statement might have sent a wrong signal about possible concessions, Japanese officials said Sunday and today that they had made no policy changes.
But the dollar, which several times last week fell to its lowest levels in three and a half years, still ended the day higher yesterday. In late New York trading, the dollar cost 106.00 yen, up from 104.17 yen late Friday.
The yen has risen more than 15 percent in value in recent months on evidence that Japan's long-stagnant economy is finally expanding. But a stronger yen could threaten that recovery by making Japanese exports more expensive, hurting sales and profits in Japan.
The United States has said that Japan must take more aggressive steps to weaken the currency, like expanding the supply of money in the banking system -- a proposal the Japanese central bank rejected just last week. So the G-7 statement of unity concerning the yen suggested that Japan had changed its position.
As headlines around the world crowed that the bank had caved in to pressure, Masaru Hayami, the bank's governor, summoned reporters to a hastily called news conference on Sunday in Washington, where he told them they were misconstruing what had happened.
"Nothing has changed," he said in response to reports that the bank planned to print yen to spark inflation; that it had declared foreign exchange rates would play a role in setting monetary policy, and that it had otherwise amended its recent hard-line opposition to further easing its already loose monetary stance.
Indeed, comparing the statement Mr. Hayami issued at the G-7 meeting and the one issued after the bank's policy board met last Tuesday and voted not to change monetary policy, it seems no shift has occurred.
"The distinctions in what the bank has been saying have been lost in the very high level of politics between it and the Ministry of Finance," said Jeffrey Young, an economist at Nikko Salomon Smith Barney in Tokyo.
In Washington, the bank said its policy of maintaining short-term interest rates at zero percent has insured enough liquidity in the banking system -- the same thing it said last Tuesday. It said it was concerned that the yen's rise might negatively affect the Japanese economy -- which it also said last Tuesday.
Additionally, the bank said it would use what flexibility its current monetary policy offers to respond to changes in the economy and in the financial markets. Mr. Hayami made a similar statement at a news conference last Tuesday.
And the bank said it was considering how to change its money market operations to increase the money supply, something it has also previously said.
"The only thing that matters about that is by how much -- and Mr. Hayami has never said how much," said a senior bank official here. "I'm afraid the press has been fooled."

HEADLINE: A HALF-CENTURY IN CHINA: THE PANDA PROTECTOR;
She Knows Species Like Family Members

 BODY:
Lu Zhi, a leading light in China's nascent conservation movement, was only 16 back in 1981 when she tested into elite Beijing University from her home in the northwestern city of Lanzhou.
Eighteen years later, she has more than fulfilled that early promise, distinguishing herself as a scientist and an awesomely hardy field researcher who may know more than anyone about China's most famous species, the giant panda. 
 And now, looking younger than her years, she has thrown herself into the most complicated challenge yet: saving the mountain redoubts of the thousand or so surviving pandas, redoubts that happen to be densely populated by poor farmers.
Last year, a Government group named her one of China's "Top 10 Young Intellectuals," an unusual honor for a Chinese employed by one of the few foreign-based nongovernmental organizations allowed here, the World Wide Fund for Nature.
As a college senior and an unhappy biochemistry major, Ms. Lu says, she went on a field trip with Pan Wenshi, the doyen of Chinese panda researchers, and found her calling. So for her doctorate, she switched to zoology.
It was a personal breakthrough. "Up to then, I always did what people told me to do," she said in her Beijing office the other day, just before taking off for rugged western Sichuan Province.
When she started her research, little was known about panda living habits, and she would spend most of seven years tracking them in the wild, often without adequate food or warmth for herself. Today, she plays down those hardships and notes how lucky she was to find pandas quickly and then come to know them "like members of a family."
In path-breaking publications, Ms. Lu and her mentor, Mr. Pan, refuted the impression, derived from pandas in zoos, that the species has trouble breeding. Left alone in their bamboo havens, it turned out, they do just fine; the problem is protecting the land.
Another big question was whether pandas are dangerously inbred. So Ms. Lu took a fellowship at the National Institutes of Health in Maryland, where she mastered genetic analysis and found that inbreeding is not yet serious. Passing up offers abroad, she returned home to make another leap, from science to sociology. She now spends her time with local farmers trying to harmonize economic development with wildlife.
No panda pictures grace her office walls. "Sometimes I get tired of them," she says. The panda is the global logo of her organization and a lucrative tool for fund-raising, all of which leaves her cold. "To me, the panda picture becomes a symbol of conservation politics," she said. "And a panda in the office is different from a panda in the wild."
She has turned down offers of Government jobs, but is well aware of the Government's power. She expects to return to Beijing University, where, she says, she could be in a better position to help shape national policies, train a new generation of conservation scientists and perhaps even find time to re-acquaint herself with some pandas.
"I don't see them any more," she laments. "I see people now."

HEADLINE: SPORTS BUSINESS;
Refusal of Bid for Jets Raises Antitrust Issue

BYLINE: By Richard Sandomir 

 BODY:
Today, 111,031 people own the Green Bay Packers. The only publicly owned team in the National Football League is looked upon as a paragon of sports democracy -- even if shareholders have no say in team decisions or even a crack at season tickets -- and a delightful anomaly among gazillionaire owners.
For the N.F.L., one community-based ownership like the Packers is enough. Issuing stock in 1950 saved the Packers from leaving Green Bay. But no such survival issue exists with the Jets, who have 26 potential buyers in pursuit. 
 So last week, the league commissioner, Paul Tagliabue, spurned BuytheJets.com, an Internet fan initiative that has received commitments of $17.5 million, from 8,000 people, to buy the Jets, which will cost at least $500 million.
"Rules aside," Tagliabue wrote in a letter to Peter F. Vallone, Speaker of the New York City Council, "no team in any league is owned in this matter and an ownership structure of this type presents many issues and concerns." Vallone had written to Tagliabue in favor of his considering the bid.
John Crotty, a co-founder of BuytheJets.com, plans to urge the Web site's fan/investors to ask their Congressmen to review the league's antitrust exemption, which covers television contracts and labor, not ownership.
"Why should they get the exemption if they're not willing to consider the people seriously?" he said. "It's an insult to people's intelligence to say our structure doesn't work. Who can argue it doesn't work?"
In prohibiting Green Bay redux or corporate ownership, the league insists that teams be owned by individuals and that the managing partner control at least 30 percent of the team's stock and have no more than 25 partners.
"I think all restrictions about corporate ownership and the number of people who can own a team are potential antitrust violations," said Andrew Zimbalist, a sports finance expert and economics professor at Smith College.
But why is the BuytheJets.com plan anathema to the league?
First, with thousands of individual owners, the league wonders who would be in charge and who among the investors possess unsavory connections.
Second, it has doubts that the cash pledges to BuytheJets.com will yield real money.
Third, it wonders if the fan/investors can raise a half-billion dollars for the team, can they raise still more to operate the team and help finance a stadium? Would there be periodic Web pleas when cash gets tight?
Four, the league likes the status quo. "Fans have been very well served by local ownership and nowhere is that more true than in New York, where the Mara, Tisch and Hess families have been model sports owners," said Jeff Pash, an N.F.L. executive vice president.
Zimbalist said: "They'd rather have one zillionaire owner than any other form. But the big issue is control." The league fears that "another Al Davis" would emerge from the thousands of BuytheJets.com owners, he added.
Gary Roberts, a professor of sports and law at Tulane Law School, said no valid antitrust claim existed against the league for restricting ownership.
"I think an antitrust challenge is nonsense, but rhetorically, it sounds like an antitrust case," Roberts said, adding that a lawsuit could receive a sympathetic hearing depending on the judge. But he said an antitrust standard -- of consumer harm because of restricting ownership forms -- did not exist.
"Consumers are indifferent to who owns a football team," he said.
Still, BuytheJets.com could argue that it would be more likely to hold down ticket-price increases than a single owner looking to increase revenues.
The call here is for the N.F.L. to open the process to BuytheJets.com after performing a preliminary check on the credibility of the founders. The bid would be great for fan relations. And if the Web site becomes the J-E-T-S' O-W-N-E-R, the Giants Stadium superfan Fireman Eddie Anzalone could become its president.
 
Miller's (Small) Regrets
Johnny Miller cannot help himself. The NBC golf analyst says what he thinks far more than most analysts, and during the weekend's Ryder Cup telecast, his comments riled a few United States team members. "I've never been a players' announcer," Miller said yesterday.
On Saturday, Miller said that Ben Crenshaw, the United States captain, had erred by replacing Jeff Maggert with the underachieving Justin Leonard for the afternoon's matches. "I said, 'The only place Leonard should be was in his room watching TV,' " Miller said, recalling his remarks. "Maggert and Sutton were the stars so far. But if I had to say it again, I might not have. After I said it, I knew inside that I shouldn't have said it."
Leonard, the hero of the United States comeback, Davis Love 3d and Jim Furyk rejected Miller's comments after their matches Sunday. "I had three different arrows stuck in me, but they must have said this was their chance to tell Johnny Miller he was too strong in what he was saying," Miller said.
Furyk was miffed that Miller called him the underdog against Sergio Garcia in Sunday's singles. "It teed him off for me to say he was the underdog to a 19-year-old," Miller said. "I probably could have said 'slight underdog.' "
Miller went back on the air to defend his style of commentary after the golfers took their hacks at him. "I didn't want to, but I was told I had to," he said.
For NBC, the United States comeback produced its highest-ever overnight Nielsen rating for the Ryder Cup, a 6.3, up 62 percent from 1997. http://www.nytimes.com

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Latin American Markets Respond Calmly to Default
Stocks rose and currencies steadied across much of Latin America, the first day of trading after weekend news that Ecuador would default on some foreign debt and Colombia abandoned its system for defending the exchange value of its currency.
Analysts attributed the generally calm regional response to relief that both nations had ended weeks of speculation and uncertainty about how they intended to address their economic problems. Even with the Colombian central bank no longer intervening to prop up the value of the peso, the currency there slipped barely 1 percent. Page C6. 

Veba and Viag Agree to Merger
The German energy and chemical conglomerates Veba and Viag agreed on a $14.1 billion merger. "Size matters, especially in the energy sector," said Ulrich Hartmann, chairman of Veba. The merger is taking place as the competitive market for electricity in Germany has driven prices down by 30 percent in the last year. C4.
 
U.S. Share Prices Rise Slightly
Stock prices rebounded as investors became selective buyers of shares that were hit hard by last week's selloff. The Dow Jones industrial average was up 24.06 points, to 10,303.39. The Nasdaq composite index rose 21.34 points, to 2,761.75. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index gained 5.95 points, to 1,283.31. C13.
 
Treasury bond prices fell for the first time in four sessions as the recovery in stocks dampened the demand for bonds. The yield on the 30-year Treasury bond rose to 6.01 percent from 5.96 percent on Friday. C13.
 
Italy Investigates Money Laundering
A two-year criminal investigation in Italy has turned up evidence that Russian organized crime operators have funneled millions of dollars through the Bank of New York in what Italian prosecutors called a money laundering scheme. Through an examination of bank records and telephone wiretaps, investigators in Rimini say they have uncovered the first concrete examples of organized Russian gangs in Italy moving illicit funds through a group of accounts at the Bank of New York that are under investigation by the Federal Bureau of Investigation. A1.
 
Internet Price War in Britain
America Online's British affiliate said it would lower the monthly cost of subscribing to its service so it could compete against companies that provide Internet access for the price of local phone calls. C4.
 
Copper Concerns Rethink Merger
Asarco and Cyprus Amax Minerals said they would seek alternatives to their merger agreement of last July, moves which may have the effect of putting the companies on the auction block. Phelps Dodge, which has made a hostile bid for Asarco and Cyprus Amax, and Grupo Mexico, Mexico's largest copper producer, which offered to buy Asarco for $1.03 billion in cash on Friday, are both pursuing takeovers. Shares of Asarco rose $3.375 yesterday, to $26.9375 C2.
 
Merrill to Offer After-Hours Trading
Merrill Lynch plans to offer after-hours trading to its five million brokerage customers next year. The announcement came two weeks after Merrill Lych purchased 14.3 percent of Archipelago, an electronic communications network. C8.
 
I.B.M. Wins Contract With Dell
I.B.M. won a contract to provide $6 billion in services to Dell Computer during the next seven years, enhancing Dell's effort to sell sophisticated computers to corporations. The growing relationship helps Dell expand in the market for high-priced equipment like servers and data storage devices and also lets I.B.M. capitalize on its technology and reach more customers. C7.
 
U.A.W. Approves Daimler Contract
Members of the United Auto Workers approved a four-year contract with DaimlerChrysler by a wide margin. The new deal, covering 75,000 hourly and salaried workers, was approved by 86 percent of voters in balloting Saturday. C8.

HEADLINE: A Record U.S. Fine at a Connecticut Nuclear Plant

BYLINE: By MIKE ALLEN 

DATELINE: HARTFORD, Sept. 27

 BODY:
The owner of the Millstone Nuclear Power Station in eastern Connecticut admitted today that it had falsified environmental records and deliberately promoted unqualified plant operators. The owner, Northeast Nuclear Energy Company, pleaded guilty to 23 Federal felonies and agreed to pay $10 million in fines, the largest penalty ever for a nuclear plant in this country.
The violations took place from 1994 to 1996. 
 In entering the guilty plea in United States District Court here, Northeast admitted that hydrazine, a toxic chemical used to reduce corrosion of pipes, had gushed into Long Island Sound at a rate of one gallon an hour during 1996 and that testers at the company had diluted their samples with ocean water to hide the problem from Federal regulators.
Federal prosecutors said today that they were unable to prove environmental harm from the hydrazine.
The company also admitted that after it submitted fraudulent information to the Nuclear Regulatory Commission, 12 control-room operators received Federal licenses. Problems with the training program at Millstone came to light in 1996 when six of seven candidates for control-room operator licenses failed Federal exams, prosecutors said.
In presenting the evidence in court today, the prosecutors said the corner-cutting could have put the plant in the hands of workers unable to prevent a crisis.
The investigation, previously secret, came to light in court today when the company waived indictment and pleaded guilty. As part of the plea agreement, the company was put on three years' probation, which allows prosecutors to monitor operations more closely.
The Millstone case is just the second time that a nuclear power plant owner had been found guilty of felonies. Fifteen years ago, after the 1979 accident at the Three Mile Island plant near Harrisburg, Pa., which began the industry's plunge from public acceptance, Metropolitan Edison pleaded guilty to falsifying records at the plant.
Officials at Millstone said they hoped today's pleas would end the long period in which the plant has been considered a national symbol of mismanagement in the nuclear power industry. In 1997, Millstone was assessed a civil fine of $2.1 million by the Nuclear Regulatory Commission. In 1996, a Time magazine cover story treated the plant as an egregious example of lax enforcement of Federal regulations.
The three reactors in Waterford, Conn., were shut down under orders from the Nuclear Regulatory Commission because of problems with design, safety and paperwork. One of the three reactors is being decommissioned, while the other two have been restarted and are producing energy.
Since 1996, the utility has hired new executives and taken steps that it says would prevent violations in the future. Federal prosecutors, while describing the old offenses in harsh terms, said in today's proceedings that they had confidence in the new managers.
After the large number of Millstone candidates flunked their licensing tests, investigators discovered that they had not put in the required number of training hours nor mastered the maneuvers that Millstone had falsely claimed, the Government charged. Some of the candidates have since been retrained and are now on the job, the company said.
Federal prosecutors said economic pressure brought on by deregulation of the nuclear industry had contributed to the violations. "Rather than treat the problem," said Joseph C. Hutchison, an assistant United States Attorney, "the shortcut was taken so there was some economic saving."
Mr. Hutchison said complaints by community groups and workers at the plants had led to the three-year Federal investigation.
Stephen C. Robinson, the United States Attorney for Connecticut, said of the agreement, "No matter who you are, no matter how big or how powerful, if you endanger our citizens, if you violate the law, if you lie to regulators and choose profits over the public, we will come after you."
Michael G. Morris, the chairman and and chief executive of Northeast Utilities, the parent of Northeast Nuclear, did not dispute a statement that the violations were deliberate, but said the public had never been in danger. As he left the courthouse today, Mr. Morris attributed the violations to "inattention to detail" and "inadvertence," not deregulation. "The whole notion that you react differently in a competitive marketplace is true, but it doesn't cause this kind of behavior," he said. He said the lesson for plant owners was "it's better to operate within all of the laws and the requirements because these kind of fines, these kinds of embarrassments, will come your way if you don't."
Mr. Morris was hired in 1997, after the offenses took place. Nevertheless, Judge Robert N. Chatigny called him to the front of the court this morning and told him sternly he hoped the plea reflected a commitment by the company "to be a better citizen in the future than it was in the past."
Judge Chatigny added that despite the efforts of those who work in the public interest, "Ultimately, the public has to depend on the good faith, honesty and integrity of the people who manage our large companies."
A spokeswoman for Northeast Utilities, Mary Jo Keating, said the plea agreement should help with the plans to sell the plant, as required by state legislation deregulating the industry. "The worst thing in the market is any kind of uncertainty," she said from the company headquarters in Berlin, Conn.
David M. Pittinos of the Toxics Action Center, an environmental group in West Hartford, said today's plea vindicated residents who had been worried about mismanagement and safety at the plant. The admissions were especially damning, he said, because "these companies typically offer to do just about anything to weasel out of criminal charges and reach some out-of-court settlement."
In addition to the 23 counts involving the nuclear plant, another subsidiary, Northeast Utilities Service Company, pleaded guilty to two felonies resulting from the use of a fire hose to dilute water samples taken at Devon Station, a coal-fired power plant in Milford. Prosecutors said that their case against the service company was complete, but that their investigation was continuing and that individuals might be prosecuted.
As part of the $10 million in fines, Northeast Utilities agreed to donate $1 million to endow a business ethics chair at the University of Connecticut in Storrs, and $650,000 for an environmental clinic at its School of Engineering; $1 million to help local towns buy riverfront land for conversion into public parks, and $650,000 to Riverfront Recapture, a Hartford group, for its leadership camp for disadvantaged city youth.
The agreement specified that the gifts were not tax deductible. The company said it did not intend to pass the costs on to consumers.

HEADLINE: Crowded Florida Keys A Paradise in Trouble

BYLINE: By RICK BRAGG 

DATELINE: KEY WEST, Fla., Sept. 23

 BODY:
For a minute or two, as the tall, tanned, beautiful woman from Norway splashed and laughed in the shallows, it was just another picture-postcard moment in the blue-green waters of Key West.
The vacationing Norwegian, Linda Eng, had not seen the signs on George Smathers Beach that warned that this water she was wading in was swimming with potentially deadly bacteria, and she was still flashing a 60-watt smile when a passerby told her that the placid, inviting water was contaminated. 
 "With what?" she asked.
Human feces, she was told.
Her smile turned upside down.
"I want to take a shower now," she said, looking stricken.
Signs warning of that hazard have been posted most of the summer on beaches on this coral rock island of Key West, perhaps the most storied and romantic tropical hideaway in the Eastern United States. Earlier this month, health officials posted more signs on beaches on Marathon and Tavernier Keys, after water there showed fecal contamination.
In Key West, antiquated, leaking sewage lines are allowing raw sewage to seep into the shallows, and work crews are tearing up streets all over the city to repair the sewer system, a project that the state -- even before this crisis -- ordered the city to complete by 2002.
State health officials are still trying to determine why the human waste showed up in the Upper Keys off Marathon and Tavernier, closer to the Florida peninsula. Probably, Keys residents reason, it is coming from old septic tanks and sewer lines there, too, overwhelmed by overdevelopment, and never compatible with the high water table and porous limestone ground in the first place. It is a serious health risk. E. coli bacteria, found in human waste, can kill.
"Young children and the elderly are at the most risk," said Stephanie Walters, administrator for the Monroe County Health Department, "especially children, because they tend to drink the water while they are swimming." The bacteria in the water can cause nausea, diarrhea, ear and eye infections and respiratory illness, she said.
The contamination has had only a minor impact on tourism -- in part because vacationers usually learn about the hazard only after they have arrived, in part because most people who come to Key West are more interested in diving in cleaner reef waters offshore -- but the hazard has prompted some to cut vacations short, hotel managers said.
"They come all this way, just to find a sign on the beach with a skull and crossbones," said Carl Hiaasen, the best-selling novelist whose books on South Florida, beginning with "Tourist Season" and more recently "Lucky You," have harpooned politicians and developers for their ruination of Florida's ecology.
"Every time a toilet is flushed in the Keys, these E. coli paddle out on their little surfboards," said Mr. Hiaasen, who lives in Islamorada in the Upper Keys and writes a popular, politically acerbic column for The Miami Herald. "We're poisoning it," he added, more seriously. "It's heartbreaking."
A survey by the Key West Hotel and Motel Association, expected soon, will determine how much contaminated beaches are costing the Keys in tourism dollars, said Jack Smith, the association's executive vice president. But more than money is at stake.
"This will be the thing that kills the Keys if we don't do something about it," said Nora Williams, the Monroe County Commissioner who called for water testing earlier this year. She did that, she said, because the water looked dirty, something other Keys residents have noticed with increasing dismay. Water is what lures people here, to live or to play.
Although the city of Key West has an airport that can handle commercial jets, many people choose to drive down from Miami, across a connect-the-dots highway of bridges, more than 120 miles in all, that link the island chain with the rest of the state.
Look to the west, driving from the peninsula to Key West on Route 1, and small, green islands stretch across the shallows toward Florida Bay and the Gulf of Mexico. Look to the east, and the Atlantic shimmers blue toward a seemingly endless horizon, but with Cuba just 100 miles away. Some islands are barely wider than the road, with just enough room between asphalt and open water for tiki bars, charter-boat businesses and of course real estate offices.
It is easy to believe, floating in such beauty, that it is still paradise, residents here say, but for a long time it has been changing. Route 1 creeps with bumper-to-bumper traffic, even on a weekday afternoon, the convertibles and rental mini-vans of tourists sandwiched by trucks hauling mobile homes and concrete. At night, on Marathon Key, it is possible to hear the electricity hiss and buzz through the power lines as it travels from the peninsula to the bars on Key West's Duval Street, where the venerable Sloppy Joe's, where Ernest Hemingway drank, now shares the street with the Hard Rock Cafe and Planet Hollywood.
According to the Bureau of Economic and Business Research at the University of Florida, the 1990 population of Monroe County, which includes the Keys, was 78,000 people, and environmental groups were screaming that the chain of islands, with so little dry ground, was overdeveloped. In 1998, the population of the islands had swelled to 85,646.
In April, there were 27,698 people living in Key West, compared with 24,832 in 1990. But in Key West alone, more than 18,600 visitors a day, on average, fill the island, according to the Key West Planning Department.
To see the impact, just stroll to any canal. "In the past couple of years, I've seen largely reduced visibility and increased murkiness," said Ms. Williams. "Most visitors would not notice it -- people thought it was so clean they could drink from it -- but it has become pretty noticeable to natives and residents.
The Key West City Commission voted unanimously last week to put a bond referendum on the Nov. 2 ballot, asking voters to decide if the city should borrow up to $23 million to accelerate work on the sewer lines and upgrade the city's sewage treatment plant. Elsewhere in the Keys, county officials have a block-by-block plan to repair damaged and obsolete sewage systems, said Jack Teague, environmental administrator for Monroe County.
What is really needed is a new waste-water treatment plant for the Upper Keys, some residents and environmental groups say.
"Mother Nature is saying, Enough is enough," said Lynne Cameron, who heads a privately financed social service agency in Miami, but lives part of the week in Tavernier.
Fran Decker, who lives in Marathon and heads the local advisory council to the Florida Keys National Marine Sanctuary, has seen the canal behind her home grow murky, and the fish and lobster populations there have dwindled. "I feel like many people were in denial," Ms. Decker said. "They didn't feel like it could happen here. It kind of contradicts what the Keys are about."
It only became a real issue, said Mr. Hiaasen and others who live here, when it became an economic one. "The sad truth is, this had been around for a long time," Mr. Hiaasen said. "They only just started testing for it. Some people were actually upset that the news was published before Labor Day. God forbid we should warn anybody."
But even then, city and tourism officials have put a positive spin on it. "I've read that the beaches here are closed, but none of the beaches are closed," said Mr. Smith, of the Key West Hotel and Motel Association. Sunbathing, he said, is perfectly safe.
"Now," he added, "going in the water is another story."
Hoteliers are taking the threat seriously, posting the warning at front desks.
"We've had our grim days this year," with cancellations, said Mike Proimos, general manager of the Marriott Casa Marina Resort in Key West. But like many others here, he points out that Key West has much more to offer than beaches.
Diving trips to the coral reef that runs alongside Key West have been mostly unaffected, because that water is not tainted with the feces, and the Key West bars -- the real reason many people come here -- are still open. Like New Orleans, Key West is one of those cities where it is common, if not acceptable, to be dog-drunk at 10 A.M.
"They come down here because they like the atmosphere, the landscape, the partying," Mr. Marshall of Sloppy Joe's said.
"I would still go swimming. It wouldn't bother me," he said. "You don't see raw sewage floating around beside you or anything."
At night, people still gather to watch the sunset, drinks in hand. But Smathers Beach was recently deserted, except for a middle-age couple who were very careful not to get their feet wet.

HEADLINE: Noisy Party For a Man Known to All

BYLINE: By JAMES BARRON 

 BODY:
There were those in the noisy crowd at Tavern on the Green last night who had to ask who was the woman over there (a boldface name) or the guy in the plaid suit (a judge) or the man in the glasses (a millionaire -- no, a billionaire). But no one had to ask, Howard who?
It was a party for the man everyone seemed to know, Howard J. Rubenstein, the president of a 160-person public relations firm and an adviser to mayors, cardinals and other powerful figures in New York City, as well as Leona M. Helmsley and George M. Steinbrenner 3d. 
 "He's like an octopus," said Beth Rudin de Woody, whose father, the real estate developer Lewis Rudin, is a longtime Rubenstein client. "His tentacles reach out all over politics, entertainment and sports. He's affected so many people's lives in terms of bringing them to fame. Or hiding them from fame, protecting them."
Mr. Rubenstein has been at it for 45 years, ever since he dropped out of Harvard Law School. He has represented nightclub owners and international bookmakers, and charities and colleges. Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani told the crowd that Mr. Rubenstein was the only person in the city who could represent both the Roman Catholic Archdiocese of New York and the Brooklyn Museum of Art.
The Mayor also read a proclamation that called Mr. Rubenstein "the dean of damage control." In the crowd, some of his clients praised his unflappability in handling crises.
"You think yours is the biggest thing in the world," said Tim Zagat, a co-publisher of the Zagat restaurant guides and chairman of NYC and Company, the entity that includes the New York City Convention and Visitors Bureau. "He says, 'This is not nearly as big as the city's financial crisis that I handled in '75.' "
As Mr. Zagat spoke, Mr. Rubenstein was shaking hands with the likes of Leonard A. Lauder, the chairman of the Estee Lauder Companies; Henry J. Stern, the city's Parks Commissioner; the singer Michael Bolton; Cristyne F. Lategano, Mr. Giuliani's former communications director and the president of NYC and Company; the director Mike Nichols and his wife, Diane Sawyer of ABC News; and Raoul Lionel Felder, the divorce lawyer whose mother was Mr. Rubenstein's first client. (She ran a home for the elderly in Brooklyn.)
"One day I made an appointment with Howard, just to talk," said Dr. Ruth Westheimer, the psychologist and sex-talk show host. "While I was there, in the middle of a sentence, he went to the phone. He got a call from a duchess."
That call led to a one-word question that Mr. Rubenstein whispered to one of his vice presidents last night. The question was, "Fergie?"
"I don't have two-way, but I'm told she's coming in," the vice president said.
"Howard! Howard! Howard! Behind you!" another Rubenstein employee all but shouted.
There was Sarah Ferguson, the Duchess of York, who is a spokeswoman for another Rubenstein client, Weight Watchers International. "He has given me back my confidence," she said. http://www.nytimes.com

HEADLINE: Greenspan Urges Japan to Widen Capital Sources

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 27

 BODY:
Alan Greenspan, the Federal Reserve chairman, urged Japan today to develop more vibrant stock and bond markets, saying that the country's over-reliance on its moribund banking system had deepened its economic downturn and impeded its recovery.
Speaking to the annual meeting of the World Bank and the International Monetary Fund, Mr. Greenspan said countries like the United States that had well-developed capital markets as well as strong banking systems weathered global and domestic financial crises far better than those that relied primarily on banks. 
 Mr. Greenspan's remarks covered the experiences of a number of countries, from Thailand -- whose economy was decimated by the market turmoil that swept through Asia in 1997 and 1998 in part because of its reliance on banks for capital -- to Sweden, which withstood an acute domestic banking crisis in the early 1990's because companies there were able to raise money through other sources.
But the Fed chairman's message seemed aimed most directly at Japan. It followed two days of meetings here in which finance ministers and central bankers from the United States and Europe again pressed their counterparts from Tokyo to do more to revive the Japanese economy and clean up the huge overhang of bad loans that has all but paralyzed the country's banking system.
Mr. Greenspan said Japan was the "archetype of virtually bank-only financial intermediation." When the banking system got into trouble, he said, the result was a deep and continuing credit squeeze that has crippled an economy that had been Asia's linchpin.
"The Japanese Government has intervened in the economy and is injecting funds in order to recapitalize the banking system," Mr. Greenspan said. "While it has made some important efforts, it has yet to make significant progress in diversifying the financial system, which arguably could be a key element, although not the only one, in promoting long-term recovery."
Mr. Greenspan also reiterated a message that the United States has long delivered with little effect: that Tokyo will save money in the long run if it stops dithering and acts more forcefully to close failed or failing banks, sell foreclosed real estate and other assets, and create a smaller but healthier banking system.
"Speedy resolution is good, whereas delay can significantly increase the fiscal and economic costs of a crisis," Mr. Greenspan said.
Mr. Greenspan did not address the current state of the American economy or provide any hints as to whether the Fed will raise interest rates when its policy-setting committee meets next week. Analysts say the central bank is increasingly likely to hold rates steady and wait for further evidence as to whether the economy is slowing sufficiently to avert any buildup in inflationary pressures.
Mr. Greenspan also avoided any mention of the Japanese currency. The rise of the yen against the dollar over the last several months prompted the Japanese central bank to make statements this weekend that some took to be hints that it might reverse course and increase the money supply to weaken the currency, a step the United States favors.
Mr. Greenspan, whose speech was titled "Lessons From the Global Crises," said one reason the American economy held up so well last year was that as the bond market seized up in the aftermath of Russia's debt default, the commercial banking system stepped up its lending.
"With the process of credit creation able to continue, the impact on the real economy of the capital market turmoil was blunted," Mr. Greenspan said.
But Fed officials are now increasingly worried that bank lending standards in the United States have grown too lax. Laurence H. Meyer, a Federal Reserve governor, told the Institute of International Bankers in a speech today that while overall loan portfolios remain sound, there has been a modest but troubling increase in questionable lending.
"Lenders are relying too much on the continuation of good times," Mr. Meyer said. "They're assuming a very optimistic view of their borrowers' operating prospects and that their borrowers always will have ready access to the financial markets. And sometimes they're failing to subject loans to meaningful 'stress tests' that would, for instance, tell them if their borrower could withstand an unexpected shock to operating revenue." http://www.nytimes.com

Italian Inquiry Indicates Russian Money Scheme
A two-year criminal investigation has turned up the first concrete evidence that suspected organized crime operators from Russia have funneled millions of dollars through the Bank of New York, prosecutors in Italy said. A1 

Kosovo War Crimes Case
French police in Kosovo notified the families of 26 men missing for five months that the men had been killed by Serbs and dumped in a mass grave. The French have arrested four suspects. The case is the first time that foreigners have completed a war-crimes investigation in Kosovo, working from the first reports of missing people to finding the grave and making arrests. A1
 
Settlers Expand Under Barak
The Government of Prime Minister Ehud Barak of Israel has authorized construction in the West Bank of Jewish settlements at a pace exceeding that of the Netanyahu Administration. On Sunday, Mr. Barak ordered a review of the Housing Ministry, which had authorized the new housing. It was unclear whether the Ministry had authorized the building with Mr. Barak's tacit consent or without his direct knowledge. A16
 
Pinochet Extradition Hearings
Hearings began in London on Spain's effort to extradite Gen. Augusto Pinochet, the former Chilean dictator, to stand trial on torture charges. Chanting and flag-waving supporters of the general outnumbered opponents outside court. A8
 
Ruling on Gay Soldiers in Britain
The European Court of Human Rights declared that Britain's longstanding ban on homosexuals in the military was a violation of the basic human right to privacy. Human rights lawyers said the ruling would probably force Britain to allow openly gay men and women to serve. A8
 
Support for Paying U.N. Dues
Some members of Congress said there was sufficient support on Capitol Hill for paying much of what the United States owes the United Nations. The United States faces losing its General Assembly vote in less than 100 days for not paying its dues. A8

The legislation gives patients the right to sue health insurers for punitive damages and solicit outside reviews of decisions denying them coverage. Managed care providers will have to pay for second opinions on some treatments, cover the testing and treatment of breast cancer, pay for contraceptives and expand coverage for serious mental illnesses. HEADLINE: Transit Strike Threatened Unless Bigger Raises Are Offered

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE 

 BODY:
In an opening salvo before negotiations begin, the president of the transit workers' union is threatening a strike in December if the Metropolitan Transportation Authority does not offer raises larger than those in the current contract.
At a news conference yesterday, Willie James, the president of Local 100 of the Transport Workers Union, said the union wanted the M.T.A. to devote much of its anticipated budget surplus to its 33,000 unionized workers. 
 Backed by a phalanx of city, state and national labor leaders, including John J. Sweeney, the president of the A.F.L.-C.I.O., Mr. James took a tougher line than in the past, partly because many city bus drivers and subway motormen are angry that their recent wage increases have lagged behind the inflation rate.
With the union's current contract expiring on Dec. 15, Mr. James raised the possibility of a strike shortly before Christmas and New Year's Eve, a move that could throw the city into chaos.
"There's definitely a possibility of a strike," he said. "But there's always the possibility of a fair and just settlement without a strike."
The union is scheduled to make its first formal bargaining offer on Oct. 7, with negotiations expected to begin later in the month.
Thomas Kelly, a spokesman for the M.T.A., which is the parent agency of New York City Transit, said, "The M.T.A. would hope a fair and equitable settlement could be reached, balancing the needs of the transit workers with the needs of the riding public."
At the news conference, Mr. James vowed that labor leaders would not let the M.T.A. cry poverty. He said that his members, by accepting concessions in recent contracts, had shared in the city's pain during hard times, but now, he insisted, union members should share in the boom times.
Under their current three-year contract, transit workers -- whose annual salary averages $39,000 -- received a single 3.75 percent across-the-board wage increase, a lump sum averaging $800 and, for the 30 percent of workers with at least 15 years of experience, a supplementary pay increase of $200 to $500.
M.T.A. officials said the transit authority's projected revenues for this year would be $40 million more than anticipated. Mr. James said M.T.A. officials were underestimating the size of the surplus to weaken the union's hand in negotiations.
Mr. Kelly asserted that the transit authority did not have a surplus, but merely $40 million more in revenues than had been projected. He added that the transit system "has always operated on a deficit because the fares and other M.T.A. transit monies only equal about 65 percent of the operating revenues," with additional funds coming from taxes and tolls.
Mr. James declined to disclose bargaining positions, although he said the union would demand "a substantial wage increase."
In May, he called for a 30 percent raise over three years, but yesterday he indicated that his initial bargaining offer would be less than 10 percent a year for three years.
But he added: "My membership is worth double-digit raises. State politicians and city politicians have given themselves double-digit raises. My members do essential work, and so why don't they deserve double-digit raises too?"
Many union leaders joined Mr. James's news conference, partly because the transit contract could set a pattern when City Hall begins bargaining later this year with a coalition of unions representing more than 200,000 municipal employees. "The Transport Workers Union contract would have some influence on city negotiations -- everyone sees it that way," said Randi Weingarten, president of the United Federation of Teachers.
The labor leaders pledged their support to the transit workers partly to strengthen the union's hand in negotiations. Since the state's Taylor Law prohibits transit workers and other municipal unions from striking, they proposed asking all union members in the city to help pay any fines the transit workers might face. The last transit strike was an 11-day walkout in 1980.
Mr. Sweeney, whose father was a bus driver who belonged to Local 100, said that with the city booming, the transit workers should be given a contract that lets them benefit from the good times. http://www.nytimes.com

HEADLINE: The Guggenheim Proposes Gehry Museum for New York

BYLINE: By RALPH BLUMENTHAL with CAROL VOGEL 

 BODY:
The Solomon R. Guggenheim Museum has submitted a proposal to New York City for a dramatic new curvilinear building designed by Frank O. Gehry that would rise as high as 45 stories on a platform over four East River piers near Wall Street.
The project, larger than one the museum had envisioned last year for a West Side pier, has been budgeted at about $850 million and would contain large exhibition wings, a theater, a skating rink and other public amenities, according to an internal financial memorandum and accounts from officials familiar with the plans. More than a third of the cost has already been privately pledged, said the officials, who estimated that the complex could take six years to build. 
 Thomas Krens, the director of the Guggenheim, declined to publicly discuss the proposal, one of nine for the site. The city's Economic Development Corporation is expected to select one within the next few months, but a spokeswoman, Bernadette O'Leary, declined to give detailed descriptions of the proposals, which include a hotel, a cruise-ship dock and an aquarium.
The Guggenheim complex would be substantially larger than the $100 million free-form museum that Mr. Gehry built for the institution in Bilbao, Spain. Opened in 1997, the 250,000-square-foot Bilbao museum with its rakish silhouette and titanium skin has been widely hailed as one of the 20th century's architectural marvels and draws more than 1.4 million visitors annually to the revived Basque riverfront city.
Mr. Gehry fended off questions about the project as premature. "We're studying the way you build a building over the water and need some input from City Planning," he said.
Joseph B. Rose, chairman of the City Planning Commission, said the proposal had not come before the panel and that he could not comment.
A model of the Gehry design had been displayed in Mr. Krens's office. It showed a cloudlike shape out of which the building rose irregularly to a maximum height of about 45 stories. The structure was on a platform above the piers.
The 10-page Guggenheim financial memorandum, sent anonymously to The New York Times and authenticated by the museum, also cites plans for two other Guggenheim projects, an arts and technology center on piers in Brooklyn Heights and exhibit space on Governors Island, which the city has been seeking to redevelop since the United States Coast Guard abandoned the island a year ago.
Judith Cox, a deputy director of the museum to whom the June 24 memorandum was addressed, acknowledged receiving it from its author, Beverly M. Wolff, a museum consultant, but said that Ms. Wolff merely "speculated on the potential development of related sites, including Brooklyn."
Ms. Cox added: "That memo represents purely internal ideas that emerged from a brainstorming session. They were entirely speculative and have not been developed."
Ms. Wolff did not respond to a message left on her home telephone last night.
Mr. Krens has taken the Guggenheim into a vigorous expansionist phase, adding far-flung branches to its core location on Fifth Avenue at 89th Street. Last year he explored building a Gehry-designed museum on Pier 40 on the Hudson River off Houston Street. But other groups had already made recreational proposals for the site, and with city and state officials miffed that the Guggenheim had not cleared its plans with them beforehand, the idea languished.
The new concept for the East River site was submitted to the Economic Development Commission in February in response to a request for proposals inviting bidders interested in developing the site. The property includes three piers: 9, which is decrepit; and 13 and 14, with tennis bubbles on them. A fourth pier, 11, is being retained by the city as a ferry terminal, but would be integrated into the Guggenheim plan.
Museum officials have briefed some outsiders on aspects of the plan on a confidential basis, and yesterday Newsweek published a short account of the project.
Ms. Wolff's memorandum was titled "Naming Opportunities for the Manhattan Projects." While suggesting how big donors might be attracted to a $700 million fund-raising campaign, Ms. Wolff enumerated the features of the Gehry building, which would serve as a largely autonomous branch of the museum's flagship, designed by Frank Lloyd Wright. The Guggenheim already has a SoHo annex, which the museum has periodically considered closing.
In addition to exhibition wings and galleries, she wrote, the building would include a theater that could be sponsored by an entertainment giant like Sony, Samsung or Warner Brothers; a grand entry hall and visitor's center; an arts education center; a sculpture or photography gallery; a film department; a library and study center; an entertainment hall or boardroom, and a members' lounge. The memorandum also cited a new department of architecture and design, suggesting that the Guggenheim might be moving into a field long identified with the Museum of Modern Art. Outside there would be a promenade, sculpture garden, park and skating rink.
The other projects mentioned were a proposed Center for Arts and Technology envisioned for piers 1 through 5 in Brooklyn Heights, and eight "pavilions" on Governors Island, where the Guggenheim would display art.
Ms. Wolff urged the museum to seek three "founders" who together could put up $450 million, just over half the latest anticipated cost. Although the Guggenheim has not approached the city for money, the Museum of Modern Art has been promised $65 million in city money for its $650 million expansion and the Guggenheim could be expected to request the same 10 percent contribution, or about $85 million.
Ms. Wolff argued against seeking to name each project for one large donor. "Rather," she wrote, "I would recommend that serious consideration be given to using generic names for each complex, with naming opportunities relating only to parts of the complex." Thus, she noted, the wings and each gallery within them could be separately named.
She added, "Donors could possibly be enticed by naming the architect if different architects are used, selecting the architect from a list of 'approved architects.' "
Paul Goldstein, the district manager of Community Board 1 in lower Manhattan, said that he had heard only in general that the Guggenheim was a bidder for the site, along with operators of a floating theater and hotel, a youth hostel, a cruise-ship dock, an aquarium and tennis courts, among other uses. The board advises the Manhattan Borough President on planning issues.
"The E.D.C. had told us to expect a decision by June," Mr. Goldstein said. Asked how the community might react to the museum's proposal, Mr. Goldstein said that residents primarily sought parks and open space. "We didn't want any kind of structure more than a few stories," he said. Forty or so stories, he said, was "huge." But he perked up at the mention of Mr. Gehry, whom he called admiringly "that architect with the great reputation."
 http://www.nytimes.com

HEADLINE: WINE TALK;
For Fall, 3 Reds That Won't Break the Bank

BYLINE: By Frank J. Prial 

 BODY:
WINEMAKERS are harvesting their grapes this month, their heads full of ideas for wines that will come to market in a year or so. In the spirit of such forward thinking, what better time to freshen your collection for the coming months? 
 Here are three handsome wines -- reds from Italy, France and California -- that will complement hearty autumn meals. And their merits include their moderate prices.
Antinori Santa Cristina 1998, Tuscany, Italy. About $9.
Once designated a Chianti Classico, Santa Cristina was dropped to a table wine in 1987. This did not mean that Santa Cristina's quality had dropped, but that Chianti Classico's was shooting up, a win-win situation.
The wine, which has been in the Antinori portfolio since 1946, is named for a family vineyard in the Chianti region. The name of the principal grape, sangiovese, was added to the label in 1991, probably because the United States is a good market and Americans are used to seeing grape names on their wines. In 1994, Antinori edged away from the classic Chianti blend by substituting merlot for the canaiolo grape. The blend now is 90 percent sangiovese, 10 percent merlot. This is a simple, straightforward and very tasty wine. It's a good buy and widely available.
Pellegrini Family Vineyards Pinot Noir 1997, Olivet Lane Estate, Russian River Valley, California. About $20.
As evidenced here, it's getting so that the title of some wines is often as long -- if not as boring -- as the description. Actually, this is a good thing. It shows that California winemakers have recognized that the vineyard is paramount, that all the stainless steel gadgetry in a winery doesn't make much difference if the grapes are not first-class.
This wine has two things going for it. First, the grapes come from the Pelligrinis' Olivet Lane Vineyard, which has provided fruit for some spectacular wines, including some memorable Williams & Selyem pinots that regularly turn up at auctions at staggering prices. And it was made by Merry Edwards, a pinot noir expert who knows how to bring the very best out in the fruit.
At 13.8 percent alcohol, this is a big wine, particularly for a pinot noir, but it holds together beautifully and should age well.
Chateau Lagrezette 1996, Cahors, France. About $20.
No one has worked harder to put the wines of Cahors, in southwest France, on the map than Alain-Dominique Perrin, the owner of the magnificent Chateau Lagrezette. Mr. Perrin was for many years president of Cartier, the jewelry house, and currently is head of the Vendome Luxury Group, which includes Cartier, Alfred Dunhill and several other luxury-goods producers.
From his investment over the years in Lagrezette, it's apparent that he maintains the same high standards for his wine as he does for his jewelry. To that end, he consults regularly with Michel Rolland, one of Bordeaux's best-known enologists.
Cahors wines long had a reputation for off-putting intensity. By adding up to 20 percent merlot to the classic Cahors grape, the malbec -- called auxerrois in this part of France -- Mr. Rolland has produced a full-bodied but nicely rounded wine with a pleasant and distinctive bouquet.
AMONG my mail recently was a letter from Ted Conklin, owner and manager of the American Hotel in Sag Harbor, N.Y. Mr. Conklin, who transformed a faded hotel into a popular inn and restaurant, and who also has one of the most sophisticated wine cellars in the country, took issue with a recent column that discussed the many factors that go into the cost of a bottle of wine in a restaurant. The column pointed out that a large majority of whatever the customer pays goes to the restaurateur, with the winemaker often receiving less than 50 cents for a bottle that can cost the consumer 100 times that amount.
Most restaurant owners I talked to said they were merely passing along high costs imposed on them by the distributors, or that high wine prices were the only way to subsidize the costs of food and its preparation.
Mr. Conklin noted that while price-gouging may exist, "restaurants either make prices fair or they fail -- in the best of capitalistic traditions. Competition has already established the fair-market value for wines. This is especially true for restaurants."
He objected to any comparison of the net profit of wineries and a wine's markup in restaurants, because net profits, he said, are calculated after such costs as salaries, land and travel expenses are paid. And wineries, he pointed out, "enjoy significant tax advantages from depreciation and agricultural subsidy."
Restaurants, he continued, "aren't even on the same planet." If a restaurant sells a thousand bottles of wine at a profit of $33.50 a bottle, he noted, "it has $33,500 to pay" for serving the wine "in 3,000 to 4,000 wine glasses, a lot of which break."
"Do you want your readers to believe," he asked, "that restaurateurs should feel guilty about their wine profits and the consumer should sympathize with Robert Mondavi and Ravenswood?"
Actually, neither. But the fact remains that wine prices are appallingly high. More and more money is chasing a small and unchanging amount of fine wine. When the economy cools down and demand drops off, the winemakers will lower their prices; they always do. And restaurateurs like Mr. Conklin will surely follow suit. And all polemics aside, it can't happen soon enough.

HEADLINE: On a New Year's Eve For the Ages, Some Restaurants Will Sit It Out

BYLINE: By ADAM NAGOURNEY 

 BODY:
JO JO, the deluxe Upper East Side restaurant, celebrated New Year's Eve in characteristic style last year: it opened early and kept going until 5 A.M., offering a fixed-price dinner of $500 a head, plus wine. The restaurant was packed, for each of two seatings, as night turned to dawn.
And how will Jo Jo mark the New Year's Eve ushering in the year 2000? By shutting down for the night. The chef, Jean-Georges Vongerichten, is locking up Jo Jo and his four other New York restaurants and flying to Bali to spend his first New Year's Eve out of the kitchen in 27 years. 
 And that is not unusual. While many restaurants are planning extravagantly prepared and stratospherically priced meals to mark the passing of the 1900's, a growing number of New York's best restaurants have quietly decided to stay dark for the night. Gramercy Tavern, Union Pacific, Union Square Cafe, Balthazar, Vong, Mercer Kitchen, Le Bernardin, Eleven Madison, Tabla, Judson Grill, Aquavit, the Oyster Bar at Grand Central Terminal and Verbena are among those turning away people calling for a reservation. Other restaurateurs are considering shutting down on one of the year's most profitable nights, weighing the financial benefits against this year's unusual complications.
The reasons reflect the anxiety felt by New Yorkers in general as they contemplate the approaching New Year's Eve.
Most obviously, there is the fear of rowdiness and drunken revelers harassing customers and employees, especially in restaurants anywhere near Times Square. (The owners of the Local, a plush new restaurant on West 47th Street, are not only closing, but even boarding up the windows to guard against rowdies.) Beyond that, some of New York's most experienced restaurateurs are worried about customer expectations that can never be met, employees who would resent having to work and the promise of high-priced everything -- Champagne, caviar, entertainment -- cutting down the profit margin and stirring the resentment of their regular clientele because of the inevitably huge tabs.
"We foresee a lot of problems with the night," said Chris Cannon, the general manager of Judson Grill, the highly rated restaurant on West 52d Street, in the heart of the district where streets will be closed to traffic. "Our attitude is, how much money can you make in one night? We have a lack of confidence in people getting to the restaurant effectively, about how much business are we going to do and concern for our own staff."
Danny Meyer, the owner of Gramercy Tavern, Union Square Cafe, Eleven Madison and Tabla, said he had decided to close his restaurants after concluding that there was no way he could meet "the overblown expectations" that people were sure to bring in the door. "Somebody is not going to get the best table in the restaurant," Mr. Meyer said. "Someone is not going to like the size of the piece of foie gras and the dollop of caviar or the band you picked.
"It's got to be the best night out that you've ever had in your life, or it's a failure. The evening is going to cost so much money, and people are going to feel so ripped off anyway, so why do it? If we weren't going to make money, if we weren't going to make our guests happy, if we weren't going to make our staff happy, then why open?"
Not surprisingly, word that some restaurateurs are taking the night off (or like Le Cirque 2000, opening but donating the proceeds to charity) is stirring some resentment among those restaurateurs who are looking forward to what has always been one of the biggest moneymaking nights of the year. "The minute they say, 'We're not opening, we want to be fair to our employees,' that is a really unfair thing to do to everyone else," said Drew Nieporent, whose restaurants include Montrachet, Nobu, Layla, TriBeCa Grill and Berkeley Bar and Grill. "Also, to create the charitable thing, I respect that, I really do. But we live by the adage that the show must go on. This is an important day for us. That's the way we pay our bills. If we all wanted to play hooky on a major holiday, what would happen?"
Whatever the reason, closing on New Year's Eve could not have been an easy decision, at least to judge by the menu prices that have been announced so far. Lespinasse is charging $2,500 a person for its late-night dinner, including wine and service. (If that's too pricey, there's a discounted meal of just $1,700 a head, with a more modest selection of wines.)
Dinner at Daniel will be $1,250, plus wine. At Chanterelle, Karen and David Waltuck are planning a black-tie dinner, which will include a wine from each decade of the 20th century, at a cost of around $2,500 a head. "This only happens once in a while, right?" Ms. Waltuck observed. "It's a pretty big nut to crack for our clients. But I figured I should go all out." (So far, reservations can still be booked at most of these high-price restaurants.)
It is also doubtful that any restaurant would have any problem selling out: letting someone else do the work has always been the best way to have fun on New Year's Eve. Sirio Maccioni, the owner of Le Cirque 2000, said that 1,000 people had already called to put themselves on his New Year's Eve waiting list, even though he has yet to announce a single detail about the night, from menu to cost.
But the economic incentives to open are less strong this year than usual. For one, there's the expected high cost of supplies for the night. For another, this has been a good year in the business, which means that owners can afford to take a night off.
"We've had a great year at Aquavit," said Hakan Swahn, the owner of the Scandinavian restaurant in midtown. "I feel the least we can do is say to our staff that worked, 'It's the millennium, enjoy yourself: we'll see you in the next millennium.' "
And the prospective profits are being weighed against a growing concern among business owners in New York, who are beginning to worry about just what the streets of Manhattan will be like that evening. "The level of clientele that we cater to is not going to be frequenting Times Square on New Year's," said Franklin Becker, the chef at the Local on West 47th Street. "The people that are going to be here are going to be transients. They are going to be young kids with ill motives that aren't going to be able to afford our restaurant. Quite frankly, to put our guests at risks or to put anyone at risk, including our employees, for one evening, it just doesn't make any sense for us. We're putting braces behind the windows."
Joe Bastianich, an owner of Babbo, said he had not made a decision on what, if anything, to do. "Is the potential downside worth the potential revenue?" he asked. "The danger to people? I don't know what it's going to be like."
In the end, for all the concern voiced by restaurant owners, there does appear to be an ultimate factor nudging these restaurateurs to spend the night someplace else. After all, this is a millennial moment. Who wants to work?
Mr. Vongerichten, for instance, is designing the menu for a particularly large celebration at the Javits Center but has no plans to be near Manhattan that night. "I never took off a New Year's Eve," he said. "I deserve it. I'm giving it to myself."

HEADLINE: Foreign Affairs;
Give That Man a Cigar

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN 

DATELINE: HAVANA

 BODY:
American foreign policy toward Cuba is really unique among U.S. foreign policies. It isn't just 15 degrees off. It has the distinction of being 360 degrees wrong.
That is, the longstanding U.S. boycott and isolation of Cuba is a failed policy if your desire is to punish Cuba's people so they will oust Fidel Castro, and it is a failed policy if your desire is to ease the pain on Cuba's people while they wait for Mr. Castro to pass away. It is a failed policy if you think we should be preparing for a post-Castro Cuba. And it is a failed policy if you think we should be trying to bring down Mr. Castro today. It is a failed policy if you believe in human rights, and it is a failed policy if you believe only in trade. 
 With Mr. Castro having celebrated his 40th anniversary in power, it's safe to say U.S. policy hasn't worked in any direction. And no wonder. It's not really a policy. It's an attitude -- a blind hunger for revenge against Mr. Castro by a small, exile Cuban-American minority residing largely in Florida, and a blind hunger for campaign contributions from this Cuban minority by U.S. Congressmen.
What would a sensible Cuba policy look like? First, it would acknowledge that the unilateral U.S. boycott of Cuba has not weakened the regime. Cuba can still trade with 160 other countries. Does that mean Cuba's economy is flourishing? No, but not because of our boycott. It isn't flourishing for the same reason that the Soviet economy crumbled -- because Communism doesn't work. Communism is great for producing missiles, but terrible when it comes to breakfast, lunch and dinner. What ails the Cuban economy is the internal embargo here, the self-imposed sanctions that come from Cuba still adhering to failed Marxist economics.
The only thing the U.S. embargo does is obscure that fact and give the regime another excuse for remaining iron-fisted. Mr. Castro argues to his people that they are under siege from America, therefore they have to remain mobilized and can't loosen up.
Make no mistake, this is a repressive regime. It has taught its people to read in record numbers, but won't allow them to read what they want; it has educated its people better than anywhere in the Caribbean, but won't allow them to speak freely; it is a worker's paradise where people still have to live off ration books. But it is a repressive regime led by a charismatic figure who knows how to stay in power. Which is why a wise U.S. policy today would be focused primarily on shaping the post-Castro Cuba, so that we don't have another Haiti -- where, after the regime collapses, everyone tries to build a raft for Miami -- and the Cuban people don't have another 40 years of repression.
Last July the U.S. diplomatic mission here held a lottery for 20,000 immigration visas to the U.S. It received in 30 days 541,000 applications. That meant that roughly one-tenth of the total eligible population of Cuba applied to leave. Imagine the scramble to get out if Mr. Castro suddenly goes. There are no trade unions here, no free non-governmental organizations, no business groups -- none of the elements of civil society that hold a country in place. It is Haiti waiting to happen, only closer to our shores.
By not helping Cuba develop a transition system that can lead to a soft landing after Mr. Castro (who is 73), we are insuring a hard landing. Our best tool for shaping post-Castro Cuba is engaging with Cuba now: letting more Cubans study in the U.S., training their technocrats to run a modern state, and, yes, opening the way for some U.S. investment and trade.
Sure, in the short term Mr. Castro would use any openness to his advantage. But he's using our boycott to his advantage already. The difference is what U.S. engagement with Cuba would produce in the long run. At a minimum it would put pressure on Mr. Castro to ease the human rights abuses, by making it much more difficult for him to justify continued repression. And at a maximum it would help lay the foundations of a Cuban economy that could thrive in the future, and insure that whoever succeeds Mr. Castro has to be more open.
Everywhere you turn here you see American influence -- despite the boycott. They love our Chevys, they love American dollars, they love American baseball, and they love American democracy. American values permeate this place without even trying. Imagine what would happen if we actually tried.

HEADLINE: Supreme Court Roundup;
Justices to Rule on Right of Women to Sue Their Attackers

BYLINE: By LINDA GREENHOUSE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 28

 BODY:
Taking up a case with broad implications for its continuing reappraisal of Federal power, the Supreme Court agreed today to decide whether Congress had the authority to open the Federal courts to civil lawsuits by victims of rape, domestic violence and other crimes "motivated by gender."
At stake is the fate of a 1994 law, the Violence Against Women Act, which Congress passed after concluding, on the basis of four years of hearings, that state court systems were not dealing adequately with an epidemic of violence against women. The law created a new Federal civil remedy, permitting victims to sue their attackers for damages, including punitive damages. 
 In a ruling last March, the Federal appeals court in Richmond declared the law unconstitutional and dismissed a suit brought by a Virginia college student against two fellow students, football players who she contended had raped her in her dormitory room shortly after the start of her freshman year in 1994.
The suit, filed by Christy Brzonkala, was the first to be brought under the law's civil damages provision, and has been watched closely on its journey through the courts. The two men defended against the suit by arguing that the civil damages section of the law exceeded Congress's authority to assert Federal jurisdiction over matters of traditional state concern. One defendant was eventually punished by Virginia Polytechnic Institute and State University with a deferred suspension that let him complete his college football career; the university's judicial committee took no action against the other.
The decision by the United States Court of Appeals for the Fourth Circuit striking down the law was cast in very broad terms, with Judge J. Michael Luttig, writing for the 7-to-4 majority in a 168-page opinion, declaring that the Violence Against Women Act "simply cannot be reconciled with the principles of limited Federal government upon which this nation is founded."
The ruling held that because violence against women had only "an attenuated and indirect relationship with interstate commerce," the law was not a proper exercise of Congressional authority under the Constitution's commerce clause. Nor was the law a valid exercise of Congress's authority to enforce the equal protection guarantee of the 14th Amendment, the Fourth Circuit held, because the law sought to regulate purely private conduct rather than the state action at which the 14th Amendment is directed.
Both these broad propositions place the case in the center of the Court's continuing debate over defining the boundary between Federal and state authority. In a series of decisions over the last four years, culminating in June with three handed down on the final day of the Court's term, the Court invoked principles of federalism and sovereign immunity to circumscribe the power of Congress to identify problems in need of uniform national solutions.
The Clinton Administration appealed the Fourth Circuit decision, as did the NOW Legal Defense and Education Fund, which has represented Ms. Brzonkala in the case. The Justices combined the two appeals, United States v. Morrison, No. 99-5, and Brzonkala v. Morrison, No. 99-29, and will hear the case in January. The Court had already scheduled arguments in three other federalism cases in its new term, which officially begins on Monday.
Both halves of the Fourth Circuit's analysis have implications beyond the specific law at issue. On the question of Congress's power to regulate interstate commerce, the appeals court relied on the Supreme Court's ruling in a 1995 case, United States v. Lopez, which invalidated a Federal law that made it a crime to carry a gun near a school. In that case, the Justices said that the activity involved did not have a "substantial relation to interstate commerce."
In passing the Violence Against Women Act, Congress found that such violence did affect the economy by, for example, limiting women's participation in the labor force. However, the Fourth Circuit rejected those findings, raising the question of the extent to which Federal courts should defer to the rationale offered by Congress for its actions.
The Fourth Circuit's limitations on Congressional authority to legislate under the 14th Amendment raise questions about the validity of civil rights laws enacted under that authority, since those also apply to actions by private individuals who are not "state actors." The Clinton Administration is arguing that because the states themselves have failed to address the problem of violence against women, the issue is one of official behavior and not simply of private conduct.
While the Court ordinarily waits to review a case until a disagreement has developed among the Federal appeals courts, the Justices acted today in the absence of such a conflict. The Fourth Circuit was the first appeals court to decide the constitutionality of the act, although the law has been upheld by 14 Federal District Courts.
The law is familiar to the Justices, although they have not ruled on it. Chief Justice William H. Rehnquist criticized it before and after its enactment, singling it out in speeches on the new burdens that Congress was placing on the Federal courts.
This case was one of nine new appeals the Court accepted today. The cases, which will be argued in January, bring the number on the Court's calendar to 44, well over half the cases the Justices are likely to decide over the nine-month term.
These were among the other new cases:
 
Grandparents' Rights
The Court agreed to decide the constitutionality of a Washington state law giving grandparents and other "third parties" the opportunity to petition a court for visitation rights "when visitation may serve the best interest of the child."
The Washington Supreme Court, in a ruling last December, declared the law unconstitutional as violating the fundamental rights of parents not to have the state interfere with their child-rearing decisions.
Only where "the state seeks to prevent harm or a risk of harm to the child" can the parents' fundamental right be overcome, the state court said, adding that "parents should be the ones to choose whether to expose their children to certain people or ideas." Even when there is a plausible argument that continued contact with grandparents or other relatives, following a divorce or the death of one parent, would be beneficial to the child, "state intervention to better a child's quality of life through third-party visitation is not justified where the child's circumstances are otherwise satisfactory," the Washington court said.
The case, Troxel v. Granville, No. 99-138, is an appeal by the parents of a man who committed suicide after fathering two daughters by a woman to whom he was not married. After the father's death, the mother married a man who adopted the girls. The mother and her husband then sought to limit the girls' contact with their paternal grandparents, who invoked the state law and won regular visitation rights under a decision that the Washington Supreme Court then overturned.
Most states have laws permitting grandparents and others to seek visitation rights, and the state courts are split over the laws' constitutionality.
 
Abortion Protests
The Court agreed to hear a challenge to a Colorado law making it a crime to approach another person within a distance of eight feet, while in the vicinity of a health care facility, for the purpose of distributing leaflets, displaying signs or engaging in conversation unless invited to do so. The challenge was brought by three anti-abortion protesters who travel to abortion clinics around Colorado for what they call "sidewalk counseling," trying to dissuade patients from entering the clinics for their scheduled abortions.
A Supreme Court decision two years ago, in a case from Buffalo, invalidated on First Amendment grounds a portion of a court injunction that had imposed such a "floating buffer zone" to shield abortion clinic patients from protesters.
