HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: BONDS;
30-Year Issue Leads Sharp Fall in Treasuries

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
The prices of Treasury securities fell sharply yesterday, raising the yield on the long bond to its highest level in several days and representing the sharpest one-day rise in more than two weeks.
Analysts noted a variety of factors contributing to the bearish tone and selloff, including selling by funds and dealers, supply, technical factors and a surge in the prices of gold and other commodities. 
 Even weakness in the Dow Jones industrial stock average, which was down more than 200 points at one point in the day, was unable to provide much sustained support. Traders also suggested that the supply of new debt had a hand in downward price pressures. Buying was limited as dealers set up hedges in advance of Fannie Mae's $5.3 billion bond sale.
In addition to drawing investors away from Treasury securities, government bonds can also fall during large debt sales because dealers often sell their most liquid assets, like Treasuries, as a hedge against losses should the market take a downturn before the deal is done.
The 30-year Treasury fell a full point yesterday, to 100 19/32, while its yield, which moves in the opposite direction, rose to 6.08 percent, from 6.01 percent on Monday.
The two-part issue from Fannie Mae, the nation's largest buyer of home loans, drew enough demand that Fannie Mae was able to increase its planned sale by more than 30 percent. The five-year notes were priced to yield about 59 basis points more than a Treasury security with a similar maturity and the 30-year bond was priced to yield more than 81 basis points over the Government bond. A basis point is one-hundredth of a percentage point.
Dan Antonellis, market strategist for Briefing.com in Burlingame, Calif., said yesterday's Treasury market was driven by factors that included the surge in gold prices, which affected the longer maturities, as well as rumors of hedge-fund selling on the long end of the market.
Other metals, in addition to gold, also climbed, stoking investor fears that rising prices would also increase inflation and force the Fed into action.
"There was decent bid to the front end, or shorter maturities in the market due to the weakness in stocks, as investors put a premium on shorter issues," Mr. Antonellis said. "But the flight to quality today has not been enough to keep the bond market in positive territory because the market has been burned on so many occasions this year."
The Treasury plans to sell $15 billion of new two-year notes today. In when-issued trading late yesterday, the notes were offered at a price to yield 5.61 percent.

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
White House and I.M.F. Give Ecuador the Cold Shoulder

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 28

 BODY:
For five years now the Clinton Administration has gone to extraordinary lengths to keep countries from defaulting on loans to investors around the world, fearing that national bankruptcy can set off political upheaval and international economic chaos.
That is why the White House bailed out Mexico in 1995, and why Washington moved the financial heavens to rescue Thailand and South Korea (successfully) and Indonesia and Russia (not exactly victories for American-style capitalism). 
 So it was more than a little curious that when tiny Ecuador, hardly a strategic player in the post-cold-war world, finally drove off a financial cliff this week, there was no one extending a net to cushion the fall. Inside the United States Treasury, the central command post in this era of global economic diplomacy, the message was that Ecuador got its just deserts. For months it has been ignoring warnings that it could no longer delay some enormously tough political choices. Instead, it declared it could not -- and would not -- pay many of the investors who had bought the country's Brady bonds.
That was exactly the message the new Treasury Secretary, Lawrence H. Summers, wanted to send to those investors. Since the Asian economic crisis ebbed, Mr. Summers has warned over and over again about the dangers of complacency, and said the United States and the International Monetary Fund would not always step in to rescue countries or their lenders. Finally, he proved it.
"Sooner or later, investors are going to discover that the world isn't always going to step in to stop a default," one senior Administration official said. "And if they understand it sooner, it might prevent a bigger crisis."
Much has changed since the last time the world's finance ministers and central bankers clogged Washington with their limousines for the annual meetings of the I.M.F. and World Bank. Back then the world seemed on the precipice of economic disaster. Russia had just devalued its currency, Wall Street was swooning, hedge funds were going under and the Federal Reserve was cutting interest rates to breathe life back into the world economy.
This year, those finance ministers and central bankers, bellying up to the appetizers at receptions all over the city, are congratulating themselves for the wisdom of their world-saving chess moves last year. The Governor of the Bank of France talked this morning about handing out "coups de chapeau" -- tips of the hat -- to Asia and to the I.M.F. and World Bank. Several of his colleagues from other countries talked about last year in the giddy terms of survivors of a plane crash.
All this has Mr. Summers worried, and at every opportunity he warns against the dangers of complacency and says the biggest thing the world should fear is "the lack of fear itself." So Ecuador was a chance to create a little fear in the markets.
 
China Clams Up
As the ministers toasted each other, the Chinese dithered and danced. On Monday, China's negotiator over the country's entry into the World Trade Organization, Shi Guangsheng, arrived in Washington, met with Charlene Barshefsky, the United States trade representative, and offered -- well, absolutely nothing new. Then he flew back to Beijing, in time for the 50th anniversary of the People's Republic.
Ms. Barshefsky and her colleagues in the Administration are clearly mystified. For the last few months they have assumed that China's reluctance to negotiate on the terms of its entry to the group -- a deal nearly completed in April, until President Clinton walked away -- was part of some great tactical plan hatched in Beijing.
The theory was that the Chinese, by waiting, were hoping to translate the American guilt over the bombing of their embassy in Belgrade into easier terms for entering the W.T.O. They wanted Mr. Clinton to ask China publicly to come back to the table -- exactly what he did two weeks ago in New Zealand, at a meeting with President Jiang Zemin. The meeting here was supposed to make progress.
It didn't.
Now some in the Administration wonder if Ms. Barshefsky's decision to publish a list of China's concessions in April was what one senior White House official calls "a huge mistake." Clearly it made Prime Minister Zhu Rongji vulnerable to conservative forces who want to kill any effort to allow foreign competitors equal footing in China. The question is whether those conservatives have the upper hand now, and that China is no longer willing to open up.
"Anybody who tells you they understand the Chinese strategy here isn't telling you the truth," one official deeply involved in the negotiations said today. "In fact, we can't figure out whether the Chinese know themselves what they want to do."

Going to Bat for Russia
Neither Congress nor the White House is in any mood these days to give financial aid to the money-laundering Russians, or the corruption-ridden Indonesians, especially after the bloodshed of East Timor. Today the head of the I.M.F., Michel Camdessus, had a pretty clear response: Get over it. Some countries are too big to cut off.
Speaking about Russia at the opening session of the I.M.F. today, Mr. Camdessus said, "it would be the height of irresponsibility to turn our back on this great nation; we will not do that." He urged that all the allegations of misappropriation of funds should be weighed against seven years of "real progress," and added that no one should "ignore the fundamental decision, on which Russia has not wavered, to seek to develop a modern market economy and integrate itself into the international community."
He was somewhat less enthused about the progress of change in Indonesia, but clearly was anticipating that the election of a new president would free up the I.M.F.'s coffers once again. And he talked more about rebuilding East Timor than rebuilding Ecuador.

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: ADVERTISING;
At Atlantic Monthly, a Tense Staff Sizes Up the New Owner

BYLINE: By ALEX KUCZYNSKI 

 BODY:
The Atlantic Monthly in Boston on Monday afternoon, staff members huddled around to hear the grim news.
There was the crackle of interstate static. Then, the disembodied voice of Mortimer B. Zuckerman, the New York real estate baron who has owned the magazine since 1980, wafted tinnily through the room: he was selling the magazine, a 142-year-old cultural institution that printed the first words of Emerson and Thoreau. 
 The news did not go over well -- partly because it was delivered from Mr. Zuckerman via speakerphone, "which was truly a postmodern experience," one staff member said with a sniff. More important, though, the staff was upset to learn that William Whitworth, the editor who has steered the magazine for two decades, would be replaced by someone unknown to them, a Washington editorial upstart, Michael Kelly.
But the tension was eased considerably by the soothing presence of the new owner of the barely profitable monthly. He is David G. Bradley, chairman of a little-known research company, the Advisory Board, and a little-known media company, the National Journal Group, both based in Washington. A self-effacing multimillionaire, Mr. Bradley, 46, speaks in tones so dulcet he sounds more like a sympathetic psychotherapist -- or the sweet-natured Mr. Rogers of television fame, perhaps -- than the ferocious new media mogul on the block.
When he arrived at the magazine's headquarters yesterday to confront a tearful staff at a noontime meeting, Mr. Bradley smoothed ruffled feathers with such expertise and assurance that the managing editor, Cullen Murphy, said employees who had been angry or fearful were suddenly smiling. Mr. Bradley promised not to change the editorial direction of the magazine; he also said he had no immediate plans to change the makeup of the staff or move the offices to Washington. "Everyone felt a lot better after the meeting," Mr. Murphy said said. "I did not expect that."
At a time when most magazines that purport to address any intellectual subject -- The New Republic, The Nation, Harper's, The Atlantic Monthly -- are either supported by foundations or eke out a small profit, Mr. Bradley may turn out to be the cowboy in the white 10-gallon hat. If he can turn The Atlantic Monthly into a profitable venture that retains its original sense of mission -- delivering poetry, criticism and literature along with provocative international and political reporting, a mixture that has earned it nine prestigious National Magazine Awards in two decades -- he may prove that magazines with an intellectual bent have a chance of economic survival.
Under Mr. Zuckerman's watch, an executive who works for his real estate company said, The Atlantic Monthly typically lost $500,000 in a bad year and earned $500,000 in a good year. But Mr. Bradley, who with a law degree from Georgetown University and an M.B.A. from Harvard is at his core a businessman, did not spend $17 million -- the figure cited by executives on both sides of the transaction -- to produce a magazine that loses money.
Mr. Bradley seemed an unlikely candidate for media stardom until a few years ago. The Advisory Board, the company he founded in 1979 -- equipped with four card tables and four Princess telephones, in his mother's Watergate apartment -- is, in Mr. Bradley's words, "a for-profit think tank." The company does research for 3,000 American corporations in fields ranging from banking to universities to health care.
It was his purchase of the National Journal Group from the Times Mirror Company in 1997 that transformed his growing empire from one of policy research to one of politics and media savvy.
Among the publications it owns are National Journal, a weekly magazine of government and politics that has won two National Magazine Awards; Government Executive, a monthly about government management; The Almanac of American Politics; The Hotline, a daily Web-based publication covering political reporting, and Congress Daily, a daily report from Capitol Hill.
National Journal, with a circulation of 7,000 and an annual subscription price of $1,047, has a tiny audience. But since Mr. Bradley raised the editorial budget by 50 percent and hired Mr. Kelly, the deposed editor of The New Republic, to be editor in chief, National Journal has attracted well-known writers like the legal analyst Stuart Taylor; William Powers, who had been a press critic for The Washington Post, and Carl Cannon, a former White House correspondent for The Baltimore Sun.
"It's a safe test of entering into the magazine field," Mr. Bradley said, speaking of National Journal. "It's got a significant but limited readership. So you can make mistakes and people will still be forgiving. But how do you step forward into a much more frightening space? The Atlantic Monthly is a retail magazine where you're not allowed to charge a thousand dollars for a subscription and when you make mistakes you will be a source of joy to people who like to read about stumbling."
National Journal had one such moment in April, when Mr. Taylor found himself in a conflict-of-interest battle after it was disclosed that he had considered going to work for Kenneth W. Starr, the independent counsel. Mr. Taylor made apologies on every talk show in existence, turning his stumble into an unwitting public-relations coup for the magazine.
In February, Mr. Bradley took part of his company public, netting him $142 million. With his status as multimillionaire, and with the National Journal Group and The Atlantic Monthly under his belt, he has assured his spot in media moguldom.
"Goodness!" he shrieked in a telephone interview yesterday. "How can you say that? What is fair to say is that I have increased my share of interests in magazines. But look, Henry Luce started Time when he was in his early 20's. I'm 46." So? "I think you need to start younger."
Mr. Kelly, who will be editor in chief of The Atlantic Monthly, said he had no plans to change anything. "Making a business out of creating and selling a great magazine -- a magazine that consciously exists on a pretty high plane and does not dumb itself down or tart itself up -- is a pretty tough row," he said. "The identity of The Atlantic Monthly is about literature and about journalism that aspires to the level of literature. It is about the serious, untrendy, un-buzz-driven discussion of ideas. All of those things have to continue. That is what the magazine is."
Alas, the world of magazines depends for much of its revenue on the world of advertising -- an industry known for its love of that mysterious blend of public relations and cocktail-party chatter. Alan Jurmain, the media partner at Lowe & Partners/ SMS, said: "You need some form of buzz. You'd like buzz to be something that comes from within, that is authentic, and I think they have a terrific product but they haven't had the wind at their back for a long time. If this guy can create a breeze, they can be on their way."
Mr. Kelly said he would begin spending a lot of time with the magazine's staff in Boston. As for Mr. Bradley, the staff of The Atlantic Monthly is still trying to figure out just who he is.
"Isn't there a novelist named David Bradley?" said Mr. Murphy, the managing editor. "I guess that just shows where my head is at."

HEADLINE: McCain Outlines Voucher Program and How to Pay for It

BYLINE: By LESLIE WAYNE 

DATELINE: MOUNT PLEASANT, S.C., Sept. 28

 BODY:
Senator John McCain, now a full-fledged candidate for the Presidency, called today for a school voucher program that he said should be financed by cutting Federal subsidies on oil, gas, ethanol and sugar.
With Charleston Harbor and the carrier Yorktown serving as a backdrop to highlight his Vietnam War record, Mr. McCain used the occasion to link for the first time two of the major themes of his campaign, fighting special interests and providing greater school choice, and to underscore his image as war hero. 
 "Our children deserve the best education that we can make available to them, whether that learning takes place in a public, private or parochial school," Mr. McCain told an audience filled with veterans in V.F.W. caps and military metals.
"We will find the necessary money for those most in need, by taking it from those least in need," he added. "We give hundreds of millions of dollars in subsidies to the producers of ethanol, to oil and gas and sugar manufacturers who are wealthy beyond the dreams of most hard-working Americans. I challenge my opponents to join me in taking the money that we've been giving to the special interests and spend it instead on a child in need."
Mr. McCain coupled his attack on subsidies with his long-standing crusade for campaign finance reform. In his mind, reducing the influence of money in politics would reduce the pressure on Congress to finance pet projects of major contributors.
Last summer, Mr. McCain introduced a bill to finance school vouchers by cutbacks in the ethanol subsidy program, only to have it defeated in the Senate.
Today, he added oil and gas and sugar subsidies to the list of possible financing sources for school vouchers, a program favored by Republicans.
While Mr. McCain's talk was about schools and special interests, the day was also heavy in symbols of the military theme that runs through Mr. McCain's campaign: As a Navy pilot, he was a prisoner of war for five and a half years.
The setting here, the Patriots Point Naval and Maritime Museum, highlighted not only his own military history but also one that goes back three generations in his family.
The Yorktown was part of the carrier force that was commanded by Mr. McCain's grandfather, Admiral John S. McCain, and on its flight deck it carried a replica of the A-4 Skyhawk that Mr. McCain was flying when he was shot down in 1967. Men in "Comrades for McCain" T-shirts populated the crowd, and a submarine, a destroyer and a Coast Guard cutter added to the backdrop. And, as Mr. McCain spoke, a parade of military aircraft flew overhead, en route to the nearby airport.
In his speech here, Mr. McCain restated much of what he said on Monday, when he announced his candidacy in New Hampshire: Calls for higher defense spending, denunciations of economic protectionism, and stressing the need for having had "broad and deep experience" when making the "life and death decisions" as President to send troops into combat.
And while these themes produced hearty applause from the crowd of 500, the focus of the day was on Mr. McCain's plan for financing the voucher program by cutting subsidies to the ethanol, oil and gas and sugar industries. His campaign against ethanol subsidies is one reason he steered clear of the Iowa straw poll, where much of it is produced, and his attacks on oil and gas interests hit an industry that is a leading contributor to the campaign of Gov. George W. Bush of Texas.
Mr. McCain said the "biggest challenge" is to finance a voucher program, allowing students to switch to private or parochial schools, while not cutting government support to the public school system. And he said the money could be found if campaign finance reform were enacted and subsidies cut.
"Let's take that money, and instead of using it to pay off campaign contributors with special tax breaks, let's spend it on the children who have been trapped in our worst schools," Mr. McCain said. "Let's take that money, and use it to set children free, to give them an educational opportunity that can provide them with a real future."
Still, what many in the crowd said was Mr. McCain's uppermost appeal was his status as a war hero and his call for less spending on high-cost weapons systems and more spending on enlisted personnel and veterans. That Mr. McCain suffered greatly while in prison, and emerged to become a member of the Senate, was enough for many here to believe that he has the character and leadership to run the nation.
Michael Burkhold, a money manager from Charleston, said, "It's important to me that John McCain is a hero. It's important to me that we dignify the office of the President with a person who has the status of being a hero."
And Will Buddin, a real estate agent from Summerville, said he felt Mr. McCain's war experiences meant that "he can give leadership back to this country."
"It's not just giving us economic strength," Mr. Buddin added, "but for the world to look to us as a power to emulate, not to denigrate."

SECTION: Section B; Page 1; Column 2; Metropolitan Desk 

LENGTH: 1536 words

HEADLINE: Suburbia Learns It Has Paved Over the Natural Defenses to Flooding

BYLINE: By JANE GROSS with ROBERT HANLEY 

 BODY:
When the torrential rain and surging rivers from Tropical Storm Floyd drove thousands from their homes and knocked out essential infrastructure in New Jersey and the lower Hudson Valley, suburbanites there got a terrifying look at what can happen when rapid development compromises Mother Nature's fundamental system of flood control.
But as officials in a dozen devastated counties scramble to draw lessons from the recent storm, which produced some of the worst damage in the metropolitan region this century, they are seeing two different pictures, one on each side of the Hudson River. 
 In low-lying New Jersey, the worst destruction took place in known flood plains, leading to quick and unambiguous plans to redesign or relocate crippled telecommunications centers in high-risk areas and push forward with a stalled Federal flood control project that should protect communities on the Raritan River.
In hilly Westchester, Rockland and Putnam Counties, by contrast, the damage was more random, often the result of aging storm drainage systems, which were overwhelmed by swollen streams and brooks. But the solutions are murkier than in the New Jersey flood plain, since the culverts and storm drains that exploded like water cannons in New York's northern suburbs have performed reliably under less extreme circumstances and would not necessarily be the same ones to fail in a comparable storm.
Communities on both sides of the river shared a wake-up call about the hidden costs of development, particularly New Jersey's commercial building boom of the last two decades. Floyd reminded them, or showed them for the first time, how paving what was once green with malls, office parks and new residential communities makes the ground less porous in times of flood. And the storm brought home the importance of regional planning in communities accustomed to going their own way.
Residential development is nothing new in these parts, said James W. Hughes, dean of the School of Planning and Public Policy at Rutgers University. But the 1980's and 1990's have produced both accelerated home-building and the proliferation of office parks, he said.
"More of the region is at risk as we spread the economy out," Dean Hughes said, noting that New Jersey has seen an 80 percent increase in office inventory since the 1980's, or 110 million square feet. That is equal to the entire office inventory in Houston, he said.
The deluge that accompanied Tropical Storm Floyd would have caused problems under any circumstances. In New Jersey, according to the National Hurricane Center, a downpour of those dimensions can be expected once ever 200 years and in the Lower Hudson Valley once every 100 years. No estimate of total damage is available.
"It came like a tidal wave," said Mayor Frank J. Gilly of Bound Brook, N.J., a 74-year-old resident of the region's hardest-hit community. "I've never seen anything like it."
Bound Brook, where the Raritan River crested at 41 feet, was a predictable trouble spot, sitting in the flood plain, hammered to a lesser extent by earlier storms and plagued by increasing run-off problems due to intensive development since the late 1980's in western Somerset County. "We just can't force another town to have a moratorium on construction," Mayor Gilly said. "But we catch all the drainage."
The Army Corps of Engineers had begun planning for a flood control system of dikes and berms after a severe storm in 1971. But the project was stalled by environmental opposition and political infighting. As it happens, the disputes were resolved this year, the permits issued and the work scheduled to begin in the spring. Mayor Gilly estimated that the dikes and berms would have held back about 80 percent of the water.
Also in a flood plain and thus a sure bet for damage was a Bell Atlantic telecommunications hub in Rochelle Park, a quarter-mile from New Jersey's Saddle River. It was inundated, burying critical wiring in the basement under six feet of water and knocking out telephone service to about a million local customers and to 8,000 automated teller machines throughout the country. Flood waters also swamped an adjacent AT&T building that handles wireless calls for parts of New Jersey, New York and Connecticut.
Both buildings went up before the area was declared a flood plain by the Federal Government. After that declaration, Bell Atlantic built a two-and-a-half-foot concrete berm at the riverside of the building and then added a five-foot bank of dirt as Floyd bore down. AT&T, for its part, stopped putting up new buildings in flood plains and decided the equipment in Rochelle Park was too heavy to move upstairs safely.
Both companies made quick reassessments after Floyd. Bell Atlantic executives announced that all power equipment would be moved to the upper floors of the eight-story building. AT&T executives said they would move all switching equipment to other locations in the metropolitan area, out of the flood plain.
Also in the Raritan flood plain is the 68-year-old Elizabethtown Water Company's treatment plant, it, too, constructed before the Federal designation but secured by 40-foot dirt berms after the damaging storm in 1971. Three years ago, a sister plant was built across the river on high ground, which saved the center of the state from a total water shutdown. The new plant, untouched by Floyd's flooding, will be enlarged and take on the lion's share of pumping.
In the lower Hudson Valley, Hillsides slid atop houses in Katonah; Piermont was buried in mudslides, and roads collapsed in Cortland. Seven Bridges Road in New Castle is now Two Bridges Road. And throughout the region, there were evacuations, downed power lines, interrupted telephone service, closed schools, flooded basements and transportation disruptions.
In Westchester, Rockland and Putnam Counties, trouble spots were less predictable than in New Jersey's flood plains and solutions less obvious. "Most of our communities survive most storms," said Anthony Landi, Westchester's Commissioner of Public Works and Environmental Facilities. "Redesigning entire systems -- I don't think you'll see that. Where do you stop? How much money do you spend?"
Home rule in New York makes each municipality responsible for its own storm drainage systems, thus work is done piecemeal, or not at all. "It's not like New York City, where Guiliani can raise his hand and part the waters," said Susan Tolchin, a spokeswoman for Andrew J. Spano, the Westchester County Executive.
In Hastings-on-Hudson, for instance, a village of 8,000, faulty storm drains were being upgraded one at a time and some were ready for Floyd and some not. Neil P. Hess, the village manager, said it would cost $36 million to upgrade all the storm sewers on 35 miles of streets. But even if the taxpayers were willing, he said: "It just doesn't pay because then we'd have to assume everybody else does the same thing. If we do it and Yonkers doesn't, for instance, we'll still wind up with flooding conditions because the systems are interconnected."
Rockland hopes to find a countywide solution by taking control of all 550 miles of local streams, according to Scott Vanderhoef, the County Executive. This would enable the county to exert more influence on development adjacent to streams and also conduct intermunicipal repair and maintenance of storm sewers, culverts and the like.
"We must insure that the region thinks regionally," Mr. Vanderhoef said. "A piece at a time doesn't do the trick."
Thanks to Floyd's rampage, help for expensive hydrology projects is now on the way from the Federal Emergency Management Agency, which this week announced that the 12 counties in the region that have qualified for emergency aid would also be eligible for Federal funds to bring drainage systems, roads and the like up to modern standards, with 75 percent of the cost of approved projects coming from Washington.
Ossining, where homes, roads, businesses, and a public works garage were washed away, has already hired an engineering firm to "examine the hydrology of each failure," according to O. Paul Shaw, the village manager.
The closest thing to a flood plain in Westchester is its north-south ribbons of rivers and parallel roadways, with their aqueous names, like the Saw Mill River, Bronx River, Hutchinson River and Sprain Brook Parkways, that trace the natural downhill slope of the county. These rivers and their many nameless tributaries are known to spill their banks, and residents in the vicinity are used to flooded roadways and basements, though usually to the depth of a couple of inches rather than several feet.
One solution tried elsewhere is not an option.
After the massive Mississippi River floods of 1993, the Federal Government, the builder of dikes and insurer of last resort, persuaded at least one flooded town to relocate, at Government expense, to higher ground.
Could this be Bound Brook's future, Mr. Hughes, the planning expert at Rutgers, was asked? Impossible, was his reply. The Government would never offer buyouts where homes cost $250,000, not $25,000, he said. Plus, where could you move a town in America's most densely populated state?

HEADLINE: G.M. Agrees To 4-Year Pact With Union

BYLINE: By ROBYN MEREDITH 

DATELINE: DETROIT, Sept. 28

 BODY:
The General Motors Corporation and the United Automobile Workers union reached agreement on a new, four-year labor contract late tonight that would give the company's 143,200 factory workers annual raises of 3 percent plus adjustments for inflation.
The union and the company jointly announced the agreement, but declined to comment further. 
 The deal is significant for workers, of course, because of the hefty raises. But it is also notable because it might signal G.M. has turned the corner in its previously frosty relations with the union. The agreement came without a strike, and without the customary saber-rattling from a frustrated union struggling to get the company's attention at top levels. Last year, G.M. lost $2 billion because of a 54-day strike, its 17th strike since 1993. This year, both the company and the union seemed eager to avoid a repeat of the experience.
"It is clear that G.M. is trying much harder to work with the union," said John A. Casesa, an analyst at Merrill Lynch & Company. "G.M.'s position reflects a genuine desire to pursue a path of cooperation rather than confrontation and an unwillingness to take the market share loss that would come with a strike."
The agreement comes as the auto industry is selling vehicles nearly as fast as it can build them and earning huge profits, which it does not want to interrupt.
The terms of the G.M. pact mirror those negotiated by the DaimlerChrysler Corporation, which reached agreement with the union Sept. 16. The union said Monday that DaimlerChrysler workers approved the agreement by a margin of 86 percent. Later this month, G.M. workers are expected to vote to ratify the contract language the union and the company settled on tonight, which, like the DaimlerChrysler agreement, also provides for a $1,350 signing bonus.
"It is an expensive contract, but given the fact that the companies are making record sales and earnings and have a strong cash flow, it is hard for them to plead poverty," said David B. Healy, an analyst at Burnham Securities.
Stephen P. Yokich, the union president, said last week that this year's labor contracts would allow workers to share in the prosperity of their companies, "It is our turn now that things are good," he said after briefing local union leaders on the deal reached with DaimlerChrysler.
"This is a good contract," he said of that agreement, which has the same principal terms as the one reached with General Motors tonight.
Mr. Yokich personally negotiated with G.M.'s chairman, John F. Smith Jr., in the last three days to reach the deal, company officials said.
The union is expected to turn its attention later this week to the Ford Motor Company. Three-year labor agreements at the three companies expired Sept. 14, but have been extended as the union negotiations continue. The new, four-year contracts cover 400,000 factory workers at the three companies.
Ford is likely to make a similar deal with the union, but negotiations may not go as smoothly as they have at DaimlerChrysler and G.M. The reason is that workers building auto parts at Ford's Visteon subsidiary are nervous that their jobs and wages will be less secure if Ford follows through on its plans to sell Visteon.
According to the union, DaimlerChrysler agreed to language in the contract barring sales or spinoffs of assets, which had little impact on DaimlerChrysler because it had no immediate plans to sell chunks of the company. But because the union typically reaches agreement with one company, then forces the others to accept the same terms, the clause may make it more difficult, or more expensive, for Ford to reach a deal with the union.
"The strong anti-spinoff language in the DaimlerChrysler contract is a real problem," Mr. Healy said. "I don't know how they are going to solve it."
One possibility is that the union will agree to allow Ford to sell Visteon provided it follows the example of General Motors, which spun off its Delphi auto parts division to shareholders in May; the unit is now Delphi Automotive Systems Inc. The terms of the new G.M. contract apply to Delphi's 48,000 workers, according to the announcement tonight that the union had reached a deal with G.M.
Seeking to at least temporarily put its past antagonistic labor relations behind it, G.M. last year put Gary L. Cowger in charge of negotiations as G.M.'s group vice president for labor relations. Mr. Cowger is a former factory manager with excellent union contacts who was running G.M.'s German operations at the time.
G.M.'s president, G. Richard Wagoner Jr., and its senior vice president for North American manufacturing, Donald E. Hackworth, handled much of last summer's negotiations with the U.A.W. but did not get along well with union leaders. Mr. Hackworth in particular ended up on poor terms with the union, mainly because he strongly criticized union leaders in several voice mail messages that he recorded and put on a toll-free number for G.M. employees to call. Union leaders also found that they tended to be considerably older and had little in common with Mr. Wagoner, who is only 46 years old.
Sean McAlinden, an auto industry labor economics expert at the University of Michigan, said that G.M.'s personnel change had improved matters. "The important thing at G.M. was the personalities there changed from Wagoner and Hackworth to Cowger, and that made the difference," he said.

HEADLINE: Bradley Presents Health Plan For Almost All the Uninsured

BYLINE: By JAMES DAO 

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, Sept. 28

 BODY:
Former Senator Bill Bradley today proposed a $65 billion-a-year plan that he said would make health care available to virtually all uninsured Americans through an ambitious expansion of Federal tax subsidies and insurance programs.
The proposal by Mr. Bradley, who is seeking the Democratic nomination for President, would offer health coverage to lower-income adults and children in one of two ways: by allowing them to join, with little or no premium costs, the Federal Government's health insurance system for its employees or by providing tax credits to subsidize their premiums in private insurance plans. 
 Under the proposal, a family of four earning as much as $49,200 in gross annual income would be eligible for at least a partial Federal subsidy of its health insurance premiums. And Mr. Bradley would reduce health care costs for people in higher income brackets by excluding all health insurance premiums paid by individuals, regardless of their income, from Federal taxes.
Mr. Bradley said that if all eligible people participated in his plan, 95 percent of the country's 45 million uninsured adults and children would receive health care. But health care experts said it was highly unlikely that so many people would participate in a voluntary plan.
The former Senator said his plan would not require new taxes, and could be financed using the non-Social Security surplus, projected to be more than $1 trillion over the next decade, as well as cost-saving measures. But he cautioned that he did not have a backup financing plan if the economy turned sour and the surplus evaporated.
Throughout his 30-minute speech at a community college here, Mr. Bradley sounded a populist tone, calling good health care a solemn right, not a luxury.
"We are not talking about the irresponsible or the negligent," he said of the uninsured, but about "people you and I know" who are one catastrophic illness away from bankruptcy. It is wrong, he continued, "that kings and dictators can come to this country to get the best medical care in the world, while Americans two blocks away may not be able to afford any care at all."
The former Senator from New Jersey also said he would require all children to have health insurance. But he did not propose an enforcement mechanism, saying, "You have to have trust in the basic goodness of the American people."
Mr. Bradley's plan is more ambitious than one proposed earlier this month by his lone rival for the Democratic nomination, Vice President Al Gore, and thus continues his effort to sharpen his appeal to liberal Democrats. Mr. Gore proposed making health care available to all children but did not go as far as Mr. Bradley in making it accessible to adults.
By contrast, Mr. Bradley's plan is less extensive and regimented than President Clinton's complicated proposal that came under fierce attack from Republicans and the health care industry and was killed by Congress in 1994. That plan would have required all employers to provide health insurance for workers and their families.
Today Mr. Bradley, who did not play a central role in the 1994 fight, described his plan as the kind of bold stroke the nation needs to solve its serious health care problems. But he also asserted that it would have a better chance of passing than Mr. Clinton's proposal because it was simpler, required less bureaucracy, gave people greater say over their insurance plans and would be debated more openly.
At a news conference after his speech, Mr. Bradley also took a sharp shot at Mr. Gore, calling the Vice President's health care plan timid. "I think there are differences between trying to do something big, to deal with a big problem that affects almost all Americans, and continuing to be advocating incremental change," Mr. Bradley said. "It is a fundamental difference between the candidacies."
He also criticized Mr. Gore for not having laid out the cost of his proposal, saying, "A campaign proposal without a price tag is just another politician's promise."
But aides to Mr. Gore said Mr. Bradley's plan failed to address the financial problems of Medicare, the Government health care program for the elderly. The Clinton Administration has proposed setting aside $374 billion of the surplus over the next 10 years to shore up the program. Mr. Bradley's proposal would eat up too much of the surplus to do that, the aides said.
Elaine Kamarck, a policy adviser to Mr. Gore, said, "The shortchanging of Medicare is, I think, a huge problem with the Bradley plan." She also said Mr. Bradley had probably underestimated the cost of his plan.
The plan drew an odd mixture of reactions from people on both ends of the political spectrum. Liberals, while applauding its intent, said it fell short of universal care, while conservatives praised its use of tax credits.
"It's a step in the right direction," said Stuart Butler, vice president for domestic research with the Heritage Foundation, a conservative policy organization. "It's a very shrewd plan that builds on a bipartisan base."
Ron Pollack, executive director of Families USA, a national organization for health care consumers, warned that the plan might encourage companies to eliminate health insurance for employees, pushing them into the Federal plan. "I've never been a fan of using the tax code as the best vehicle for achieving coverage," Mr. Pollack said. "It does have the possibility of providing tax breaks for people that don't need them. And it could result in employer-based coverage being scaled back."
Mr. Bradley's proposal would essentially replace Medicaid, the Government health care program for the poor, with a new system run entirely by the Federal Government, which would continue to finance care for the poor.
Medicaid costs are now split among Federal, state and local governments. Under Mr. Bradley's plan, the Federal Government would pay the full cost of acute care for low-income adults and children, while states would pick up the entire Medicaid cost of long-term nursing home care for the elderly. The swap would reduce states' costs by $18 billion a year, Mr. Bradley's aides said.
But the plan would go beyond current government programs by using the tax code to help the working poor and higher income people.
Under the plan, Mr. Bradley would give lower income families and individuals tax credits to help pay for premiums. Children living in families earning under 200 percent of the poverty line -- or $32,800 a year in gross income for a family of four -- would receive a $1,200 tax subsidy. That is enough to pay annual premiums in many private plans, Mr. Bradley's aides said.
A smaller tax credit would be available to families earning between 200 percent and 300 percent of the poverty level, which is $49,200 a year in gross income for a family of four.
Adults, with or without children, would receive a $1,800 tax credit if their income was below poverty level, which is a $16,400 gross annual income for a family of four. Adults earning between 100 percent and 200 percent of the poverty line would receive somewhat smaller credits.
Mr. Bradley would allow people to use the tax credits, which would be paid in advance directly to insurance companies, to pay for insurance premiums. The credits could be used either with the Federal Employees Health Benefits Program, a system of private companies that insures Federal employees, or with any other private insurer.
Nine million Federal workers, including members of Congress, now belong to the Federal employees system. Mr. Bradley's aides estimated that as many as two-thirds of the 33 million unemployed adults in the country might join the Federal system if the plan was enacted.
Health care experts cautioned that opening the Federal Employees Health Benefits Program to a tide of lower-income people, many of them unemployed, would place costly new burdens on the program and potentially drive up premiums.
Mr. Bradley would also exclude all health insurance premium payments by individuals from Federal taxes. And he would create an optional prescription drug benefit for Medicare recipients that would have a $500 deductible, $25 monthly premium and 25 percent co-payment fee.

HEADLINE: Chase Agrees to Acquire Hambrecht & Quist

BYLINE: By JOSEPH KAHN and PATRICK McGEEHAN 

 BODY:
The Chase Manhattan Corporation agreed yesterday to buy San Francisco-based Hambrecht & Quist for $1.35 billion, giving one of America's dominant traditional banks an entree into the world of Internet start-ups and bringing it a step closer to becoming a full-service financial concern.
The proposed cash deal pairs two financial companies -- Chase, the heavyweight banker for large corporations, and Hambrecht, a boutique catering to Silicon Valley entrepreneurs -- that had begun to look like lonely singles at a swinging square dance. Chase's major competitors have all bought or merged with investment banking firms. Hambrecht is the last of its class of finance firms that catered to technology companies to find a larger and deeper-pocketed partner. 
 But the marriage, if it is consummated, falls far short of the megamerger that Chase executives, and many on Wall Street, have talked about for more than a year. The New York-based bank, the third largest in the nation, has made no secret of its desire for a "transformative" deal that would create the world's most potent competitor in everything from bank loans to initial offerings of stock. Chase officials were quick to point out that yesterday's purchase did not fulfill those ambitions.
"It's important to say what this deal is and what it isn't," said Marc Shapiro, a Chase vice chairman. "What it is is an investment in the new economy. But as a total solution to the global platform we are looking for -- we have not crossed that bridge."
The Hambrecht deal seemed to some analysts to be a tacit admission that Chase's recent overtures to Merrill Lynch, Morgan Stanley Dean Witter and Goldman, Sachs & Company, the three biggest Wall Street firms, had been unrequited, at least for now. But many said that they expected the consolidation in the financial industry to continue.
"Could we wake up one morning and find that Chase has merged with Merrill? I don't think this changes that possibility," said David Berry, a bank industry expert at Keefe, Bruyette & Woods. "But for some reason, there's been no big deal for Chase so far. And at some point you have to get on with life. This makes sense for them."
One possibility, bank analysts said, is that Chase's pursuit of Hambrecht signals its willingness to build a stock underwriting franchise brickby brick, rather than spending at least $30 billion to buy one of the top three Wall Street firms. That suggests that Chase could try to buy a midsize underwriter like Lehman Brothers or Paine Webber next.
Though it leads the pack in the traditional lending business, Chase had been slow to seize on the opportunity to expand into stock underwriting. Competitors including Citigroup, Bank of America and BankBoston have been quicker to snap up stock underwriters when Depression-era laws that separated banks and brokerages began being eased several years ago.
The Hambrecht match also underscores the increasing importance of what analysts call the "new economy" -- high-technology and Internet companies that, at least in their early years, are too small or lose too much money to do business with the likes of Chase. Such start-up companies have often traditionally relied on specialist firms like Hambrecht for financial guidance because they were ignored by large banks and most Wall Street firms.
Hambrecht's roots in the technology banking business run deep. Daniel H. Case 3d, the chief executive, is the older brother of Steve Case, chief executive of America Online. Hambrecht's reputation for picking small but promising winners was made in 1980, when it brought Apple Computer to market, and sealed in 1995, when it backed Netscape's initial public offering.
For Hambrecht -- its name would change to Chase Securities West if the deal is completed, as expected, by the end of the year -- Chase seems an ideal match after a long search. Mr. Case has talked to numerous potential banking partners in the past, including the German banks Deutsche Bank and Dresdner Bank, as well as Merrill Lynch, bankers said. Hambrecht's relative position in high-technology investment banking had slipped behind Goldman, Sachs, Morgan Stanley and other top Wall Street firms in the last few years, in part because it did not have the range of services of its rivals.
"Our little entrepreneurial part of the economy has become the dominant part of the total economy in the 1990's," Mr. Case said in a interview at his headquarters, a distinctive steel and glass structure designed by Mies van der Rohe in San Francisco's financial district. "But our ability to serve our clients has been narrowed by products and geography. Now we can grow with them."
The sale is also a financial coup for Hambrecht executives. Chase will pay $50 a share to buy the firm, 22 percent more than Monday's closing price of $41.0625. It will also set aside $200 million in stock to retain key bankers at the combined firm. Shares of Hambrecht rose $7.625 yesterday, to $48.6875, a record high; Chase fell 31.25 cents, to $73.8125.
Hambrecht could bargain from a position of relative strength this year after its underwriting business slumped in 1997 and 1998. It has underwrotten 17 initial public offerings valued at $972 million by the Securities Data Corporation so far this year. That is almost exactly what it underwrote in 1996, though its share of the business is less than 5 percent now, compared with 15 percent three years ago. It stock has more than tripled in the last 12 months.
For Chase, the benefits of Hambrecht are less certain. Their main clients are on almost opposite poles of the economy. Chase serves large corporations that have sophisticated banking needs and proven profit records. Hambrecht prides itself in spotting new companies that would not qualify for a bank loan.
"There is definitely a substantial difference in the size of our clients," said James B. Lee Jr., a Chase vice chairman who heads the bank's investment banking arm and sat next to Mr. Case to explain the merger to Hambrecht employees yesterday. "But the wonderful thing about the tech business is that those companies grow fast."
For several years, Chase has been trying to extend its franchise into investment banking, with mixed success. It is the No. 1 provider of syndicated loans, one of the top three underwriters of junk bonds and a top 10 player in mergers and acquisitions. But it has yet to make a mark in equities, the core investment banking business.
Chase is not the first New York bank to go west to take advantage of the prolonged boom in technology stocks. Two years ago Merrill Lynch came very close to buying Hambrecht amid a spree of acquisitions of midsize investment banks. Morgan Stanley and Goldman, Sachs have also beefed up their West Coast presences and grabbed larger shares of the technology investment banking business. In terms of technology company underwriting, Goldman, Sachs and Morgan Stanley are now No. 1 and No. 2, respectively, by a wide margin. So far this year, Hambrecht ranks No. 9.
A buyout by Chase would end the independence of the last remaining member of four smaller technology-focused investment banks based outside of New York that was known on Wall Street as the HARM group. The group comprised Hambrecht; Alex. Brown & Sons; Robertson, Stephens and Montgomery Securities. Within the last two years, each of those firms has been gobbled up by a large commercial bank and each of the acquisitions has been followed by defections of large numbers of investment bankers and other key employees.
The most notable example of post-merger conflict came after the Nationsbank Corporation bought San Francisco-based Montgomery Securities for $1.2 billion. A year later, Thomas Weisel, who had run Montgomery, left to start a new firm and recruited several of his highest-ranking colleagues to join him.
"The issues are cultural -- the bureaucracy of a larger bank being superimposed on an entrepreneurial shop," Mr. Weisel said yesterday, reflecting on his experience at Nationsbank. Mr. Weisel said he had respect for Chase executives, but predicted that they would also face obstacles integrating Hambrecht.
Mr. Case and Mr. Lee acknowledged that there was not a particularly successful model for the sort of the deal they are proposing, but they said they expected a different result. "This is about a lot more than just a successful integration, but without a successful integration nothing else matters," Mr. Case said.
Still, Chase is setting aside $200 million of stock to retain a pool of Hambrecht's key employees. Mr. Case declined to say how many people would share that money but he said it would be distributed "broader and deeper" than in other acquisitions in keeping with the "egalitarian" culture of the firm.
Mr. Lee said those extended payouts would not be the key to making the integration work. "Money can't fix a bad cultural fit," he said.
Mr. Case and the man he referred to as "my new boss," Mr. Lee, are a study in contrasts. Mr. Case, tall and slim at 41, still looks the part of the wunderkind he once was. A Princeton University graduate, he joined Hambrecht after completing a Rhodes scholarship at Oxford University. He was named co-chief executive in 1992 and sole chief executive two years later.
Mr. Lee, 46, in French cuffs and suspenders, looks and talks more the part of the powerful New York investment banker. Chase executives said the Hambrecht deal increased his responsibilities within the bank. Mr. Lee already oversees Chase's substantial syndicated lending and merger and acquisitions businesses.

HEADLINE: Shoring Up the Volunteer Army

 BODY:
For the better part of two decades, the catchy advertising jingle "Be All That You Can Be," along with promises of on-the-job training and money for higher education, helped the United States Army meet its recruiting goals and gave the country a high-quality volunteer force. But now the Army, along with other branches of the armed services, is facing a serious recruiting gap that is forcing it to rethink its hiring practices and marketing strategy. The proposed changes in tactics outlined in an article in Monday's Times seem like a good start on what is likely to be a long and difficult effort to turn things around. As a collateral benefit, these changes may help society as well.
Officials at the Pentagon offer several reasons for the declining lack of interest in military service. One is purely statistical. The pool of young men and women between the ages of 18 and 22, the optimum age for recruits, has shrunk to about 21 million, down 5 million from the 1980 level. Meanwhile, a robust economy has created abundant job opportunities for those who might otherwise be interested in enlisting, while making it financially feasible for more young people to attend college. 
 There may also be more amorphous influences at work. Louis Caldera, the Secretary of the Army, notes that many of the parents of the present generation of recruits came of age after the draft ended in 1973, and thus have no tradition of military service to pass along to their offspring. Representative Steve Buyer, an Indiana Republican who heads the House subcommittee on military personnel, believes that the armed forces have never codified their basic mission since the end of the cold war and, under President Clinton, have begun to resemble an international fire brigade with poorly defined, helter-skelter responsibilities that might best be left to regional powers.
Whatever the reason, the numbers are not good. When the fiscal year ends tomorrow, the Army will fall short of its goal for the second year in a row, signing up 7,000 fewer enlistees than the 74,500 it needs to maintain its force at current levels. The Air Force is expected to miss its target by a lesser amount and the Navy will just squeak by. Only the Marine Corps has kept pace. Like all the services, the Army has increased its advertising, raised its "signing bonuses" and increased its contribution to a recruit's post-military college tuition from $40,000 to $50,000. The Army has also sent young soldiers back into their neighborhoods to deliver pep talks to their civilian contemporaries.
These tactics make sense, but the most promising strategy may lie, paradoxically, in the Pentagon's willingness to ease its standards. Under present rules, 90 percent of the Army's recruits must have at least a high school diploma. In recent years, however, the Army has found that recruits with lesser academic credentials like a general equivalency diploma -- G.E.D. for short -- have performed just as well as those with high school degrees. Thus, just as the Navy did last year, the Army is thinking of relaxing the 90 percent requirement, allowing more G.E.D. students into its ranks. It is also planning a pilot program that will pay 6,000 high school dropouts to pursue a G.E.D. so they can qualify to join the active force or the reserves. In a similar vein, the Army will try to sign up students who have started college but dropped out.
In effect, the Army will increasingly be seeking its recruits among dropouts of one sort or another, as long as they possess or can be persuaded to obtain minimal credentials. This strategy could yield a double benefit. It could not only help the Army deal with its manpower problems but also give a new sense of direction to many thousands of young people who might otherwise wind up on the streets, unemployed or in dead-end service jobs.

HEADLINE: Ever-Burning Issue

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 28

 BODY:
This was going to be the year that Congress and the Clinton Administration de-electrified the third rail of American politics, putting partisanship aside to insure the long-term viability of the Social Security system.
"So first and above all," President Clinton said during his State of the Union address in January, "we must save Social Security for the 21st century." 
 It never happened. Instead, Social Security proved to be as irresistible and potent a political weapon as ever, if not more so. And decisions by both parties to use the retirement program's almost sacred place in the hearts of voters as a club against the other help explain why Congress and the White House are again headed toward a budget showdown.
Conceding that Congress will be unable to pass the spending bills necessary to keep the Federal Government operating after the start of the new fiscal year on Friday, the House and the Senate both passed a measure today to provide the necessary money for three weeks while Republicans try to work out their differences with Mr. Clinton.
In large part because of the way they chose to play the Social Security card, the two sides are in a fiscal box of their own making. Mr. Clinton blocked Republican efforts to enact a big tax cut by arguing that projected Federal budget surpluses should be set aside until Social Security's long-term financial problems had been resolved.
Frustrated by the success of Mr. Clinton's strategy -- and fed up with the Democratic Party's claims over many decades to be the one true protector of Social Security -- Republicans tried to turn the tables. Eager to advance their goal of reducing the size and influence Federal Government, they demanded earlier this year that the President scale back his spending requests to avoid dipping into the portion of next year's surplus generated by excess Social Security revenue.
Unwilling to cede the high ground, Mr. Clinton took the Republicans up on their challenge this summer. But with time running out to pull together next year's budget, neither side has a politically realistic way of making good on its promise to keep its hands off Social Security, at least not without resorting to wholesale gimmickry. And both sides are largely ignoring the reality that spending a small portion of Social Security revenues will have little or no effect on the retirement system's long-term health or on the economy.
"It will be difficult for either side to gain significant advantage," said Robert D. Reischauer, a former director of the Congressional Budget Office who is now a fellow at the Brookings Institution, a liberal-leaning research group. "The Republicans made a premature promise that was reiterated by the President. It's something neither party could fulfill."
Neither party, though, is yet willing to give up, fearing that to do so would hand the other side an explosive issue to exploit in next year's election campaign.
"It looks like they're both trying to run feeder lines from any available source of power to make this issue even more electrified," said David C. John, a senior policy analyst at the Heritage Foundation, a conservative research organization. "If you can accuse the other side of being opposed to motherhood and the flag, by God you'll do it."
In a technical sense, spending some part of next year's Social Security surplus has almost nothing to do with the longer term issue of assuring that the retirement system is financially sound enough to weather the impending retirement of the vast baby boom generation.
The Government has spent excess Social Security revenue routinely for decades. But that was before the Government moved from budget deficits into surpluses and gained the luxury of differentiating between revenue from general taxation and revenue generated by the payroll tax that finances Social Security.
Other than cutting taxes, the Government can do two things with its excess revenue. It can spend the money or reduce the part of the national debt held by the public.
Social Security revenue will far exceed what is needed to pay benefits to current retirees for another decade or two, and then will fall well below what is needed to pay full benefits. Next year's projected $147 billion Social Security surplus will reach $235 billion by 2009.
If the Social Security surplus is used to reduce the national debt, it will help keep interest rates low, spur additional economic growth and leave the Federal Government in better financial condition for the day when it has to begin borrowing again to meet Social Security obligations.
But spending a small part of the money would still allow the Government to reduce the national debt substantially in coming years. And spending the money -- using it to cover a shortfall in the Government's general accounts -- would not in any way change the retirement system's financial condition, since Social Security revenues are not set aside on the Government's books specifically to pay benefits.
"Many Americans -- and more than a few policy makers -- appear to believe that using a portion of the Social Security surplus to cover a deficit in the non-Social Security budget would constitute a 'raid' on Social Security, reducing Social Security's assets and impairing its ability to pay benefits in the future," the Center on Budget and Policy Priorities, a liberal-leaning research organization, said in a recent report. "These beliefs, however, are not correct and appear to be rooted in a misunderstanding of how Social Security finances work."
In that sense, drawing a line in the sand over spending Social Security revenue is an arbitrary way of deciding how to apportion projected surpluses. It is more about political symbolism than about policy or economic choices.
"There desperately needs to be a decent, civil discussion about what we do with Social Security and for that matter what we do with our entire budget," said Mr. John of the Heritage Foundation. "I'm afraid 1999 and 2000 do not look like the years that is going to happen."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Ecuador Allowed to Default As a Warning for Markets
The Clinton administration's decision to let Ecuador default on its debts is being interpreted as a message from Lawrence H. Summers, the Treasury Secretary, that a little fear would be good for the markets.
That message, China's possible entry into the World Trade Organization and more aid for Russia are among the topics for the International Monetary Fund and the World Bank. Page C4. 

Big Crackdown by S.E.C.
Stepping up its oversight of corporate accounting, the S.E.C. brought charges against 68 companies and individuals, who were accused of accounting fraud at 15 companies. Among 11 chief executives named in the complaints was Fran Tarkenton, the former quarterback who became a software entrepreneur. C2.
 
Showdown Seen Over Stock Rules
The New York Stock Exchange appears to be headed for a showdown with the chairman of the Securities and Exchange Commission over how the exchange will be regulated when it converts to a publicly traded business. C12.
 
Stocks Rebound; End Down Slightly
Stocks staged a late-session rally, with the Dow Jones industrial average gaining nearly 200 points in the last two hours, to close down 27.86 points, to 10,275.53. The Nasdaq composite index fell 5.50 points, to 2,756.25. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index declined 1.11 points, to 1,282.20. C14.

Competition on Internet Addresses
Network Solutions, which has held a monopoly on registering Internet addresses for six years, agreed to open the process to competition -- but only after winning a key concession from the Clinton Administration that effectively limits the authority of the Internet's new governing body. C12.

The decline followed Aetna's meeting with analysts, who contended that Aetna failed to explain how the deal would deliver the immediate increases in profits that Aetna had projected. C25.

HEADLINE: Aviation Experts Bemoan Delays in the Sky

BYLINE: By MATTHEW L. WALD 

 BODY:
Flight delays across the nation were miserable last spring and summer and they may get worse, experts from the airlines and the air traffic controllers' union agreed yesterday, but they disagreed about why, who was to blame and how to fix the problem.
The experts, speaking in a session in Manhattan yesterday with a top official of the Federal Aviation Administration, said that delays were up sharply for the first eight months of the year, to a national total of 265,200 delays of more than 15 minutes. But a deeper problem, according to R. John Hansman, a professor of aeronautics at M.I.T., is that the F.A.A. has failed in several major efforts to modernize the air traffic system, and the volume of flights has grown to the point that the capacity of the system is falling. 
 Several airline representatives and Monte R. Belger, the associate administrator of the F.A.A. for air traffic services, said that the job of handling traffic would be performed better by a private organization or a quasi-governmental agency. The F.A.A., part of the United States Department of Transportation, is hampered by Government procurement and budgeting procedures and by political interference from Congress, outside aviation experts say.
"The Government has simply got to get the hell out of this," said Robert L. Crandall, retired chairman of American Airlines. He and the other experts spoke at a session sponsored by the Air Safety Alliance, a nonprofit consortium of airlines and airplane and engine makers, and the Newseum, a news museum.
The Clinton Administration tried in the mid-90's to spin off the F.A.A. as a Government-owned corporation, but the idea went nowhere in Congress. Mr. Belger said that the privatized Canadian air traffic system, Nav Canada, had raised controllers' salaries 31 percent and still cut its costs through efficiencies that only a private enterprise could manage.
Nationally, for the first eight months of this year, delays of more than 15 minutes because of weather, air traffic volume, breakdown of air traffic control equipment, runways out of service and other problems were up 19.6 percent over the same period a year earlier. April through August was particularly bad, partly because the F.A.A. installed new computer screens in several air traffic control centers and the controllers, while getting used to them, spaced planes more widely, F.A.A. officials say.
Airports in the Northeast, including the three major airports in the New York region, are chronically congested.
Mr. Crandall pointed out that in 1977, the last year before airline deregulation, the airlines carried 220 million passengers domestically; last year, it was 560 million.
Capt. Bill Cotton, manager of air traffic flight systems at United Airlines, said that the descriptions of the air traffic system sounded like trying to get 10 pounds of potatoes into a 5-pound bag. "My response," he said, "has been for the last 32 years, 'Why don't you build another bag?' "
The system is "technologically archaic," he said, and he offered his own flight from Chicago to New York as an example. It was on time, he said, but only because the airline had scheduled the trip to take 30 minutes longer than it did 25 years ago.
Most parties deflected blame. "When the F.A.A.'s performance deteriorates, no one notices," said Mr. Crandall, "particularly since it is easy to blame the airlines for every problem."
But J. Randall Schwitz Jr., a vice president of the controllers' union, said, "I guarantee, on any airplane you get on that's delayed, what will come over from the voice in that cockpit, is air traffic control delay, no matter what the reason." He said there was lots of spare capacity in the system, but that the hub-and-spoke system had led to the airlines' creating several rush hours a day at their hub airports.
If the economy stays healthy and fuel prices stay low, most experts expect more passengers and more air traffic delays. The F.A.A. is installing new equipment, but much of it will not give controllers new tools to handle traffic. New computers and monitors, for example, are intended to replace old, frail parts that are no longer available with standardized computers. Eventually, the new equipment can be programmed to help controllers do more work.

HEADLINE: WORK IS PROPOSED ON 2D AVE. SUBWAY

BYLINE: By THOMAS J. LUECK 

 BODY:
Aiming to capitalize on a booming economy while addressing some of the New York region's mass transit needs, the Metropolitan Transportation Authority said yesterday that it planned to spend $1.6 billion for a new Long Island Rail Road tunnel to Grand Central Terminal and $700 million to restart the long-dormant Second Avenue subway project.
The proposals, in the M.T.A.'s five-year capital plan, still face scrutiny in Albany, where Assembly Speaker Sheldon Silver has warned he may block the plan because it favors suburban commuters. There are also questions about how it will be financed. 
 Still, the five-year plan is a blueprint that represents the authority's long-term priorities: It calls for spending $16.5 billion on such things as new buses, subway cars and station improvements, as well as new tracks and tunnels. An additional $1 billion would be provided for the Triborough Bridge and Tunnel Authority for bridge improvements.
In another large subway expansion project, advocated strongly by Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani, the plan would dedicate $645 million for an extension of the N line in Queens to La Guardia Airport.
The spending plan, to be presented to the M.T.A. board today, is the largest in the history of the authority and would represent the biggest expansion of the city's mass transit system since the 1930's, said the M.T.A.'s chairman, E. Virgil Conway.
Mr. Conway acknowledged that large questions remained over how the plan would be financed, saying it would require either a large infusion of new government subsidies, or fare increases.
The political hurdles to the plan were already apparent yesterday. In an interview, Mr. Silver, who represents the Upper East Side and has veto power over the capital spending plan, said he would "not allow it to go forward" unless more priority was given to construction of the Second Avenue subway.
Mr. Silver's criticism reflected the concerns of transit advocates and some urban planners that the plan would pay for only an abbreviated version of a Second Avenue subway line. Instead of having it run from northern Manhattan to the Battery, the M.T.A. envisions a new tunnel under Second Avenue from 125th Street to 63d Street, where the line would turn west along an unused subway spur that lies under the southeast corner of Central Park.
Using that spur, the line would connect to what is now the express track of the N and R lines, which slices through the middle of Manhattan on its journey downtown. As a result, it would not provide convenient service to the East Side of Manhattan below 63d Street.
"Everybody wants the entire Second Avenue line to be built," Mr. Conway said, adding that the five-year plan provided for only a "first phase of the project," namely, engineering, environmental studies and some limited construction. He said later extensions of the line could, in theory, allow it to continue down Second Avenue toward the Battery.
The Second Avenue subway has long been a dream of mass transit advocates who have been pressing to relieve crowding on the Lexington Avenue line. Work on the huge project had been under way by the mid-1970's, when the city's fiscal crisis forced a halt to the construction, leaving three short tunnels, at 120th Street, 110th Street and Canal Street, that have never been used.
Transit officials said they had limited the plan for new construction to upper Manhattan because of the engineering difficulties and expense of extending a new line under the more congested parts of midtown.
But critics of the authority's proposal said the line would not alleviate what is expected to be a sharp increase in congestion at Grand Central should the Long Island Rail Road begin taking passengers there, perhaps as early as 2009.
"Even before you add the Long Island Rail Road riders, the Lexington Avenue line will be physically unable to accept more riders," said Jeffrey Zupan, a transportation analyst for the Regional Plan Association, which earlier this year provoked widespread interest in the Second Avenue project with a report in which it advocated a new line down the entire East Side of Manhattan, and extensions into the Bronx and Brooklyn. "They can describe this as a first step, but they have no second or third step," he said.
Mr. Silver, one of three members of an M.T.A. oversight board whose approval is needed for the five-year capital plan, said he would insist that the Second Avenue project and the new tunnel to Grand Central "be built in tandem."
In addition to the oversight board, the capital plan must be approved by Gov. George E. Pataki, who exercises broad influence over the M.T.A. board and is believed to have given the green light to the plan.
If adopted, the plan would be virtually without parallel in scope for government agencies in the New York region because most are not required to forecast their finances so far in the future. Although similar plans have guided the M.T.A. for decades, they are not legally binding and can be amended because of shifts in the economy or changes in the level of government subsidy.
The plan to be presented today, which would become effective on Jan. 1, is far larger than its predecessor, which called for capital spending of $12.5 billion during the five years that end on Dec. 31.
Although the new plan is unusual in that it would set aside large investments in new subways and train service, it allocates most spending -- $13.5 billion -- for maintenance and improvements to the existing system. Under the plan, $2 billion would be spent on 1,130 new subway cars, resulting in an addition of 200 cars.
The Long Island Rail Road, which has faced broad criticism of the condition of its trains, would receive $934 million to buy and renovate cars. New York City Transit, which operates buses and subways in the city, would be given $416 million for 1,056 new buses, 300 powered by alternative fuels but most fueled by diesel.
The plan to acquire more diesel buses set off an outcry yesterday by environmentalists.
"With this plan, we remain way out of step with other cities," said Richard Kassel, senior attorney for the Natural Resources Defense Council, adding that many big-city transportation authorities had eliminated or severely restricted the use of diesel-powered buses.
Mr. Conway said the biggest challenge facing the authority would be filling a $4.44 billion gap between what it expects to receive in government support, revenues from riders and other sources, and what it would need to pay for its plan.
He said part of the gap could be filled by reducing expenses, itself a difficult proposition since the M.T.A.'s contract with its largest labor union, Local 100 of the Transport Workers Union, expires in December. On Monday, senior officials of the union said they might strike New York City Transit if their demands for wage increases were not met.
Mr. Conway said the largest part of the financial gap facing the authority would have to be filled through "new government assistance or other resources," which could include fare increases.
