HEADLINE: Law-Enforcement Rivalry in U.S. Slowed Inquiry on Russian Funds

BYLINE: By TIMOTHY L. O'BRIEN and LOWELL BERGMAN 

 BODY:
The investigation of Russian money flowing through the Bank of New York has been snarled by one of the oldest and most bitter rivalries in American law enforcement -- between the Manhattan District Attorney and the F.B.I.'s powerful New York office.
During the last year, law enforcement officials say, investigators for each agency separately discovered that shadowy figures were using an obscure company, the Benex Corporation, to move money out of Russia. 
 The inquiries began from separate points -- a kidnapping case for the Federal Bureau of Investigation and a suspected money-laundering operation for the New York prosecutors. Each side had information the other lacked that ultimately showed these were two pieces of a single puzzle.
But the information was never pooled, officials said.
In fact, F.B.I. officials are said to have been angered when British investigators, who were conducting their own investigation of Russian organized crime, told a New York prosecutor and a State Department official what they knew about the Federal case.
Since the investigation burst into public view in August, each side has been trying to leave the impression that it was hotly pursuing the case while its counterpart was dragging its feet or chasing down empty leads.
Relations have become so frayed that Federal investigators have let it be known that bureau officials and Federal attorneys even discussed whether they could file obstruction-of-justice charges against counterparts in other agencies whom they perceived as getting in the way.
These disputes -- and the larger question of how they may affect the outcome of the investigation -- are far from resolved. Federal law enforcement officials insist that the New York prosecutors know little of value for their case, while defenders of the District Attorney say the bureau is still missing out on important leads. But several points are clear.
The office of the District Attorney, Robert M. Morgenthau, was the first to find the link between Benex and the Bank of New York and has looked closely at the maze of companies that his investigators believe were the conduit for the money.
But, Federal investigators said, the prosecutor never closely examined the bank's role in the affair and did not obtain crucial bank records that disclosed billions of dollars in suspicious transfers from Russia.
The F.B.I. focused on the bank more than a year ago and was the first agency to understand the size of the Benex transactions. But the bureau has been faulted by some Administration officials for failing to tell other agencies, including the Treasury Department and State Department, what it knew about such an important banking case with broad diplomatic implications.
 
The Rivals
Whose Turf Is It, Contenders Ask
At the heart of the turf war are legendary personalities and longstanding rivalries between two of the country's most aggressive investigators.
In one corner stands Robert Morgenthau, 80, the powerful District Attorney, whose unsuccessful prosecution of the Bank of Credit and Commerce International earlier in this decade left Federal agencies suspect of his crusades against white-collar crime.
In the other corner is Louis J. Freeh, 49, the hard-driving director of the Federal Bureau of Investigation. Mr. Freeh, who began his career as an F.B.I. agent and Federal prosecutor in New York, has ample personal experience in the often-tense relations between Mr. Morgenthau's office and the bureau's agents.
The tension has been enhanced by the presence of Mary Jo White, the United States Attorney in Manhattan and a long-time rival of Mr. Morgenthau, who is working closely with the bureau.
Mr. Morgenthau has long viewed Federal investigators, particularly the F.B.I., as slow-footed and timid in their pursuit of financial criminals. The bureau considers Mr. Morgenthau an interloper who is overzealous in his attempts to broaden his jurisdiction.
Nonetheless, Mr. Morgenthau's jurisdiction is Manhattan, the financial capital of the world, and that gives him powers greater than a typical local law enforcement official's if a case revolves around white-collar crime.
Mr. Freeh has been anxious to broaden the bureau's authority as well. He has expanded its presence overseas in an effort to counter sophisticated international criminal networks that operate in the United States. That expansion has irritated some Foreign Service professionals, complicating matters for the bureau, which is often perceived as arrogant.
The stakes hold more than parochial interest. The Bank of New York affair has already touched off a broad debate about American policy in Russia, the role played by Western institutions in perhaps facilitating corruption there, and how power is wielded in an economically troubled country with stores of nuclear arms. The investigation, and any facts it may uncover, touches on all of those concerns.
 
The Conduit
So Many Dollars, So Few Accounts
The focus of the battle between Mr. Morgenthau and Mr. Freeh is Benex, a mysterious money-moving company run by an equally elusive Russian emigre named Peter Berlin.
Between 1996 and August of this year, Benex moved at least $7.5 billion from Russia through just nine accounts at the Bank of New York, according to the bank. The bank has not been charged with wrongdoing and its senior executives are not targets of the Federal investigation, according to law enforcement officials.
Although the F.B.I. has been sifting through reams of bank data for about a year, the exact source of the funds remains unclear.
Investigators say part of the money is from ordinary wire transfers and tax avoidance, much of which is perfectly legal in Russia, while some may be tied to organized crime, corporate embezzlement or political graft. Law enforcement officials say proof of the latter can only be unearthed by Russian authorities.
The pursuit of Benex began in February 1998 when Mr. Morgenthau convened a state grand jury and issued subpoenas in response to British investigators who were seeking help in examining possible money laundering through Benex.
It seemed a natural request. The British were looking into the activities of a London law firm, Talbott, Creggy & Company, which had set up offshore shell companies used to move money. Mr. Morgenthau was examining the same firm for its role in facilitating a New York-based stock fraud.
A month later, Mr. Morgenthau asked the Federal Government for help. But he did not speak with the F.B.I. Instead, he sent a memo to the Customs Service noting that large sums of money with suspect origins were flowing through Benex into accounts at the Bank of New York.
The British and American inquiries focused on a Russian emigre, Roman Amiragov, who was suspected of money laundering, and who may have been a client of Talbott, Creggy. According to law enforcement officials, Mr. Amiragov ran a company in Britain and the United States, Portrait Trading, that in early 1998 alone moved $300,000 to $500,000 a week through Benex into the Bank of New York.
Little is known about Mr. Amiragov. He and his family broke their lease and abruptly left their Jersey City apartment shortly after the Bank of New York investigation became public in August. Mr. Amiragov left the country but returned on Sept. 15. His current whereabouts could not be determined.
Officials say Mr. Morgenthau's office did not see the Bank of New York as an important part of its inquiry until very recently. The office did not issue subpoenas to the Bank of New York until shortly before the Federal investigation burst into public view, leading Federal investigators to suggest that Mr. Morgenthau's office was poaching.
In June 1998, the F.B.I began following a separate trail toward Benex and the Bank of New York. At the request of the Russian Government, the bureau looked into a kidnapping ransom paid to organized crime figures through a Benex account at the Bank of New York.
Suspicions about Benex deepened when another New York bank, Republic Bank, reported to the Federal Government that it had handled several suspicious transfers of money from Russia involving Benex.
A Federal grand jury was convened to handle the case in September 1998, and the F.B.I. approached the bank to secure its cooperation.
This move was a calculated risk. In return for a chance to monitor the flow of money through the accounts, the bureau assured senior executives that they would not be targets of the investigation, even though the bank's role in the matter was not yet entirely clear.
 
The Conflict
Confidential Data, Interagency Tussle
Bureau officials say their effort is now the biggest money-laundering investigation in history. The dragnet, involving dozens of agents and analysts examining more than 100 banks in more than 40 countries, is described by one Federal law enforcement official as "an octopus."
Word of the F.B.I.'s investigation began to spread.
In October 1998, John Moscow, a prosecutor with the District Attorney's Office, traveled to London to meet with the National Crime Squad. Mr. Moscow, also a veteran of the failed B.C.C.I. prosecution -- and also viewed dimly by the bureau, where he is considered a conspiracy theorist -- heard a lot in London about apparent money laundering at the Bank of New York.
Mr. Moscow relayed what he had learned to Jonathan Winer, a specialist in international crime at the State Department. Mr. Winer, an ally of Mr. Morgenthau's office from the B.C.C.I. days, was the first to alert the Administration to the troubling dimensions of the Bank of New York matter, information that Mr. Winer believed should have helped inform foreign and economic policy assessments of Russia.
After visiting London himself in March this year, Mr. Winer returned with a sheaf of documents and serious qualms stemming from his belief that the bureau had not notified the White House or any national security specialists about the scale of the problem.
So Mr. Winer rang a few alarms.
In March he gave copies of his documents to National Security Council officials and then notified the White House, the Central Intelligence Agency and the Justice Department.
The news was not well received at the F.B.I., where investigators discovered to their dismay that Mr. Winer was circulating information that the bureau had passed on in confidence to British investigators.
Other Federal agencies found out about the investigation piecemeal. The Treasury Department, which has played the leading role in formulating American economic policy toward Russia, did not learn about the investigation until April 1999, and its knowledge came from the Federal Reserve Bank in New York. Even the Attorney General, Janet Reno, had not received a briefing -- and did not until August, after the investigation was reported in The New York Times.
And then the real fighting began.
Mr. Morgenthau's office complained that the F.B.I. never took the Bank of New York matter seriously enough until it was highlighted in the news media. While the British were told that an F.B.I. agent in New York, Steve Chapman, was leading a task force examining the bank's operations, the British felt that Mr. Chapman was the only agent involved. According to American investigators familiar with the British viewpoint, London dismissed Mr. Chapman's effort as "Task Force Steve."
People close to the F.B.I. retort that the only reason the investigation appeared so thinly staffed is because the British were not aware of its scope.
Moreover, those people say, Mr. Morgenthau is a publicity seeker who never devoted serious resources to his own investigation of Benex until it burst into the public spotlight. Those people said that if Mr. Morgenthau ever had significant information to share about Benex or the Bank of New York, the bureau would have welcomed it.
A recent series of meetings were convened at the United States Attorney's office in Manhattan that in the eyes of the F.B.I. were meant to seek the cooperation of various law enforcement agencies in the investigation.
The District Attorney's office had a different view: Its investigators said they were told "to get out of the way."
 
http://www.nytimes.com

CORRECTION-DATE: October 3, 1999, Sunday

 CORRECTION:
An article on Tuesday described the ways in which the investigation of possible Russian money laundering through the Bank of New York had been snared in an old rivalry between the Federal Bureau of Investigation and the office of Robert Morgenthau, the Manhattan District Attorney.
In discussing Mr. Morgenthau's record, the article referred to his "unsuccessful prosecution" of the Bank of Credit and Commerce International.
The reference did not fairly characterize Mr. Morgenthau's handling of the case. While the prosecutions of some individuals failed, the article should have credited Mr. Morgenthau with important breakthroughs on the case, including helping to reveal and shut down illegal financial operations and winning more than $1.5 billion in fines, which were turned over to the bank's creditors.

HEADLINE: LIBRARY/CD-ROM MAPS;
Magic Carpets for Armchair Explorations

BYLINE: By SHELLY FREIERMAN 

 BODY:

NATIONAL GEOGRAPHIC MAPS
(National Geographic Society; $49.95; Windows 95 and later.)
GENERATIONS of map lovers have delighted in all the sensations associated with the maps that come tucked inside National Geographic magazines: the crisp unfolding of paper and faint smell of ink as the reader bends over the table and lingers on the strange names of cities and rivers. 
 The National Geographic Society has collected every map included in the magazine through 1997 -- all 535 of them -- on a set of eight CD-ROM's. The first fold-out map, which shows the area from the Meadow Creek Mountains in Tennessee along the French Broad River to Asheville, N.C., appeared with the "Round About Asheville" article in the October 1889 issue. The last, from December 1997, is a two-sided map of Mesoamerica published with "The Royal Crypts of Copan."
"Maps are intimately intertwined with the culture" of the National Geographic Society, said Allen Carroll, chief cartographer at National Geographic Maps. "The society's more-than-century-long mission is to increase and diffuse geographic knowledge," he added. "People tend to think of photographs first, but I hope they also think of maps."
The National Geographic Society, which has sponsored exploration and anthropological and environmental research, formed its own cartographic division in 1915. Its maps have since set the standard: they are dense with raw geographical data on the physical features of the world and the names and symbols humans use to place themselves in that world. The maps in this collection offer a window onto history, war, culture, science and commerce.
And they can be downright beautiful. "Bird Migration in the Americas" (August 1979) shows the migration routes of birds from North America to South America with arrows sweeping down through the bottleneck of Central America and fanning out toward Tierra del Fuego. The continents are surrounded by drawings of the ruby-throated hummingbird, the wandering albatross, the blue-and-white sparrow and 64 other species, in full, flying color.
Where the maps alone do not tell the story, there are insets. A two-sided map that accompanied "Panama: Ever at the Crossroads" (April 1986) has a political map of Central America on one side and insets and several smaller maps on the other, with insights into archeological sites and the European invasions of the region by explorers. It also shows the economic outlook, with a map showing things like natural resources.
The CD-ROM collection also offers users historical perspectives. A 1922 map of the world contains regions at both poles marked "unexplored." "The Antarctic Regions," a 1932 map, shows the Polar Plateau with a largely empty interior, traced with the routes of the early explorers: Shackleton, Amundsen, Scott. "Antarctica" (1987) is fully mapped, with international scientific bases and detailed topographical information like ice thicknesses and mountains and glaciers, including Amundsen Glacier and Grosvenor Mountains (named for Gilbert H. Grosvenor, the first editor of The National Geographic magazine and the society's second president).
Among the many other map pairings that will interest history enthusiasts are the theater of war in 1918, paired with a 1922 map showing "Countries as Established by the Peace Conference at Paris," and a map of the races of Europe and Africa from 1918, with "Dawn of Humans" (1997).
To provide users with context, the software includes a history of cartography ("from the first line drawn in the sand") and a history of the National Geographic Society.
There are also nine video tours, on topics like European history, Canadian history and the environment. "You can't assume that everyone who picks one of these maps up is a map geek," said Bob Madden, editorial director of the CD-ROM set.
The set's most cumbersome aspect -- that it is included on eight CD-ROM's, which users must slip in and out of the drive to view the maps -- has been mitigated by several search and sorting provisions. The maps can be searched on any of the CD-ROM's by categories like politics or history, and by geographic regions like North America or space. Each map can be viewed in a thumbnail version, and users can call up a summary with the map's title, publication date, size and geographic names; a list of the insets, and the title of the article (if any) relating to the map. Users can also keep several maps open on their screen, even after switching CD-ROM's.
The printing feature of the CD-ROM set is flexible: each map can be sized to fit a standard page or printed on several pieces of paper. The printouts are clear and easy to read, and the subtle colors reproduce well. There is little to complain about in this software, but it would be nice if the map summary had a synopsis of the articles the maps appeared with and if the summary could be called up from the map image and not just from the search page.
Navigation within the maps is typical of map software: the viewer can zoom in and out, using a thumbnail image to center the map in the viewing window and a Find button to search for places or insets. The maps can also be "turned over" so users can see the back of any two-sided map.
Users can view a list of alternative place names since borders, cities and countries on maps have changed with wars, colonialism and independence movements: Burma became Myanmar, for example, and St. Petersburg became Leningrad, and then St. Petersburg again.
"Maps present a hierarchy of information," Mr. Carroll said. "You need a mental or visual starting point. With a map of central Texas, there are a lot of lines and squiggles and names, but if Houston and Dallas are in big type, you can say, 'I know where I am.' "
Map-making today combines technology with graphic traditions, Mr. Carroll added. "If the information is not presented properly," he said, "it is almost worthless: counterintuitive software has the important stuff buried, and a poorly designed map can be a jumble of confusing code. We've all encountered ugly software, and the same is true of maps. Maps should be as accurate as possible and as beautiful as possible."

HEADLINE: Beyond Geography: Mapping Unknowns Of Cyberspace

BYLINE: By PAMELA LICALZI O'CONNELL 

 BODY:
THE mapping of that vast territory known as cyberspace has begun in earnest.
Cyberspace maps are being produced by geographers, cartographers, artists and computer scientists. They range from glorious depictions of globe-spanning communications networks to maps of Web information. 
 Many have no geographic references, instead turning to nature, the cosmos or neuroscience for spatial models. They stretch the definition of a map in their effort to capture, sometimes fancifully, what is sometimes referred to as the "common mental geography" that lies beyond computer screens.
The maps hold the potential to change, subtly or perhaps more directly, the relationship of the average person to cyberspace, the world of electronic communication that includes but is not limited to the Internet. How people envision the on-line landscape influences their behavior there, experts say.
"We need maps not just to navigate but to define and control new territory," said Martin Dodge, a researcher in the Center for Advanced Spatial Analysis at University College London. "Simply having a map allows a new perspective, a new way to orient yourself. Relationships otherwise obscure may be revealed."
The largest collection of maps can be found at Mr. Dodge's Web site, An Atlas of Cyberspaces (cybergeography.com). The dozens of examples there include many that arise from science and instrumentation, while others are more products of imagination.
"We are in the very early stage of map making -- these maps are far from perfect at the moment," Mr. Dodge said. "Most have been produced outside traditional cartography by people such as data visualization researchers who may not even call their results a map."
Maps of cyberspace fall into two categories: those depicting the physical structure and information traffic patterns of global networks, and those addressing the content and social spaces of the electronic world.
Structural maps, although seemingly straightforward, have proved quite thorny to create. Since the National Science Foundation relinquished its stewardship of the Internet in 1995, there has been no central source of information about the Net's backbone networks and traffic. Instead, there is a jumble of networks owned by phone companies and Internet service providers, some of which do not share information, for competitive reasons.
The Cooperative Association for Internet Data Analysis, or Caida, at the University of California at San Diego develops tools to collect and analyze data about the Net, like the specific paths a test packet of information may follow.
"We are not at a point yet where we are drawing maps with any compelling utility for any particular community," said K. C. Claffy, a researcher with the association, via E-mail. "Though at the same time I contend we are doing 'state of the art' stuff at the frontier of this field. I just think the field's pretty young right now."
Useful or not, the Internet analysis group's maps can be quite beautiful. Some look like pointillist paintings. Others have brightly colored grids and globe-spanning arches, suggesting a world that is deeply, even inescapably, interconnected.
Connectivity, not necessarily geography or distance, is the primary parameter of a cyberspace map, said Gregory C. Staple, president of Telegeography, a company in Washington that provides statistics and maps of the telecommunications industry. His company has produced maps of "telecontinents," redrawing countries and coastlines to show how the flow of communications has created a new economic geography. At a glance, these maps reveal areas that are the telecommunications haves and have-nots, which Mr. Staple calls "the deep-water ports and teledeserts" of the information age (there are many more deserts than ports). Telegeography also offers maps of the Internet through its Peacock Maps subsidiary (peacockmaps.com).
Geography is completely shunned by some mappers, like William Cheswick, a researcher at Lucent Technologies' Bell Laboratories. "I want to take the Net on its own terms -- it's its own space," Mr. Cheswick argued. "Geographical maps I've seen have not worked very well. They're very blobby."
His maps, like those from the Cooperative Association for Internet Data Analysis, are based on information probes sent to tens of thousands of destinations on the Net. Maps resulting from such probes are featured on his Web site (www.cs.bell-labs.com/who/ches/map /index.html). They look something like promiscuously propagating sea ferns, with countless feathery vines.
Are they useful? Although his maps may inspire awe because of their complexity, Mr. Cheswick explains that until such maps are interactive, allowing access to the database that created them, they are more powerful as symbols, perhaps, than as maps.
Most so-called content maps are interactive and are focused on the portion of cyberspace now most populated: the Web. They are, Mr. Staple said, "the equivalent of land-use maps in the traditional cartographic world."
A new site called Newsmaps.com (newsmaps.com) creates "visual information landscapes" -- grid-based topographical maps -- of Web-based news articles and on-line discussion groups on topics like fears of year 2000 computer problems. Peaks appear on the map where there is a high concentration of documents or messages about the same topic. The distance between peaks shows how topics are related.
In a different approach, the artificial intelligence laboratory in the management information systems department at the University of Arizona at Tucson (ai.bpa.arizona.edu/start.html) drew a map of more than 100,000 entertainment Web sites pulled from Yahoo's database using an automatic-indexing system. The map looks something like a jigsaw puzzle with colored pieces. Users can select categories like movies or comics, or terms like "love" or "beer," and the map will adjust, "shifting down a layer" until a customized map listing specific sites is reached, explained Dr. Hsinchun Chen, head of the laboratory.
Lee Boot doesn't deal in math-based systems. Mr. Boot, a self-described "telemedia" artist who teaches film, video and computer art to high school students in Baltimore County, Md., recently created what he calls a Web Map About Teen-Agers' Happiness (www.bcpl .net/lboot/webmap2). "Mapping and diagramming is basic to the kind of art that I do," Mr. Boot said. "My map is crafted rather than automatic."
The map itself suggests doodles from a brainstorming session. Words of different sizes and colors are arranged within seemingly penciled circles, and lines indicate relationships between terms. A click on a word (say, "drugs") takes you to a site Mr. Boot has chosen on his theme of teen-age happiness. It is, he writes on his site, an attempt both to create "a visual presence" for an issue and to suggest his feelings about it.
Mr. Boot's map was included in an on-line exhibition titled Omnizone: Mapping Perspectives of Digital Culture (www.plexus.org/omnizone), in which artists were asked to contribute "artworks that function (however obtusely) as maps of digital space and culture," a guideline to which most contributors religiously adhered. Another on-line exhibition, an ongoing effort by the Guggenheim Museum called Cyberatlas, set its sites a little broader by not limiting the maps included to those dealing with cultural content (cyberatlas .guggenheim.org/home/index.html). Almost all the maps in both exhibitions are interactive.
"We need to find representations of cyberspace that encourage us to see advantages we can exploit -- ways we can bend and stretch the metaphor of space into new possibilities," said Jon Ippolito, the assistant curator of media arts for the Guggenheim and organizer of the Cyberatlas project. "We don't want to be stuck in older geometries."
Is a map of cyberspace, particularly the Web, needed at all? Lev Manovich, an artist and assistant professor in the visual arts department at the University of California at San Diego, argues that the hyperlinked Web "is in a way its own map -- its own reality."
"It's not like a painting, which is a representation of reality," he said.
Still, some people see it as something close to a moral imperative to develop cyberspace maps.
Bruce Sterling, a science fiction author and self-described pop-science journalist, says that such maps could alter our experience of the on-line world. "The best maps won't be true to our experience -- they will serve to refigure and change our experience," he said. "What makes the invisible visible? New metaphors, sometimes, but I'd be betting on better instrumentation. A picture of the Earth from space did more for environmental awareness than any number of ecological urban legends."
The era of personal maps that can provide that sort of perspective is approaching. A company called Invisible Worlds (invisible.net), founded by two men who have helped developed many Internet technology standards, Carl Malamud and Marshall Rose, is readying the underlying technology that will allow such maps to be created and shared. Although the demonstration on the Web site is not visually compelling, the company hopes that independent developers will create various appealing interfaces as the technology catches on.
"Having maps will be like turning on the lights on the information," Mr. Malamud said. "Regular people can experience the structure of the Net as a tool they can use. We can create a conception of space that is good and usable."
The essence of Invisible Worlds' idea is to provide an Up button for people traveling in cyberspace to go along with the current, and restrictively linear, Forward and Back controls. By providing a vista from above, Mr. Malamud said, the user's "viewpoint becomes one of the parameters of the map, or even the key parameter."

HEADLINE: TURF;
As Market Booms, Real Estate Brokers Slip Their Chains

BYLINE: By TRACIE ROZHON 

DATELINE: ROSCOE, N.Y.

 BODY:
BOB LAMBRIGGER, 61, the owner of a small real estate company here in self-proclaimed Trout Town, U.S.A., wheeled his pickup truck into the dirt driveway of a printing contractor. He was picking up his new brochures -- the ones without the Century 21 logo.
Over the summer, Mr. Lambrigger cut the cord binding his company to the national chain. "I felt my agents were bringing in all the business themselves, and I was left with just paying the fees," said Mr. Lambrigger, who five years ago paid $25,000 for a five-year franchise, and until August, surrendered 8 percent of every sales commission to the chain. 
 When he signed up, Mr. Lambrigger said he thought the real estate chains, brand names like Century 21, Prudential and Coldwell Banker, were the wave of the future. But now, he's not so sure. With business booming in his little four-agent office, he said he could make as much money as he had with the affiliation, and keep all the commissions.
Mr. Lambrigger thought that Century 21 was going to send him customers, what in the trade are called referrals, from all over the country. He thought the company's Web site would be another draw. "But up here in the country, we ended up feeling abandoned," he said. "All the stuff they promised us didn't materialize, and we hated writing those monthly checks when it was all our own hard work."
Mr. Lambrigger is not alone.
For the first time since 1978, the percentage of local companies affiliated with the large national real estate companies has decreased. According to statistics about to be released by the National Association of Realtors in Washington, independent local companies affiliated with franchises increased steadily, from 17 percent in 1978 to 37 percent in 1996, the last time the association did a national survey.
But for 1999, the figure has dropped to just over 33 percent, not a big drop, but a significant one, said Kevin Roth, a senior economist for the association. "We think it's a trend," he added.
But Ted Deutsch, a spokesman for Cendant, the conglomerate that owns Century 21, ERA and Coldwell Banker, said that the drop was only part of the picture, adding that he believes the total dollar amount of real estate sold by the affiliates, compared with the independents, is probably on the increase. But he conceded that during a hot real estate market like today's, companies might not feel so much pressure to come under a corporate umbrella and share their commissions, but instead drop out of an affiliation or not sign up at all.
Advocates of franchise affiliation insist that the consumer benefits when dealing with a nationally known name. In some cases, they say, the chain or its corporate parent owns a mortgage company, security company or other service and can deliver "one stop shopping." (Cendant, for instance, owns Avis, Welcome Wagon and a mortgage company.) And perhaps most significant, they point to the chains' national referral network, allowing a Prudential seller in California access to Prudential listings in New York City, for instance, making the transition from coast to coast smoother.
But brokers who resist affiliation argue that it can be a costly waste of bookkeeping time, with no benefit at all to the customer, and can even distract a broker from the business of helping a buyer find a home. In the end, agents say, it all boils down to where the customer is looking for a house.
"I've no doubt that in some parts of the country, a national affiliation can be valuable" to real estate companies, said Frederick W. Peters, the former co-chairman of the Real Estate Board of New York's residential division. "It puts a stamp of respectability and even gives some marketing clout. And if the office is properly located, it can benefit from a stream of referral business."
In Manhattan, he continued, national chains have had a hard time getting started. "New Yorkers are by nature contrarian," he said. "A big national chain that plays well in Peoria makes them think it's not sophisticated or cool enough" for New York buyers and sellers.
Nevertheless, owners of large Manhattan companies, most of whom said they had been repeatedly approached by the chains, explained that they were keeping an eye on the progress of one local agency, Hunt Kennedy, which three years ago became Coldwell Banker Hunt Kennedy. There, JoAnne Kennedy said, business went up 53 percent since affiliation with the chain, which has 2,800 offices nationwide. Last year, she said, she got 120 deals from referrals, compared with fewer than 20 before then. She said the wider scope allowed her to offer customers the use of the chain's national Web site and to benefit from promotions created at the chain's headquarters.
Mr. Deutsch, the spokesman for Cendant, whose three real estate companies do 20 to 25 percent of the country's real estate business, said that those companies combined lost more than 100 agencies in the last year. He declined to say how many new agencies signed up.
But he argued that an average of 90 percent of Cendant's affiliates renew their affiliations for 5 or 10 years, depending on their contracts. Spokesmen for Better Homes and Gardens and Prudential, two other national chains, declined to produce statistics on dropouts or sign-ups.
Mr. Lambrigger said that he thought small, very local companies like his could give better service to the consumer if their owners didn't have to worry about the paperwork necessary to being part of a chain. There were also daylong meetings that he and other owners were supposed to attend at the company's New Jersey headquarters, taking more time from his work.
At Cendant, Mr. Deutsch acknowledged that the company had reduced the number of field representatives who visit and counsel local affiliates. But he argued that a $25 million investment in improving electronic communications from headquarters made Cendant's service to local franchises "better than ever."
Mrs. Kennedy, the Coldwell Banker booster from Manhattan, agreed with the Catskills broker that "the tone definitely changed" when Cendant took over the business and decreased the number of visits in the field.
At first, she also worried that her co-op customers would have difficulty overcoming Coldwell Banker's suburban image, which she labeled "the house and the tree," although she says house hunters have become more familiar with the brand name in Manhattan. And there were problems at first about finding Manhattan properties on the company's Web sites. She said that those have now been solved.
Clark Halstead, who heads the Halstead Property Company, New York's third-largest real estate organization, refers to the affiliations as Rent-a-Brand. He maintains that an affiliation with Century 21, Prudential, Remax or the other national chains "dilutes the name of a good local agency, rather than adds to it," making it less attractive to customers.
Mr. Halstead maintained that the chains with a New York presence -- Prudential and Coldwell Banker -- are not "injecting any money or capital for advertising or anything else" into their local affiliates.
Mrs. Kennedy is the first to agree. "We give them money," she said wryly. "The biggest thing is the brand name recognition, and that's really powerful."
During a visit, she pulled out Coldwell Banker's brochure (sold to her at a discount price), advertising the chain's new "concierge service," where the agent supervises things like turning on the gas, electric and cable service, and getting the apartment cleaned for the housewarming.
Mr. Halstead was, not surprisingly, skeptical. "But the agents already do that," he grumbled. "I can't tell you how many times I've been trying to find one of my agents and been told she's waiting for the cable guy to show up!"

HEADLINE: Rise of 0.9% In Durable Goods Orders

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 29

 BODY:
Orders to factories for big-ticket manufactured goods unexpectedly rose nine-tenths of a percent in August, led by an increase in demand for airplanes, automobiles and other transportation equipment, a Government report said today.
The Commerce Department report said orders for durable goods -- items expected to last at least three years -- grew in August to a seasonally adjusted $206.9 billion. 
 The increase followed a revised gain of 4 percent in July, which was larger than the Government had previously estimated. July's performance was the biggest one-month gain since November 1997, when durable-goods orders rose 4.4 percent.
August's performance, the 12th increase in the last 15 months, was the reverse of the expectations of many analysts, who had, on average, estimated that orders would fall nine-tenths of a percent.
"This report indicates that manufacturers, which have been suffering, continue to recover and the prospects for the future are that they will continue to improve," said Mark Zandi, an economist with Regional Financial Associates.
Despite the strong report, Mr. Zandi and other analysts said they did not expect the Federal Reserve to raise interest rates for a third time this year when it meets next Tuesday.
Orders for transportation equipment posted the biggest gain in August, rising 4.6 percent to the highest level since May, when orders rose 9.9 percent. That was the fourth consecutive increase. In July, orders for transportation equipment rose 2 percent.
The transportation sector is often volatile from month to month because it includes items like aircraft. The increase in transportation orders comes as the auto industry expects record sales in 1999.
August's overall increase came despite decreases in some areas, including industrial machinery, electronics and electrical equipment.
Excluding transportation, overall orders for durable goods fell two-tenths of a percent in August, the fifth decrease in the last eight months.
While on the rebound, American manufacturers have been battered by a global financial crisis, which has cut sharply into their overseas sales and opened them up to competition from less expensive imports.
The overall economy, however, has continued to thrive, powered by strong consumer demand.
Shipments of big-ticket durable goods, a good sign of current demand, rose 1.3 percent in August.

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: ADVERTISING;
Dot-com companies have built their brands by using more ads, in good taste or not. Often not.

BYLINE: By Patricia Winters Lauro 

 BODY:
ONE television commercial for Outpost.com featured gerbils shot out of a cannon. Another, for Ecampus.com, offered a college kid belching the alphabet.
This is dot-com -- home of some of the best and worst advertising in the industry. Dot-com advertising seems to be the place where practically no bodily function is too private and no rude behavior too coarse to be featured in a spot. It is also the habitat of clever and ingenious creativity that takes risks in the high-stakes race for category dominance on the Internet. 
 "First and foremost the job of dot-com advertising is to gain top-of-mind awareness," said Brian Mulhern, senior vice president at Cliff Freeman & Partners in New York, a unit of Saatchi & Saatchi P.L.C. that creates Outpost.com advertising. "When I sit down to buy on the Internet, if I don't remember the actual name of the site and remember it first, then there's no chance that that company is going to get my sale."
And, appropriate for the Internet, speed is everything. The speed at which an Internet company can establish brand awareness is key. Some five million new households will come on line this year, bringing the number of Internet-accessible households in the United States to 39 million, according to Charlene Li, senior analyst with Forrester Research.
"It's very important to establish brand awareness right now," said Ms. Li. "It's all about capturing and retaining customers."
While taking risks is anathema in the corporate world, dot-com advertisers say there is little choice. They have a lot of money from venture capital and initial public offerings and an edict from investors to acquire customers fast.
"These are companies that need to create brands within very short order," said Ellis Verdi, president of DeVito/Verdi in New York, which creates the Ecampus.com advertising. "Wall Street is expecting results in months, and that almost requires that they push the envelope. There's a sense that there's one big gold pile out there and everyone is running for it."
During the first six months of 1999, companies advertising on-line services spent $755 million, up from $230 million for the first half of 1998, according to Competitive Media Reporting, which tracks advertising spending. More spending is projected as the fourth quarter shapes up to be a crucial time for many dot-com retailers that are trying to grab holiday shopping sales for the first time.
"With increased competition among Web sites -- and there seems to be a million of them -- you have to be out there in the marketplace just to be seen, and whoever shouts loudest will come out on top," said Eric Goldberg, a media planner with DeWitt Media in New York.
Mr. Goldberg predicts that heavy dot-com advertising will contribute to a tight media inventory next year, which could push up prices.
Because Web companies and services are new, Ms. Lee said the advertising did not have to protect existing brand images. And because the companies are part of the Internet, Ms. Lee said consumers understand that Web advertisers have more "license to be wacky."
A sampling:
*A commercial for Beyond.com, tagged "the place to buy software," features a corporate drop-out who works in the nude at home, much to the chagrin of neighbors.
*To get its tag -- "Textbooks and stuff. Cheap" -- across, Ecampus.com shows a college student so broke that he resorts to frying a pet goldfish for dinner.
*In a spot for Outpost.com, a pack of wolves is released among a high school marching band after it forms the word Outpost.com. The reason? To get viewers "to remember our name," according to an announcer.
The companies do not seem overly concerned about offending people. Mr. Mulhern said Outpost.com received about 17,000 letters concerning the spot that featured the gerbils, with 60 percent expressing support for the ad. Only a handful of consumers even commented on the band commercial, he said.
Still, one inherent danger of bizarre advertising is that viewers will not know what is being advertised, Mr. Verdi said.
With that in mind, Mr. Mulhern said that Outpost.com's second flurry of ads -- spoofs of 1950's science- fiction movies -- promotes various benefits the company offers, including free overnight delivery of goods.
"We seeded our name with the original campaign, now we really need to differentiate the company from everyone else," he said.
A lot of the advertising needs to be sharp because it is intended to reach hip, young people. Boo.com, a site that will begin operations later this year, will sell sports apparel and footwear and offer advertising that features anti-fashion figures: computer geeks. TV ads to be shown in November will depict geeks trying desperately to participate in sports. Print ads, taken from the television spots, feature scenes like a geek in running shorts on a stretcher.
Karen Shapiro, Boo.com director of marketing, says geeks are cool and the perfect anti-status image. NBC recently began a TV series called "Freaks and Geeks." "Geeks have become heroes, they're chic," she said.
The outrageousness of some dot-com advertising has forced brick-and-mortar companies like Staples Inc. to reconsider whether their on-line advertising is outrageous enough -- and they haven't always opted to go that extra step.
"When we looked at the landscape of on-edge advertising, we saw that they were doing things to get people's attention and perhaps shock them into logging on," said Jeanne Lewis, president of the Staples.com unit. "But we realized we didn't need to do that because we already have a creative strategy with our existing advertising that does get noticed."
Still, Ms. Lewis said she appreciated a lot of the dot-com advertising for its edginess.
"I think the ideal is to combine the newest techniques out there with some good basic marketing fundamentals that can't be forgotten," Ms. Lewis said.

HEADLINE: Avon's Stock Declines 27% After Warning About Profit

BYLINE: By DANA CANEDY 

 BODY:
Avon Products Inc. yesterday became the latest multinational consumer products company to be pummeled by investors after management announced a surprise warning that it expected to report disappointing financial results.
Avon shares fell $9.875, or 27.6 percent, to close at $25.8125 after the company said it was suffering from weak economies abroad, currency volatility and stiff domestic competition. The announcement came a day after the Gillette Company made a similar announcement. 
 Avon, the door-to-door cosmetics seller, said it would meet expectations of earning 34 cents a share in the third quarter, which ends today, but would probably be short of analysts' estimates for the fourth quarter. Analysts had expected profit of 63 cents a share in the fourth quarter, but Avon said yesterday that it expected profits to be even with or only slightly higher than the 56 cents earned in the period a year ago.
Avon, which is based in New York, gets two-thirds of its profits from outside the United States but is being hit by domestic woes as well.
Largely because of a currency devaluation in Brazil and increased competition and heavy sales discounts in Mexico, "results are not expected to reach the level previously anticipated" in Latin America, said Charles R. Perrin, chairman and chief executive.
"In addition, in the U.S., trends in beauty sales in the fourth quarter are expected to improve significantly over third quarter, but we are seeing weakness in early sales of holiday gifts, causing us to revise the sales outlook in the U.S.," Mr. Perrin said. Avon had expected domestic revenue to increase by 5 percent but now says it will not meet that target.
For the year, Avon expects to report a double-digit increase in profit over last year's $1.48 a share. Analysts polled by First Call had expected the company to earn $1.71 but revised the number yesterday to $1.64. Some also downgraded the stock, despite Avon's assurances that it was adopting a growth strategy and expected sales growth to pick up next year.
In the United States market, the company is facing an image problem as it tries to further broaden its appeal beyond customers loyal to the Avon Lady, analysts said. "I've never seen a successful attempt to take a brand that is on one level and try to take it up market," said Melissa Grant, an analyst with Warburg Dillon Read L.L.C. LIke such formidable competing brands as Revlon and Cover Girl, Avon is a well-known franchise but suffers from a low-brow image that has long "had a certain stigma attached to it," Ms. Grant said.
Yet while investors were clearly caught off guard by the weakness on Avon's home turf, they were reacting in large part to the troubles abroad. "The problem with the stock today is that there is a lot of confusion over what is going to happen next year and whether Latin America is going to be a lingering problem," said Robert Izmirlian, an analyst with S&P Equity Group.
Avon's warning came a day after Gillette, the Boston-based maker of razors and blades, similarly stunned investors by forecasting bad news. Gillette said after the market closed on Tuesday that results in its third quarter would not meet forecasts because of weak economies abroad, currency translation issues and weakness in its Braun and stationery businesses. Investors sent the stock to a three-year low yesterday at $33.4375, down $3.5625, or 9.6 percent. Shares of other consumer giants that have recently had trouble abroad, including the Procter & Gamble Company, also declined yesterday. P.& G.'s stock closed at $95.8125, down $3.125, or 3.2 percent.
"I think this is the kind of thing we need to expect more in the future," Mr. Izmirlian said. In times of economic turmoil abroad, "it's not always easy to assess the magnitude of the effect on a company," he said. "In the past, these companies really had some steep multiples; going forward, investors need to be a little more cautious."

HEADLINE: A Corporate Chess Game;
Copper Industry Rivals in Nasty, Convoluted Negotiations

BYLINE: By LAURA M. HOLSON 

 BODY:
Last Friday, top executives from three of the nation's top copper companies secretly convened in a room at the Trump International Hotel in Manhattan to try to resolve one of the nastiest, most convoluted corporate takeover fights in recent years. When they emerged more than two hours later, it was nastier and more convoluted than ever.
The meeting brought together Douglas Yearley, chairman of Phelps Dodge, the nation's largest copper producer, with the chief executives of two competitors, Asarco Inc. and the Cyprus Amax Minerals Company, that had agreed last July to merge with each other. Mr. Yearley, rather than see his smaller rivals join hands, had started his own $2.8 billion hostile offer to acquire both of them for Phelps Dodge. Finally, after weeks of rebuffing Mr. Yearley, Francis McAllister of Asarco and Milton Ward of Cyprus Amax had agreed to talk. 
 Mr. Yearley presented his best offer, he recalled. But he was not willing to meet their demand -- $3.1 billion for the combination. When the meeting broke up, Mr. Yearley was still determined to own both companies and Mr. McAllister and Mr. Ward, eager to protect their interests, quickly began a search for a white knight, adding yet another player to the corporate chess game.
Six months ago, a brawl in the copper industry was unthinkable. Ailing economies in Asia and Latin America had left demand for copper waning. Prices plummeted to a 12-year low then -- a paltry 63 cents a pound. Profits at copper mining companies tumbled, with some companies forced to cut production to help arrest the price slide. In fact, it was the industry's bleak prospects that proved to be the catalyst that prompted Mr. McAllister to talk to Mr. Ward in the first place last May.
How quickly that world has changed. Copper prices have risen 33 percent since then. Now, Asarco and Cyprus Amax, instead of being left alone to embrace each other out of desperation, have, in effect, become much more valuable prizes on the auction block.
Indeed, as soon as they walked out of the meeting last Friday, the two sought a bid from Grupo Mexico, a Mexican copper producer and Asarco's largest shareholder. Grupo Mexico, which had been watching the action for a while, immediately offered to pay $1.03 billion in cash for Asarco, barely edging out Phelps Dodge's $1.01 billion stock and cash offer. Asarco and Cyprus Amax, essentially acknowledging that their original deal was no longer viable, postponed shareholder votes scheduled for today on the merger, giving each other until Tuesday to reconsider their options.
The four-way fight is indicative of what has happened in stagnant commodity-driven industries seeking some way to shore up production without losing their shirts in the process. Low prices in slow-growing industries, including oil, aluminum, copper and chemicals, have made even the most unattractive partners appealing to formidable competitors hoping to cut costs and restore profitability to their slim-margin businesses. And with 1980's style corporate takeover tactics back in vogue, properties expected to benefit from rising prices are now worth fighting over.
"When copper was 65 cents per pound, no one thought anyone would have been fighting over these two companies," said Tom Burnett, founder of Merger Insight, which is based in New York. "The worm has turned. Commodity prices are on the move and companies don't want to be left behind."
Spokesmen for Asarco and Grupo Mexico said executives would not comment on recent events. Executives at Cyprus Amax did not return phone calls. But people involved in the talks said Cyprus Amax had been scouting for another partner and could possibly strike a deal soon with Phelps Dodge. Asarco, too, has been negotiating with other suitors this week.
Mr. McAllister of Asarco, who became chief executive last spring, never planned for his company to become a target, executives close to him said. Instead his solution to Asarco's woes was for him to act as buyer, squeezing savings out of any merged entity. Mr. McAllister wanted to turn Asarco's fortunes around, one person said, and he found a willing partner in the older Mr. Ward, who was looking for a successor.
Several people involved in the negotiations said Mr. Ward had already approached a handful of potential merger partners for Cyprus Amax last year. So it was with little fanfare on July 15 that Mr. Ward and Mr. McAllister announced that their companies would join forces in a merger of equals, valued then at $737 million in stock. Stock traders yawned at the news: the stock of both companies dropped 3 percent on the day the deal was announced.
Analysts at the time said the ho-hum response came because shareholders of neither company were paid a premium and they did not understand how the duo could successfully improve profit margins without cutting production. "People wanted to see how they'd perform before they bid up the stock," said Ali Agha, a metals and mining analyst at Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette. But the merger did catch the attention of Mr. Yearley, who said he thought a three-way merger with Asarco and Cyprus Amax could be just the ticket to make Phelps Dodge the premier low-cost global copper producer.
Phelps Dodge is one of the few copper companies to turn a profit. And after mulling over the combination, he said he called both Mr. Ward and Mr. McAllister on Aug. 10 to propose they meet. The two rebuffed his offer, saying their merger agreement barred them from independently talking to other suitors. Still, Mr. Yearley followed up with a letters to the chief executives and boards, requesting a reply within a week.
None came, until the two companies publicly dismissed the Phelps Dodge offer on Aug. 20 and reconfirmed their own merger. So Phelps Dodge went public with a sweeter offer that day, offering stock for the pair with a combined value of $2.6 billion, a 30 percent premium to the companies' trading prices the previous day. Five days later, Asarco and Cyprus Amax snubbed that bid, suggesting they would be willing to negotiate only if Phelps Dodge put a much better deal on the table.
To deter stockholder interest in the Phelps Dodge bid, Asarco and Cyprus Amax also sweetened the terms of their own merger, offering an extra payment of $5 a share in cash once the deal was completed. Undaunted, Phelps Dodge began a tender offer for shares in the two companies on Sept. 3.
Mr. Yearley was not the only interested party, however. Grupo Mexico, Mexico's largest diversified mining company, owned a 9.7 percent stake in Asarco, the largest single holding. Its chairman, German Larrea, watched the brouhaha intensely. Not only was he interested in getting the best price for his shares, executives said, he was also potentially interested in a piece of the action himself. Mr. Larrea, according to documents recently filed with the Securities and Exchange Commission, held separate meetings in late August and early September in Mexico City with Mr. McAllister of Asarco. Executives from Phelps Dodge also tried to woo him to vote for their bid, Mr. Yearley said.
At the same time, Mr. Larrea was an attentive suitor. An executive involved with the company said Mr. Larrea "didn't want to do something hostile" after Asarco's announcement in July. But now, with Phelps Dodge in the picture, he said, Mr. Larrea "perceived himself as the good guy." A spokesman for Mr. Larrea said he was not granting interviews.
Mr. Larrea had a problem, though. Because Grupo Mexico's stock did not trade in the United States, it would have to offer cash. So the company hired Chase Securities Inc. as its adviser and to arrange financing for its bid.
Enthusiasm for Phelps Dodge's offer was growing, meanwhile, on Wall Street, particularly after the company increased its bids again, on Sept. 22, to a stock-and-cash offer worth about $2.8 billion for the two companies together. Regulators were believed to be giving the nod to the three-way merger, despite initial antitrust concerns, according to executives involved in the talks.
Asarco and Cyprus Amax, after finally agreeing to meet Mr. Yearley, laid out several demands at the session last Friday. First, Mr. Yearley said, they wanted Phelps Dodge to pay a 55 percent premium to their stocks' prices before the Phelps Dodge bid on Aug. 20. Next, they wanted Phelps Dodge to take the risk of any antitrust approval. They also wanted to avoid paying a $45 million breakup fee for canceling their merger. Top executives' pay packages would have to be honored. If Mr. Yearley could agree to those terms, he had a deal.
Mr. Yearley said he agreed to almost everything except price. Already Phelps Dodge was offering about a 40 percent premium to buy the companies, "we thought we'd gone far enough," he said.
But the skirmish took an unexpected twist later that afternoon. A representative of Credit Suisse First Boston, which was advising Asarco, contacted Mr. Larrea of Grupo Mexico, documents filed with the S.E.C. show, and made him an offer: Asarco would be willing to consider "an all-cash firm offer for all of its shares at $26 per share." Cyprus Amax was mentioned, too, but Grupo Mexico was not interested, an executive involved with the companies said.
An executive involved in the talks said Asarco executives did not know that Mr. Larrea was interested in buying the company -- despite having had several discussions with key executives at Asarco -- describing the call as "one of those interesting coincidences."
That night, Grupo Mexico announced its plans to start a tender offer for Asarco for $26 a share, slightly higher than the $25.47 Phelps Dodge had proposed two days earlier. "As you know, in the past we have expressed an interest in exploring the possibility of a combination of our two companies' operations," Mr. Larrea wrote in a letter to Mr. McAllister that evening. "Briefly put, we believe our proposal is in the best interest of your shareholders."
As advisers and executives sort through the offers this week, it is not yet clear which company will buy Asarco or Cyprus Amax, or if they end up together.
But ultimately, one banker involved in the deal said, "It was a good attempt that didn't work out that well." More to the point, he added, "Although it will end up being good for shareholders, it may not be necessarily be good for management."

HEADLINE: Crude Oil Up Again, Briefly Trading Over $25 a Barrel

BYLINE: By Bloomberg News 

 BODY:
The price of crude oil rose more than 1 percent yesterday, briefly trading above $25 a barrel for the first time in two and a half years, after a report of declining inventories signaled that the oil surpluses of early this year are gone.
United States crude oil supplies fell 1.1 percent, to the lowest level since January 1998, the American Petroleum Institute said. 
 Inventories have declined every week but two since mid-June, as exporters reduced production by about 7 percent. Prices have virtually doubled during the year.
"The U.S. is very lean on crude oil," said Marianne Kah, chief oil economist at Conoco Inc., the nation's fifth-biggest petroleum company. "It's the first time since 1996 that we've been very tight. When inventories get low, prices get high."
Crude oil for November delivery rose 36 cents, or 1.5 percent, to close at $24.69 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange. During the trading session, the price was up as much as 79 cents, to $25.12 a barrel, the highest since January 1997.
Oil company stocks followed crude oil prices higher. Shares of Texaco Inc. rose $1, to $62.3125, and the Phillips Petroleum Company rose $1.75, to $48.50.
"Oil's been stronger than I could have possibly imagined," said Charles Davidson, chairman of Wexford Management L.L.C. in Greenwich, Conn., a private investment fund. Mr. Davidson said that his fund had purchased the stocks and commercial paper of several energy companies during the spring, including the Range Resources Corporation, an independent oil company based in Fort Worth, which has nearly doubled in price since April.
Mr. Davidson said he thought that energy stocks could rise further, adding, "We've no plans to sell."
The American Petroleum Institute said after the end of trading yesterday that prices would continue to rise because oil inventories in the United States fell to 305.1 million barrels last week. Inventories have averaged 319 million barrels over the last five years.
Supplies have fallen 11 percent from their high for the year in April.
"We are fundamentally short of oil," said Alan Struth, an economist at Honeywell Bonner & Moore, a Houston-based refinery consulting firm. "If production stays around where it is, we could see $30 by the end of the year."
Gasoline inventories fell to 201.1 million barrels last week, the lowest level since October 1998 and down 9.3 percent from the year's high in March.

LENGTH: 1093 words

HEADLINE: HOCKEY: PREVIEW '99-'00;
Overtime Is Now Five Minutes in Hockey Heaven

BYLINE: By JOE LAPOINTE 

 BODY:
Shortly before the 28 teams opened their training camps, the National Hockey League executive Steve Solomon attended the revival of "Death of a Salesman" on Broadway. Solomon, second in command to Commissioner Gary Bettman, spied a hockey-friendly journalist at intermission and went into sell mode. The topic was the N.H.L.'s new four-on-four overtime.
"The most exciting five minutes in sports!" Solomon said. "It will be great." 
 It may be, but Solomon won't be around to hype it. A couple weeks later, he announced his resignation, effective tomorrow, the opening night of the N.H.L. season. Solomon is going to Univision, the Spanish television network, where he will head the sports division.
While Bettman seeks Solomon's replacement, the league skates on, with the Dallas Stars defending their first Stanley Cup and the 2000 playoffs set to end next June. Along with the new overtime concept, the N.H.L. has partly changed its video-review policy. No longer will television tape nullify apparently good goals because an attacking player had a skate in the goalie's crease. The decision regarding goalie interference will be made only by the referees, the way it used to be. And there will be two referees for 50 of each team's 82 regular-season games, up from 20 last season.
There will be a new team in Atlanta, in a new arena, with plenty of tickets sold for the Thrashers. The Carolina Hurricanes have a new arena in Raleigh, N.C., where tickets are not selling as well. The Los Angeles Kings have a new building, too, and so does the Colorado Avalanche. Locally, the Rangers signed six free agents and the Devils stood pat. The Islanders are still in their old building, with the same ownership considering a sale as the payroll shrinks, the season-ticket base dwindles and local television exposure remains uncertain. As for national TV, Fox is gone with all its incomprehensible camera angles and ABC is in with fewer regular-season games than Fox and camera work still to be determined. More games will be seen on ESPN and ESPN2, the cable partners of ABC and all under the Disney umbrella.
When the puck drops tomorrow, with the Rangers at Edmonton, it will be the first season in two decades without Wayne Gretzky, who began in 1979 with the Oilers and finished in 1999 with the Rangers. Gretzky and his No. 99 jersey will be honored in pregame ceremonies tomorrow night and he will enter the Hall of Fame in November. And this season could be the last for Hall-bound Dominik Hasek of Buffalo, who said he plans to retire. Hasek has won the Vezina trophy for the past three seasons as the league's best goalie. But Bettman emphasized that the returning younger stars include Paul Kariya of Anaheim, Jaromir Jagr of Pittsburgh and Eric Lindros of Philadelphia.
"The beauty of sports in all ages is that there is always another generation," Bettman said. As for rules changes, Bettman said, "The feedback we've gotten has been overwhelmingly positive" for the new overtime format. Under it, teams tied after 60 minutes will each get 1 point in the standing, as they always have for ties. The five-minute, sudden-death overtime will be played, however, with four skaters apiece, instead of five. If a team scores to win in overtime, it gets a second point in the standing for a victory while the losing team keeps its point for the tie in regulation.
Detroit's Scotty Bowman, the dean of N.H.L. coaches, said he is not sure how this will affect strategy.
"There's a lot of things you put in your mind," Bowman said. "What the standings are. Your opponent. How you're playing in the game. I'm not that sure of the 3-point game. It will cause a lot of parity. If you tied every game, you'd have 82 points, going for another 82. You might see some real offense for five minutes."
This should highlight players like Brian Leetch, the Ranger captain, who is a skilled attacker from his defensive position.
"I like the rule, it's exciting," Leetch said. "Teams will put skill players out there. It will help the league." Mike Richter, the Ranger goalie, said that eliminating video review for crease violations will lead to more collisions between attackers and goalies.
Off the ice, there are serious issues. With the collective bargaining agreement in place until 2004, some teams are resisting the wage inflation that has raised the average salary to almost $1.3 million. Among the stars in contract squabbles are: Aleksei Yashin of Ottawa, Keith Primeau of Carolina, Bill Guerin of Edmonton, Byron Dafoe of Boston and Darren McCarty of Detroit.
Free agents still seeking teams include Ulf Samuelsson, Pat Verbeek, Dmitri Khristich and Joe Murphy. Among old faces in new places: Theo Fleury from Calgary to Colorado to the Rangers; Zigmund Palffy from the Islanders to Los Angeles; Niklas Sundstrom from the Rangers to San Jose, via Tampa Bay, Grant Fuhr from St. Louis to Calgary. Kevin Lowe, the former Ranger and Oiler, is coaching Edmonton. Mario Lemieux, the former Pittsburgh star, now heads an ownership group that bought the Penguins out of bankruptcy.
Overall, the N.H.L. has not commanded the higher profile that seemed its destiny five years ago. After Bettman's first full season, 1993-94, the Rangers had won the Stanley Cup and Solomon was helping to negotiate big national television deals. Rap artists wore team jerseys and hockey seemed hip. The cute, new, successful expansion team was the Anaheim Mighty Ducks, founded by the Disney Company, an encouraging straw in the wind.
With baseball on strike that autumn, hockey had a wider space on the big sports stage. And then Bettman, negotiating a collective bargaining agreement, ordered a labor lockout that lasted 15 weeks. Perhaps it was only coincidental, but hockey's momentum seemed to slow.
Bettman, as sensitive to criticism as he is dedicated to his mission, is quick to counter perceptions that hockey has not attained its expected share of the American sports audience.
"There is tremendous fragmentation out there," Bettman said, referring to the sports marketplace, especially its television component. "The competition, in terms of distribution, is fiercer than ever before. On a regional basis, we continue to do great. We have a strong fan base. Our attendance has never been stronger. We're in more places than ever before. Go back to 1990. We were in 11 U.S. markets. For next season, we will be in 21. All of these things will take time. We've been heading in the right direction. It's a long road. We think we're on the right track."

HEADLINE: Billionaire, Once Refugee, Donates Millions to Museum

BYLINE: By IRVIN MOLOTSKY 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 29

 BODY:
A refugee from the 1956 Hungarian uprising who came penniless to the United States will give the Smithsonian Institution $60 million, its largest donation ever, the Smithsonian said today.
The donor, Steven F. Udvar-Hazy, became a billionaire by leasing airliners, and his gift to the Smithsonian will be directed to a new museum that is to open in four years at Dulles International Airport and that will contain objects too big -- like the space shuttle and a 747 jetliner -- for the Smithsonian's National Air and Space Museum on the Mall. 
 In return for the gift, the Smithsonian is expected to name the Dulles museum for Mr. Udvar-Hazy (pronounced OOD-vahr HAH-zee), perhaps at the next meeting of its governing board of regents in January.
Besides the space shuttle and the 747, the Dulles museum will also house the Enola Gay, the B-29 that dropped the atomic bomb on Hiroshima in 1945; an SR-71, once a secret spy plane, and a B-17, another World War II bomber.
Mr. Udvar-Hazy was traveling in Asia today and not available for comment, but the chairman of his leasing company, Leslie Gonda, described how Mr. Udvar-Hazy's knack for aviation had brought him a fortune.
"Even when he was an undergraduate at U.C.L.A., he gave classes to graduate students in airline management and administration," Mr. Gonda said.
It was at the University of California at Los Angeles that Mr. Udvar-Hazy learned that he could make money by buying an airplane from one company and selling it or leasing it to another.
In a 1990 interview with The Wall Street Journal, Mr. Udvar-Hazy said he learned in 1967 that Air New Zealand was trying to sell an Lockheed Electra and told the airline that he would sell it for a 5 percent commission. Air New Zealand agreed and Mr. Udvar-Hazy sold the airplane and made a $50,000 commission.
He was then 21 years old and he said that he carried on the deal via Telex because, he said, "I was afraid if someone saw me, they'd be disappointed in how young I was."
Mr. Udvar-Hazy is now 53 and earlier this year The Los Angeles Business Journal estimated his fortune at $1.6 billion.
In 1990, Mr. Udvar-Hazy, Mr. Gonda and Mr. Gonda's son, Louis, sold their company, the International Lease Finance Corporation, to American International Group, the insurance company, for $1.3 billion in cash and stock.
Mr. Udvar-Hazy and the elder Mr. Gonda stayed on to run the company for the new owners.
Mr. Gonda is also a Hungarian refugee, now 80, who lost much of his family in the Holocaust and arrived to the United States in 1963 after going to Venezuela in 1945.
He said Mr. Udvar-Hazy and his brother, Andre, were taken by their parents, Ernest and Ethel, to Sweden when the Soviet Union put down the Hungarian uprising in 1956.
The family then moved to New York City, where Mr. Udvar-Hazy attended Stuyvesant High School and then to California. Mr. Udvar-Hazy lives with his wife, Christine, their four children and his mother (his father has since died) in Beverly Hills, Calif.
Morten S. Beyer, chairman of MBA, an aviation consulting firm, said the company Mr. Udvar-Hazy founded is one of the largest owners of Boeing and Airbus planes, although it has no 747's like the one that will be installed at Dulles.
"He doesn't like the economics for the lessor," Mr. Beyer said of Mr. Udvar-Hazy's aversion to the 747.
After word of the donation got out, the secretary of the Smithsonian, I. Michael Heyman, noted that it was the latest in a series of "exemplary gifts to the institution."
The most recent was the bequest by Dr. Paul Singer of his huge collection of ancient Chinese artifacts to the Smithsonian's Arthur M. Sackler Gallery announced on Sept. 8. The collection has been valued at $50 million to $60 million.

HEADLINE: Detroit Silent As a Toyota Hits Market

BYLINE: By ROBYN MEREDITH 

DATELINE: DETROIT, Sept. 29

 BODY:
Despite the soaring value of the yen, the Toyota Motor Corporation introduced a subcompact car today that will be imported from Japan and will be priced well below comparable models made by Detroit auto makers.
Not long ago, such a move would probably have outraged American auto executives, prompting them to call for Federal investigations into whether Japanese cars were being dumped on the United States market. But there was silence today from the industry in Detroit, which has become increasingly global. 
 The new subcompact, the Echo, starts at just $9,995, the first Toyota in five years to be priced for less than $10,000 in the United States. The Echo costs thousands less than competitors including the Ford Escort ($11,760), Dodge Neon ($12,460) and even Ford's newest small-car offering, the Focus ($11,865).
Still, Toyota executives say that selling the car at that price is profitable for the company and that the low price comes from engineering advances.
"If anything, the low price was prompted by the last surge in the yen's value," said Donald V. Esmond, group vice president and general manager for Toyota's United States arm. "It probably was due to the yen four years ago, when the yen was so strong and our cars were so high priced."
The yen briefly peaked at 79.85 to the dollar in April 1995 before retreating beyond 100. The yen closed today at 106.98 to the dollar, after climbing more than 15 percent over the last 10 weeks.
The yen rally, which tends to make all imports more expensive, has been powered by Japan's apparent emergence from its longest recession since World War II.
Hiroshi Okuda, the chairman of Toyota, said in a telephone interview today that the globalization of the auto industry had made trade frictions less severe between the United States and Japan. "That is how we in private business, as well as in the Government, see it," Mr. Okuda said.
Of course it is harder now for American executives to call for people to "buy American." The Chrysler Corporation, whose former chairman, Lee Iacocca, prominently accused Japan of dumping cars in the United States, was acquired by a German company and is now DaimlerChrysler A.G. In addition, many other auto makers with headquarters in foreign countries are building huge numbers of cars and trucks in the United States.
In the 1999 model year, Toyota built in North America 74 percent of its cars and trucks sold here, Honda built 82 percent and Nissan built 58 percent. BMW, Mitsubishi, Mazda and Subaru also build cars in the United States. Likewise, the General Motors Corporation and the Ford Motor Company sell cars around the world and own auto makers in other countries. Ford even owns 33.4 percent of Mazda.
Starting today, investors can buy Toyota's American depository receipts under the symbol TM on the New York Stock Exchange. Toyota shares also began trading today on the London Stock Exchange. Mr. Okuda even rang the New York Stock Exchange's opening bell today.
The current surge in the yen offers the first real potential for trade issues to roil the waters in the auto industry since Daimler-Benz bought Chrysler last November.
To be sure, G.M., Ford and DaimlerChrysler still employ their longtime lobbyist, Stephen J. Collins, as president of the Automotive Trade Policy Council. He continues to warn about threats from Japanese competition and said today, "There's a sense of renewed aggressivity of Japan in the U.S. market."
He noted the widening trade deficit with Japan but acknowledged that part of the increase was coming from cars imported to help quench an unexpectedly strong American auto market -- one that United States auto makers are also struggling to supply. Though such comments used to be backed up with pronouncements by chief executives in Detroit, that is no longer the case. DaimlerChrysler, Ford and G.M. officials declined to comment today on the yen or Toyota's pricing.
Mr. Okuda said Toyota had set a goal of capturing 10 percent of the American market -- compared with 8.6 percent in the first eight months of this year -- but had set no timetable. "The rise in the value of the yen will not affect our future activities here," he added. "It will not affect how soon we attain that goal."
Toyota also plans to increase its share of the European market to 5 percent from 3 percent by 2005. Worldwide, Toyota sells about 9 percent of cars and trucks.
Mr. Okuda said the yen's effect on Toyota's American operations would continue to shrink as more of the company's vehicles are assembled in the United States. But the Echo, a subcompact with expansive headroom and crisp handling, will be imported from Japan.
Toyota expects to sell at least 50,000 of the 2000 model year Echos in the United States.
Engineers shaved about $2,600 off the price of the Toyota Tercel, the car the Echo replaced. Michael J. O'Brien, a Toyota executive, said engineers designed the Echo with fewer separate components and with parts that were easier to assemble. For example, they saved $12 a car by redesigning the air-conditioning unit in one piece instead of three. And they saved $30 to $35 a car by bolting the power-steering pump, alternator and air-conditioning compressor directly to the Echo's engine block.
"Those changes added up," he said. "Anytime there's a part, there's handling requirements, and that all adds up to money."
