HEADLINE: Canada Is Taking a Tougher Line With Illegal Chinese Migrants

BYLINE: By JAMES BROOKE 

DATELINE: PORT HARDY, British Columbia

 BODY:
When the Canadian Navy made a group of undocumented Chinese migrants get off a ship here recently, one local man brought his dogs to the dock and encouraged them to bark at the men and women wrapped in blankets against the cold.
For Canadian officials, the confrontation represented one victory in what they say is a rapidly growing effort to smuggle in illegal immigrants. This summer alone, they confiscated four trawlers and detained 560 undocumented Chinese citizens who wanted to make Canada their home. 
 Western Canada is only one destination in a pan-Pacific surge this year in illegal Chinese migrants. In the last 16 months, United States Coast Guard ships have intercepted 20 ships carrying undocumented Chinese in American territorial waters around Guam. In the last six months, Australia has intercepted 21 boatloads of illegal migrants, primarily Chinese.
"We're now experiencing the largest smuggling operation ever experienced in Canada," Rob Johnston, manager of immigration enforcement here, told reporters recently.
Steven Weeks, an Australian diplomat in Ottawa, said of Canada's sudden concern: "It's deja vu. The element of the public debate and concern is very similar to Australia."
As Australia, Canada and the United States increasingly share intelligence about vessels believed to be carrying migrants from southeastern China, Canada has started to take the harder line traditional in the other two countries.
After the first Chinese ship was intercepted, on July 20, the authorities in Vancouver released 74 immigrants, pending resolution of their petitions for refugee status. Within a month, 51 had disappeared, presumably for the United States.
Since then Canada has adopted a policy of detaining all undocumented passengers on vessels from China. Sentiment for immediately deporting them is markedly higher in western Canada than on the Atlantic seaboard, opinion polls indicate.
Vancouver's population is 20 percent ethnic Chinese, and many of these Chinese-Canadians fear that each new boatload batters a carefully constructed racial tolerance.
Bill Vander Zalm, a former provincial premier, has jumped back into politics, starting his campaign for Parliament by saying that the federal Government would pay attention to the immigration issue only if all the illegal Chinese immigrants detained in the region were shipped to Ottawa. "I am sure you would get some action then," he said.
Canadian newspapers portray the boatloads of migrants, largely from Fujian Province, as job-seekers rather than freedom-seekers. Not long ago, Elinor Caplan, Canada's new Immigration Minister, toured a Vancouver Island navy base where 225 immigrants are detained and said at a packed news conference: "We are intercepting, boarding and apprehending those ships. We are then using greater powers of detention to insure people on the boat do not flee. The system is working."
Canadian officials are also asking Chinese authorities to help.
At the recent Asia-Pacific Economic Cooperation summit meeting in New Zealand, Canada's Prime Minister, Jean Chretien, discussed the migration problem with President Jiang Zemin of China. "I told him we needed collaboration because they could hopefully stop the departure of the ships from the coast of China that are coming to our coast," Mr. Chretien said later. "He recognized that, and said he was willing to collaborate with us."

HEADLINE: Now Found in Bell Atlantic's Corner: New York Regulators

BYLINE: By SETH SCHIESEL 

 BODY:
At about 8:30 yesterday morning, workers dispatched by the Bell Atlantic Corporation, the nation's biggest local telephone company, began unloading 30 boxes of documents from trucks outside the Washington offices of the Federal Communications Commission.
The boxes contained three copies of Bell Atlantic's application to sell long-distance communications services to consumers and businesses in New York State. If the petition is granted, Bell Atlantic will become the first regional Bell admitted to the $80 billion long-distance market. 
 But for all its physical heft, Bell Atlantic's application was matched in significance by a statement of merely four sentences issued a few hours later by Maureen O. Helmer, chairwoman of the New York Public Service Commission. The commission, which is highly regarded at the F.C.C., has been jawboning Bell Atlantic to open its local network for years.
All but formally endorsing Bell Atlantic's long-distance aspirations, Ms. Helmer insured that Bell Atlantic's rivals, which will try to persuade the F.C.C. to reject the application, have a difficult task.
"I am pleased that Bell Atlantic's progress in meeting the terms of the 271 checklist has reached the point of a filing with the F.C.C.," her statement read, referring to the section of the Telecommunications Act of 1996 that details the steps a Bell company must take before entering the long-distance market. "The cooperation of all the parties during the course of this review has facilitated a tremendous amount of progress by the company in opening its local market to competition. If the filing is consistent with the terms and conditions that we have established, and the record we have developed over the past two and a half years, I am optimistic that I could offer my support."
Ms. Helmer was under no obligation to share her optimism yesterday; the state commission will not make formal comments to the F.C.C. for about three weeks.
In the days and weeks leading up to Bell Atlantic's application yesterday, many communications experts said that the F.C.C. was likely to follow the guidance of the New York regulators -- or at least give New York's recommendation far more weight than it had lent to earlier long-distance endorsements for other regional Bells from regulators in other states. Applications in some states -- including Louisiana, South Carolina and Oklahoma -- were rejected, with people close to the F.C.C. saying that the commission had not been convinced of the rigor and independence of the regulators in those states.
By contrast, William E. Kennard, the F.C.C.'s chairman, was positively glowing yesterday in his praise of the New York commission.
"I applaud the New York Public Service Commission for its work on Bell Atlantic's application to enter the long-distance market," he said in a statement. "The dedication and expertise displayed by New York's Public Service Commission and its staff are truly impressive."
Mr. Kennard's regard for the New York regulators may be enhanced by the fact that his chief of staff, Kathryn C. Brown, spent three years as director of the New York commission's consumer services division.
So at this point the tea leaves indicate that the application will be approved. That still does not necessarily mean Bell Atlantic is set to define the One True Path for other Bells to reach their long-distance nirvana.
In Texas, SBC Communications Inc. is scheduled to receive results of a test of the openness of its network on Friday, with the next meeting of the state's regulatory commission to discuss SBC's long-distance plans set for Oct. 21. If SBC is lucky -- and good -- it will be able to apply to the F.C.C. for permission in Texas soon afterward.
But the way SBC has opened its network in Texas is in some technical aspects quite different from Bell Atlantic's model. Because of the high concentration of lucrative business customers in New York, especially in Manhattan, many competitive local phone companies in New York City have found it economical to assemble relatively extensive networks of their own and to buy access to Bell Atlantic's systems piecemeal. In working with the state's regulators, Bell Atlantic has spent a lot of time and effort developing the capacity to sell access to many discrete parts of its network.
Competitive local carriers in Texas, on the other hand, appear to have more appetite for bigger chunks of SBC's network, perhaps putting more focus on less lucrative residential customers because the Texas business market is not quite as attractive as that of New York.
"SBC is following a different model and also has a pretty good chance of getting in on a slightly later time track," said Reed E. Hundt, the former F.C.C. chairman who is now a consultant for McKinsey & Company.
"What's interesting is whether or not both of them are accepted," he said. "If they are, the country will see two different models, and different states will go with different models."
To many telecommunications analysts and executives, Bell Atlantic's model seems to require more work on the part of the incumbent. That is why executives at other regional Bells have been heard to complain that Bell Atlantic has been too conciliatory, perhaps setting a bar too high for the other Bell companies to hurdle easily.
Bell Atlantic has every reason to be more conciliatory because it has already lost so much corporate business in big metropolitan markets to upstarts. It is not a new pattern. In 1990, for instance, Nynex -- later acquired by Bell Atlantic -- became the first Bell to set aside space within its switching centers for competitors to place their own equipment. The other Bells howled that Nynex was making them look bad.
"Some people would say that's being conciliatory," Ivan G. Seidenberg, who was chairman of Nynex before becoming chairman of Bell Atlantic, said yesterday. "I would say we're the first company that faces competition in the industry every time. And I can go ahead and say we have been right every time because everybody has followed suit sooner or later, though they tend to do it kicking and scratching because it's convenient to say, 'New York made us do it.'
"If we have to trade some things to get access to larger markets, I think that's the right trade-off," Mr. Seidenberg added. "While I could get excited about the word conciliatory, I might want to replace that with savvy and strategic."

HEADLINE: Fannie Mae Eases Credit To Aid MortgageLending

BYLINE: By STEVEN A. HOLMES 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 29

 BODY:
In a move that could help increase home ownership rates among minorities and low-income consumers, the Fannie Mae Corporation is easing the credit requirements on loans that it will purchase from banks and other lenders.
The action, which will begin as a pilot program involving 24 banks in 15 markets -- including the New York metropolitan region -- will encourage those banks to extend home mortgages to individuals whose credit is generally not good enough to qualify for conventional loans. Fannie Mae officials say they hope to make it a nationwide program by next spring. 
 Fannie Mae, the nation's biggest underwriter of home mortgages, has been under increasing pressure from the Clinton Administration to expand mortgage loans among low and moderate income people and felt pressure from stock holders to maintain its phenomenal growth in profits.
In addition, banks, thrift institutions and mortgage companies have been pressing Fannie Mae to help them make more loans to so-called subprime borrowers. These borrowers whose incomes, credit ratings and savings are not good enough to qualify for conventional loans, can only get loans from finance companies that charge much higher interest rates -- anywhere from three to four percentage points higher than conventional loans.
"Fannie Mae has expanded home ownership for millions of families in the 1990's by reducing down payment requirements," said Franklin D. Raines, Fannie Mae's chairman and chief executive officer. "Yet there remain too many borrowers whose credit is just a notch below what our underwriting has required who have been relegated to paying significantly higher mortgage rates in the so-called subprime market."
Demographic information on these borrowers is sketchy. But at least one study indicates that 18 percent of the loans in the subprime market went to black borrowers, compared to 5 per cent of loans in the conventional loan market.
In moving, even tentatively, into this new area of lending, Fannie Mae is taking on significantly more risk, which may not pose any difficulties during flush economic times. But the government-subsidized corporation may run into trouble in an economic downturn, prompting a government rescue similar to that of the savings and loan industry in the 1980's.
"From the perspective of many people, including me, this is another thrift industry growing up around us," said Peter Wallison a resident fellow at the American Enterprise Institute. "If they fail, the government will have to step up and bail them out the way it stepped up and bailed out the thrift industry."
Under Fannie Mae's pilot program, consumers who qualify can secure a mortgage with an interest rate one percentage point above that of a conventional, 30-year fixed rate mortgage of less than $240,000 -- a rate that currently averages about 7.76 per cent. If the borrower makes his or her monthly payments on time for two years, the one percentage point premium is dropped.
Fannie Mae, the nation's biggest underwriter of home mortgages, does not lend money directly to consumers. Instead, it purchases loans that banks make on what is called the secondary market. By expanding the type of loans that it will buy, Fannie Mae is hoping to spur banks to make more loans to people with less-than-stellar credit ratings.
Fannie Mae officials stress that the new mortgages will be extended to all potential borrowers who can qualify for a mortgage. But they add that the move is intended in part to increase the number of minority and low income home owners who tend to have worse credit ratings than non-Hispanic whites.
Home ownership has, in fact, exploded among minorities during the economic boom of the 1990's. The number of mortgages extended to Hispanic applicants jumped by 87.2 per cent from 1993 to 1998, according to Harvard University's Joint Center for Housing Studies. During that same period the number of African Americans who got mortgages to buy a home increased by 71.9 per cent and the number of Asian Americans by 46.3 per cent.
In contrast, the number of non-Hispanic whites who received loans for homes increased by 31.2 per cent.
Despite these gains, home ownership rates for minorities continue to lag behind non-Hispanic whites, in part because blacks and Hispanics in particular tend to have on average worse credit ratings.
In July, the Department of Housing and Urban Development proposed that by the year 2001, 50 percent of Fannie Mae's and Freddie Mac's portfolio be made up of loans to low and moderate-income borrowers. Last year, 44 percent of the loans Fannie Mae purchased were from these groups.
The change in policy also comes at the same time that HUD is investigating allegations of racial discrimination in the automated underwriting systems used by Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac to determine the credit-worthiness of credit applicants.

HEADLINE: Amazon.com Plans A Transformation To Internet Bazaar

BYLINE: By LESLIE KAUFMAN 

 BODY:
Amazon.com announced yesterday that it would transform itself into an Internet shopping bazaar, opening its popular Web site to merchants large and small for a minimal fee. In return, the selling powerhouse, which started as an on-line bookstore, will gather huge amounts of information on the buying habits of consumers.
The initiative, called zShops, begins today with 500,000 new products -- about four times the offerings of the average Kmart -- the company said. Whether Amazon customers are really looking to buy everything from jam made by Trappist monks to white-water rescue equipment is yet to be proved. 
 But consumer sales over the Internet will jump to $108 billion by 2003 from $3.9 billion last year, according to Forrester Research, and most major merchants are trying to develop a selling strategy to master the emerging marketplace.
Amazon, which is losing money although it is one of the most-visited sites on the Web, expects to help these companies and to expand well beyond books, CD's, electronics and toys. For Amazon, one big benefit is a steady stream of cash without the costs associated with a warehouse of products. Merchants will pay $9.99 a month to be listed on the site and will distribute their own goods. If they choose to have Amazon process their credit card purchases, they will pay an additional 4.75 percent of the total sale.
To become the Web shopping destination of choice, Amazon will probably need to persuade at least some big retailers to participate, thus giving up a portion of Internet sales and relinquishing data on customers -- something they may be loath to do.
Despite the obstacles, Wall Street applauded the move, the biggest in a series by the retailer to evolve from a Web bookstore into a full-fledged shopping portal, by sending Amazon's stock up 23 percent, to $80.75. "Consumers win, retailers win and Amazon wins big time," said Chris Vroom, an E-commerce stock analyst with Thomas Weisel Partners.
Jeff Bezos, Amazon's founder and chief executive, said he wanted to give his customers what they wanted most: endless choice. "Sixteen months ago, we were a place we could come to find books," he said. "Tomorrow, we will be a place to find anything, with a capital A."
Amazon will be experimenting with a type of business now dominated by other Internet giants. On one hand, its move to become an unlimited shopping mall is an attempt to compete with Internet portals like American Online and Yahoo, which offer links with millions of Web sites and have substantially more monthly traffic. The changes also provide an opportunity to take away some of the small-business revenue flooding into auction sites like Ebay and Microsoft, Excite@Home and Lycos, which agreed to share their auction listings earlier this month.
Most any willing merchant will be able to work with Amazon and have indirect access to it 12 million customers, but Amazon will exclude merchants its deems "offensive," including sellers of guns, hard-core pornography and hate literature.
In an important difference from real-world and Internet malls, however, zShops will be essentially organized by product and product category -- fishing rods and sporting goods, for example, -- not by stores. Only after a customer has picked a particular item from a list, say, an Orvis fly rod for $150, would he be sent to a page controlled by the merchant that would include a picture and description of the item.
To make the offer more enticing, Amazon is giving merchants a chance to use several of the marketing tools it has refined. Through what it calls relationship marketing, a small seller of Indian spices could have a link to its Web site pop up every time a customer calls up a book by Salman Rushdie, author of "The Satanic Verses," or a compact disk by the sitarist Ravi Shankar. This service will be offered free until Christmas.
In addition, for a percentage of the sale, Amazon will let any merchant offer one-click shopping. That allows customers to click on the shipping address and credit information stored in the Amazon data bank to make a purchase through another merchant. Amazon charges the card and then deposits the money directly into the seller account. The seller is responsible for shipping and product quality.
The company declined to say how many stores were participating in the experiment, but the arrangement will be less appealing to larger business. Matt Hyde, vice president of on-line sales for the sporting goods retailer REI.com, said that he had turned down the opportunity because he aims to control the quality of the shopping experience for his customers. "Amazon commoditizes the product," he said.
"We would consider it," said Anna Schryver, a spokeswoman for clothing retailer Lands' End, "However, we believe the greatest place for our customer is our Web site because of the service component."
This strategy is not without risks, the most pressing of which is quality control. Amazon now has a brand name that is widely recognized for trustworthiness. Since the retailer will only do minimal screening of its prospective partners, it could easily find itself processing orders for inefficient or dishonest merchants.
Amazon is counting on self-policing by consumer ratings to eliminate most fraud. "If you are selling on line, your digital reputation is all you have," said Jaleh Bisharat, Amazon's vice president for marketing. "They have a tremendous incentive to deliver high-quality goods."
In addition, Amazon has said it will guarantee merchandise that never arrives or differs materially from how it was described up to $1,000 if it handles the credit card payment and up to $250 if it does not.
Still analysts see a downside that could be substantial. If a Tiffany lamp ordered through zShops turns out to be a fake, will the customer still blame Amazon? "Yep," said Alan Braverman, head of Internet Research at Bank of America.
But the risk to Amazon could be well worth it if it becomes the pre-eminent vault of information for Internet shopping habits. In theory, Amazon could gather information on products that were most popular with its customers.
"One of the reasons behind adding these shops will be to help them determine what products their customers are interested in so they can offer it themselves," said Mike May, a digital commerce analyst with Jupiter Communications, an Internet research firm. "If a specific product category, say sweaters, gets hot, they have a lot of incentive to sell direct to consumers."
Even if Amazon does not eventually choose to expand into new products, it still has a lot to gain by learning more about consumer shopping habits. "The data could become the most valuable asset," predicted Mr. Braverman. He hypothesizes about a future in which manufacturers like Procter & Gamble, for example, would pay Amazon to test-market new products.
To get an idea of how such an arrangement could benefit Amazon, one need look no farther than when Charles Schwab & Company started its Mutual Fund Marketplace. The financial services giant offered to customers the ability to go to one spot to buy mutual funds from dozens of companies. Schwab took no inventory from the companies and had no responsibility for managing the money, but it did collect fees on the transactions. Schwab also kept the most valuable part of the transaction for itself: information on customers' investing habits.
The fund companies do not know who their customers are, they simply get the money. As a result, the fund companies cannot communicate directly with the investors who buy through Schwab, so have no hope of repeat sales or targeted marketing.
Schwab, on the other hand, has used this method to establish a base of customers about whom it knows habits, preferences, investment ability and more. As it has expanded its business, it has started to pick off these customers from the companies whose funds it also sells. Schwab has been the most successful company in its industry in recent years.

HEADLINE: G.O.P. Plan Would Delay A Tax Credit for Workers

BYLINE: By TIM WEINER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 29

 BODY:
Desperate for money to finance their spending bills without raiding Social Security, Congressional Republicans devised a plan today relying almost entirely on delaying billions of dollars in Federal tax breaks for millions of working-class families.
The plan was immediately questioned by Democrats and some Republicans, who called it an accounting gimmick that could hurt people on the lower rungs of the economic ladder. Frank Salizzoni, the chief executive of H&R Block Inc., said it "could cause confusion and disrupt the personal lives of hard-working American families." 
 A chart detailing the plan, obtained from a senior House Republican leader, shows Congress on course to spend $7.6 billion in surplus contributions to Social Security to run the Government in the 2000 fiscal year, which begins on Friday.
But because under the new plan about $8 billion would not be spent in the 2000 fiscal year, the Republicans' calculations show $400 million in black ink, rather than a $7.6 billion dip into Social Security money.
But the figure could be higher. The Congressional Budget Office released new figures tonight estimating that Republicans were actually on course to spend $17.9 billion out of the Social Security surplus.
Nonetheless, Republican leaders, who have vowed daily, and fervently, that they will not spend the money, stood together today under a banner reading "Stop Robbing Social Security" and unveiled a new campaign of television commercials renewing their pledge.
The House Republicans' own calculations show that the only way they can avoid raiding Social Security is to distribute the politically popular earned-income tax credit in 12 monthly payments next year, rather than in a lump sum.
Because the fiscal year ends on Sept. 30, payments for October, November and December 2000 would be counted against the 2001 budget, said John Feehery, a spokesman for Speaker J. Dennis Hastert of Illinois.
So the plan saves no money, but instead uses an accounting device that allows three months of the tax-credit payments to be counted in the next year. Mr. Feehery said delaying the payments would hurt no one and help the Government "weed out waste, fraud and abuse" that he said was rampant in the earned-income tax credit program.
The 25-year-old tax credit was designed to be an incentive to keep people working and paying taxes. It was expanded by the Reagan and Clinton Administrations, and now, about 20 million Americans in households earning $10,200 to $30,580 are eligible for it. They received $28.5 billion last year.
The average family received $1,890 in a single annual payment, allowing some to pay off debts, make a down payment on a car or pay tuition. Under the Republican plan, that same family would receive, instead, a check for $157.50 a month, a sum better suited for buying groceries or paying utility bills.
Gene Sperling, director of the White House's National Economic Council, called the plan "a forced interest-free loan to the Government" from "people scraping to get by." The White House budget director, Jack Lew, called it "kind of Dickensian."
The Republican idea of using the tax credit to offset billions in spending was first floated two weeks ago, but not as the centerpiece of a plan to leave Social Security alone. Even then, it was received unenthusiastically by much of the Republican rank-and-file. Today some conservative Republicans said the plan still made them uncomfortable.
"I have real concerns," said Representative Mark Souder, an Indiana Republican, who called the plan "a gimmick."
"We're trying to move people from welfare to work," Mr. Souder said. "But a number of our proposals seem to hit people moving from dependency to independence. Regardless of how you look at it, it's a reduction in benefits for those people."
A senior Republican aide expressed confidence in the plan. "It can be passed and it will be passed," he said.
Democrats in Congress were skeptical. "Why don't they go after some corporate tax break instead of the poorest of the poor?" asked the House minority leader, Richard A. Gephardt of Missouri.
Representative David Obey of Wisconsin, the ranking Democrat on the House Appropriations Committee, said, "It boggles my mind that they would be that callous."
The Republicans' calculations directly conflict with the new figures released tonight by the Congressional Budget Office. The budget office's figures, requested by Representative John M. Spratt Jr. of South Carolina, the ranking Democrat on the House Budget Committee, show Congress on course to spend $17.9 billion in Social Security money.
The differing figures can be explained by the fact that the Republicans have ordered the budget office to make billions of dollars in "directed scorekeeping" adjustments. That means they have told their numbers-crunchers not to count those billions, a process that Stan Collender, a Federal budget expert at Fleishman-Hillard, defines as "lying about the numbers."
The Republicans, Mr. Spratt said, "were not playing by the rules."
Calculations by the Republicans also assume that almost everything else in the increasingly chaotic Congressional spending process will turn up as they hope, which may be unlikely. Congress made no progress today in passing spending bills, leaving 8 of the 13 measures incomplete.

HEADLINE: State Takes Over East Orange Finances After 2d Year of Debt

 BODY:
Prompted by back-to-back deficits in the city's last two budgets, New Jersey officials voted yesterday to seize control of the municipal finances of East Orange. The move is required by state law whenever a city's deficit exceeds 4 percent for two consecutive years.
East Orange is the only New Jersey city currently under such supervision. The last city under state financial control was Irvington, in 1996 and 1997. 
 But the step was welcomed by top officials in East Orange, one of five New Jersey cities -- with Jersey City, Paterson, Irvington and Camden -- that have already agreed to allow close state scrutiny of their finances in return for so-called distressed cities aid.
"I welcome the decision from the perspective that we need help," said Robert Bowser, the East Orange Mayor.
The state's move is not as drastic as its takeover of troubled school districts in Jersey City, Paterson and Newark, where local superintendents and entire boards of education were replaced by state appointees. Rather, the Mayor and City Council will remain in office under the supervision of the State Director of Local Government Services, Ulrich Steinberg, who will have authority over decisions on purchasing and other spending, awarding of contracts, negotiations with unions, tax collection, issuing of new debt and other finances.
"We're not replacing the Mayor and Council; we're hoping to work in cooperation with the Mayor and Council," said E. J. Miranda, a spokesman for Mr. Steinberg.
The city ended the 1999 fiscal year on June 30 with a deficit of $8.9 million in its budget of just over $100 million, largely because of a tax collection rate of 76.3 percent, far below the state average of about 90 percent, Mr. Miranda said. The city expects to lose an additional $5 million this year as a result of miscalculation in its two-year-old deal to provide water to several surrounding communities, Mayor Bowser said.
The Mayor and leaders of a majority faction on the 10-member City Council have cast some of the blame for the city's financial woes on one another. The Mayor said the Council had not adopted a 1999 budget he submitted last September until June 1, a month before the end of the fiscal year, which delayed the collection of taxes. Councilman Mark Scotland, the chairman of the Council's finance committee, said the Mayor had failed to make layoffs he proposed last fall.
But Councilwoman Beverly Williams said she was embarrassed by the state's move. "It's a sad day in East Orange," she said.
Officials agreed that there were several deep-seated problems in East Orange, a city of 73,000 people next to Newark, that will be difficult to solve even with state supervision. Those include high unemployment and a shrinking tax base.
Robert Baily, a professor of public policy at Rutgers University, said he feared that tensions might develop between the financial watchdogs of the overwhelmingly white Republican administration of Gov. Christine Todd Whitman and the overwhelmingly black Democratic officials of East Orange.

HEADLINE: Beatty Makes No Declaration

BYLINE: By The New York Times 

DATELINE: BEVERLY HILLS, Calif., Sept. 29

 BODY:
In a discursive, sometimes impassioned half-hour speech to the Hollywood A-list and liberal advocates, Warren Beatty tonight denounced the conservative drift of the Democratic Party, the rise of corporate greed, the influence of big-money campaign contributions, lingering poverty and social injustice. And he suggested that he would "keep talking" about them loud and long.
But Mr. Beatty did not come close to declaring his own candidacy for President, as some of his supporters among the disaffectedof both major parties had hoped he might do. 
 Calling himself "an old-time, unrepentent, bleeding-heart, tax-and-spend" liberal, Mr. Beatty said, "We don't need a third party, we need a second party."
Mr. Beatty made his speech in accepting the annual Eleanor Roosevelt Award of the Southern California chapter of Americans for Democratic Action. Sometimes speaking off the cuff, with humorous asides to his celebrity friends in the audience, and sometimes rattling off reams of statistics about economic inequality, he seemed by turns confident and fidgety.
Mr. Beatty described both contenders for the Democratic Presidential nomination, Vice President Al Gore and former Senator Bill Bradley, as worthy men of high character. But he demanded, "What is this insurgency of a centrist against a centrist all about?"
He added, "What we're in danger of experiencing is a slow-motion coup d'etat of big money's interest over the public interest."
